Twentieth Century Electric Catalog

This book is an expanded and revised version of *Twentieth Century Electric Catalog*, winner of an Encouragement Award in the Novel Category at the 8th Kyoto Animation Award.





Character Introductions





Momokawa Inako — Second daughter of Momokawa Sake Brewery in Fushimi, Kyoto. Fifteen years old.  
Clumsy and constantly failing, she is always scolded by her father. A devout girl who never stops believing in gods and spirits, her catchphrase is "Faith is my greatest strength."





Sakamoto Kihachi — A boy who adores electricity. Fifteen years old.  
He left his family’s temple to live and work at his uncle’s Hōrai Buddhist goods shop. He also takes on side jobs repairing machinery.  
As a child, he wrote the *Electric Catalog*.





Kuga Kengo — A heroic figure who survived the Russo-Japanese War, nicknamed "Kuga the Steam Engine." Twenty-four years old.  
Currently devoted to charitable work, though his marriage to Noriko remains on hold.





Momokawa Noriko — Inako’s older sister. Twenty years old.  
In sharp contrast to her younger sister, she is a graceful and talented woman of both beauty and ability. Her fiancé Kuga is like a cat and dog to her; they constantly trade barbs and bicker.





2.

Soyuzo Mitzoe — Eldest son of the wealthy merchant family, Mitsue Shoten. Twenty-nine years old.

Known by the nickname "Dr. Baikal," he is widely respected and regarded as a shrewd businessman. He is currently searching for the rare text *Electric Catalog*.

Sakamoto Seiroku — Older brother of Kikichi.  
His free-spirited, unconventional way of thinking has greatly influenced Kikichi. He loves high places; his dream is to climb the Eiffel Tower.

Momokawa Jin’emon — Inako’s father. Forty-two years old.  
Possesses the dignified bearing and authority of a samurai, and frequently strikes Inako with his favorite walking stick while shouting at her.

　　　　　　　　　　I

“It marks the dawn of the twentieth century.”

How many people living in the Meiji era truly felt the arrival of this new age when they heard such words?

The first year of the twentieth century was 1901 in the Western calendar—Meiji 34 in Japan’s reckoning, a year that held no particular significance for the nation. More than thirty years had passed since the Restoration; topknots and swords were finally fading into history, and Western civilization had indeed permeated society to a considerable degree. Yet for Japanese people long accustomed to imperial eras, the term “twentieth century” still felt distant and unfamiliar.

But precisely because it *was* unfamiliar, “twentieth century” carried a fashionable, modern ring.

Much like how Murai Brothers Trading Company began selling a cigarette brand named “Twentieth Century,” merchants eagerly adopted the phrase as a catchword for advertising. As a result, the term “twentieth century” enjoyed rampant popularity, splashed boldly across shop banners and advertisements alike.

2.

Years had passed since the Restoration; topknots and swords were finally fading into history, and Western civilization had indeed permeated society to a considerable degree. Yet for Japanese people long accustomed to imperial eras, the term “twentieth century” still felt distant and unfamiliar. But precisely because it *was* unfamiliar, “twentieth century” carried a fashionable, modern ring. Much like how Murai Brothers Trading Company began selling a cigarette brand named “Twentieth Century,” merchants eagerly adopted the phrase as a catchword for advertising. As a result, the term “twentieth century” enjoyed rampant popularity, splashed boldly across shop banners and advertisements alike.

I don’t know what the “twentieth century” is. But since important people keep saying it and using it in slogans, it must be something auspicious. And when something’s auspicious, you’d better jump on board—that’s the Yamato spirit.

Just as ordinary folk once claimed they could hear the sound of “civilization and enlightenment” when striking a cropped head, common people now tried saying “twentieth century” aloud—and instantly felt reborn, as if transformed into denizens of a new age.

The word “twentieth century” is new. And new words carry authority. If you hurl it at someone clinging to old ways—“this is the coming age of the twentieth century!”—you can unilaterally brand them as “outdated.” It’s nothing less than the return of the Imperial Banner, which once wielded immense power in the Boshin War.

Young people brandished this banner of the “twentieth century,” striding boldly while preaching new ideologies like naturalism and women’s liberation. And those from the old era mocked them in return: “Hey, Twentieth Century!”

The first New Year’s Day of the twentieth century. At Zenshoku-ji, a small temple in Ōtsu, Ōmi Province in Gōshū, life went on as usual—pounding rice cakes to offer to the temple’s honzon, serving *toso* and *osechi* to visiting parishioners. Not a trace of the “twentieth century” could be felt there.

After finishing his chores, Sakamoto Kihachi—nine years old this year, and the priest’s son—entered the inner room and clasped his hands reverently before the household *kamidana*. Just then, his older brother Seiroku approached, grinning mischievously.

“Hey, Twentieth Century—what’re you so earnestly doing over there?”

2.

“Hey, Twentieth Century!” The first New Year’s Day of the twentieth century. At Zenshoku-ji, a small temple in Ōtsu, Ōmi Province in Gōshū, life went on as usual—pounding rice cakes to offer to the temple’s honzon, serving *toso* and *osechi* to visiting parishioners. Not a trace of the “twentieth century” could be felt there. After finishing his chores, Sakamoto Kihachi—nine years old this year, and the priest’s son—entered the inner room and clasped his hands reverently before the household *kamidana*. Just then, his older brother Seiroku approached, grinning mischievously.  

“What’re you so earnestly doing over there?” he called.  

“Saying hello to the ancestors for the New Year,” Kihachi replied, turning his head toward Seiroku with a slight tilt.  

“I’ve been wondering for a while—what exactly is this ‘Twentieth Century’ thing, anyway?”  

“Oh? You’ve taken an interest in the Twentieth Century, huh?”  

Shivering slightly in the cold beneath his *danzen*, Seiroku lowered himself beside him. He’d turn eighteen next month, yet his smiling face carried a certain boyishness despite its composure.  

“Well, unlike the Western calendar, we count centuries by every hundred years. From the birth of Jesus Christ up to 1900—that was the nineteenth century. This year marks the beginning of the twentieth.”  

“But why use such a confusing way of counting when we already have the Western calendar?”  

“How should I know? It’s just useful for summing up long stretches of time. If it’s a hundred years full of war, you call it ‘the Century of War’; if industry advances greatly, ‘the Century of Industry.’”  

“So what kind of hundred years will the twentieth century be, I wonder?”  

“Here—something good,” Seiroku said, pulling a clipped newspaper article from his breast pocket and handing it to Kihachi.  

“Read this. It’s an article that predicts what sort of age the twentieth century will be.”  

The clipped article was titled *“Prophecies of the Twentieth Century.”*  

It declared:  

*“Wireless telephones will advance to the point where one can call from Tokyo to New York.”*  

*“Railways equipped with heating and cooling will run between Tokyo and Kobe in two and a half hours.”*  

Among twenty-three items in total, the predictions focused chiefly on technological progress.  

“This one says, ‘Electricity will replace wood and coal as fuel’—but what does that actually mean?”

2.

He pulled it from his breast pocket and handed it to Kihachi. “Read this. It’s an article that predicts what sort of age the twentieth century will be.” The clipped article was titled *“Prophecies of the Twentieth Century.”* It declared: *“Wireless telephones will advance to the point where one can call from Tokyo to New York.”* *“Railways equipped with heating and cooling will run between Tokyo and Kobe in two and a half hours.”* Among twenty-three items in total, the predictions focused chiefly on technological progress. “This one says, ‘Electricity will replace wood and coal as fuel’—but what does that actually mean?”

“Of course, not just telephones, but locomotives, temperature-control machines, and even photograph devices—all will be powered by electricity.”

*Electricity.*

Like the twentieth century itself, electricity was something unfamiliar to Kihachi.

Whenever he heard the word *electricity*, he thought of electric lights—but such things were far beyond the reach of ordinary people, and even in this household, kerosene lamps provided the light. So four years ago, when he and Seiroku had gone to see the first electric lights lit in the town of Ōtsu, they had been astonished by how much brighter they were than oil lamps or candles.

An age in which that smokeless, miraculous light could power machines.

“I see… so the twentieth century is the age of electricity.”

Kihachi’s words left Seiroku momentarily dumbfounded, but soon he grinned, revealing his white teeth.

“That’s right. The twentieth century is the age of electricity.”

Excitement stirring in his chest, Kihachi went to the chest of drawers in the inner room and pulled out a blank folding book.  

“I’ll write my own prophecy of the twentieth century too.”

On the folding book—normally reserved for sacred texts like sutras or norito prayers—Kihachi’s crude handwriting was now inscribed in pencil. When he finished, Seiroku peered over his shoulder to read.

“*First: Yoru Shirazu*—the garment of electric light. Walking night roads without needing to carry a lamp.”

“Write more,” Seiroku urged, so Kihachi quickly added two more entries.

“*Second: Shashinki*—the image device. Capture ancestors and family spirits in photographs using electric light. *Third: Denpō*—the electric cannon. Transport humans at the speed lightning strikes the earth.”

2.

He pulled out a blank folding book. “I’ll write my own prophecy of the Twentieth Century too.” On the folding book—normally reserved for sacred texts like sutras or norito prayers—Kihachi’s crude handwriting was now inscribed in pencil. When he finished, Seiroku peered over his shoulder to read.  
*First: Yoru Shirazu*—the garment of electric light. Walking night roads without needing to carry a lamp.  
“Write more,” Seiroku urged, so Kihachi quickly added two more entries.  
*Second: Shashinki*—the image device. Capture ancestors and family spirits in photographs using electric light.  
*Third: Denpō*—the electric cannon. Transport humans at the speed lightning strikes the earth.

Seiroku burst into laughter after reading that far. Kihachi, his face puffed up in irritation, snapped, “If you’re just gonna mock me, don’t read it!” and tried to snatch the book shut—but Seiroku had already snatched it away.

“Wait. Since you went through the trouble, let me give this prophecy scroll a proper name.”

Without being asked, Seiroku swiftly ground ink and grunted thoughtfully, dipping his brush.

“Judging from this prophecy, you seem taken with electricity… All right, I’ve decided.”

With that, he wrote a single line on the cover and handed the book back to Kihachi.

『Denki Mokuroku』

That was the name of Kihachi’s prophecy scroll.

“From now on, keep writing prophecies about electricity in here. Twenty of them, to match the Twentieth Century.”

Two years had passed since that exchange between brothers. It was early summer in Meiji 36 (1903).

Kihachi crouched inside a storehouse, enduring the sweltering humidity.

The storehouse door was tightly shut, not a crack left open. Inside, the air reeked of mildew, and the silence was eerie. The May dolls, old furniture, and Buddhist altar items lurking in the darkness seemed like mute monsters silently aiming at him.

Leaning against the wall, eyes tightly shut, breathing roughly, he suddenly heard the bolt at the entrance slide open. Kihachi leapt to his feet. The double doors swung wide with force, dazzling light blinding him.

“Hey, Twentieth Century. What’re you doing cooped up in this gloomy place?”

A smug voice rang out. Kihachi squinted open his eyes slightly to see Seiroku standing in the doorway like a wrathful guardian god.

“Brother, what’re you doing here? What about work at the shop?”

2.

Old furniture and Buddhist altar items lurking in the darkness seemed like mute monsters silently glaring at him. Leaning against the wall, eyes tightly shut, breathing roughly, he suddenly heard the bolt at the entrance slide open. Kihachi leapt to his feet. The double doors swung wide with force, dazzling light blinding him.  

“Hey, Twentieth Century. What’re you doing cooped up in this gloomy place?” A smug voice rang out.  

Kihachi squinted open his eyes slightly to see Seiroku standing in the doorway like a wrathful guardian god.  

“Brother, what’re you doing here? What about work at the shop?”  

「I got the day off, so I came home. You again locked yourself in the storehouse?”  

“…This morning during the service, I was spacing out, and Father went ballistic on me.”  

“That old man—Amitābha Buddha, the merciful one, wouldn’t get angry over something like that.”  

“You say that, but you don’t believe in paradise or Buddha at all, brother.”  

“I just don’t believe in anything I can’t see,” Seiroku said with a loud gahaha laugh.  

Seiroku, despite being a temple priest’s son, refused to believe in any gods or Buddhas. Because of this, he had long clashed repeatedly with his father, Kuma’gorō—the head priest of Zenshoku-ji, whose dharma name was Shaku Enki. After graduating from a commercial school in Ōtsu, Seiroku had been living and working at his uncle’s Buddhist goods shop in Kyoto.  

Since Seiroku rarely came home, this was their first meeting in several months.  

“Still, it’s rare for you to space out during the service.”  

“…I was wondering—what kind of place is Paradise, anyway?”  

The honzon of Zenshoku-ji, Amida Nyorai, was the Buddha who saved all people, no matter where or when they were—even those in the dark depths of ignorance—with boundless light. The Pure Land, they said, was the realm created by Amida Nyorai.  

Located far beyond westward, across ten billion Buddha-lands from this world, Paradise was said to be a world without suffering, ever bathed in the light of Amida, filled with beautiful flowers and music, where birds like celestial nymphs sang songs.

2.

“It’s rare for you to space out during the service.”  
“…I was wondering—what kind of place is Paradise, anyway?”  

The honzon of Zenshoku-ji, Amida Nyorai, was the Buddha who saved all beings, no matter where or when—those even in the dark depths of *mumyō*—with boundless light. The Pure Land, they said, was the realm created by Amida Nyorai. Located far beyond the westward direction, across ten billion Buddha-lands from this world, Paradise was said to be a world without suffering, ever bathed in the light of Amida, filled with beautiful flowers and music, where birds like *tennyo* sang songs.

The palace-like *hondō* enshrining the honzon was lavishly adorned with golden ornaments, said to represent the very image of Paradise itself. It was Seiroku’s daily practice to chant the sutras with Enki at the main hall each morning and evening—but this morning, while gazing absently at that palace, he had gotten lost thinking about Paradise, and ended up mispronouncing the responsive chanting.

Suddenly, Seiroku noticed something Sakamoto Kihachi was clutching to his chest—and swiftly snatched it away.

“What’re you hugging so tight?” he said, flipping open the booklet. “Ah—so it’s an electrical catalog.”

He easily dodged Kihachi’s attempt to snatch it back and leafed through with a mocking laugh.

“‘Item Six: Dentatsu’… delivers things automatically along power lines?”

But as he turned the next page, his laughter died. He frowned, bored and disappointed, and shoved the catalog back.

“What, that’s it? Less than half of it’s even filled in!”

“Well, I don’t know anything about electricity yet,” Kihachi pouted.

“Same as Paradise. If you’ve never seen it, no amount of thinking’ll make it clear.”

“Then perfect timing.”

For some reason, Seiroku’s expression brightened. Silhouetted against the light streaming in from outside, he reached out his hand.

“I’ll take you to Paradise right now.”

“…To *where*, exactly?”

“To Paradise. If you don’t get it, seeing it yourself is best.”

“You mean… we’re dying?”

“Idiot! You think I’m gonna go all the way to the Western Ten Billion Buddha-Lands? No—we’re going west too, but only about two hours by train.”

“You really expect me to believe there’s a Paradise that close? We’re not going to see a kabuki play or something…”

---

2.

“Make it clear.”  
“Then perfect timing.” For some reason, Seiroku’s expression brightened. Silhouetted against the light streaming in from outside, he reached out his hand. “I’ll take you to Paradise right now.”  
“…To *where*, exactly?”  
“To Paradise. If you don’t get it, seeing it yourself is best.”  
“You mean… we’re dying?”  
“Idiot! You think I’m gonna go all the way to the Western Ten Billion Buddha-Lands? No—we’re going west too, but only about two hours by train.”  
“You really expect me to believe there’s a Paradise that close? We’re not going to see a kabuki play or something…”  

--- 2.

Sakamoto Kihachi clutched his head—then suddenly remembered something crucial he’d nearly forgotten. He lifted his bloodless face.  
“Did you tell Father?”  
“What are you talking about? We’re sneaking out in silence. Besides, I haven’t even told him I came back yet.”  
“No good—later, we’ll definitely get grilled for this.”  
“I won’t let us die, so don’t worry. Trust me.”  

Still hesitating, he was forcibly yanked forward by Seiroku.  
“If you keep cooped up in a storehouse like that, your future’s pitch black.”  

When they stepped outside, the sudden strong sunlight blinded them. Without their noticing, the garden trees had been covered in fresh, vibrant greenery—outside, summer had quietly begun in full force.  

“Since we’re sneaking out anyway, we might as well have gone to Ise instead,” Kihachi grumbled, sitting on the hard seat of the train.  
Beside him, Seiroku laughed. “A *nukemairi*, huh?”  

Long ago, when pilgrimage to Ise became popular among commoners, apprentices and maidservants alike would secretly slip away from their homes to visit. Because those who returned from such secret pilgrimages were believed to bring divine blessings of commercial prosperity, their masters reportedly never punished them.  

“If this *were* a nukemairi, maybe Father would forgive us. Anyway, wherever we’re headed now is probably just like festival side-stalls. Nothing special enough to impress anyone.”  

“Then where should I take you for you to be impressed?”  

He thought for a moment—and then recalled a picture postcard he’d once seen: the streets of a French town.

2.

"The same west, but only about two hours by train," he mused. "You mean… we’re dying?"  

To Ise became popular among commoners; apprentices and maidservants alike would secretly slip away from their homes to visit. Because those who returned from such secret pilgrimages were believed to bring divine blessings of commercial prosperity, their masters reportedly never punished them. “If this *were* a nukemairi, maybe Father would forgive us. Anyway, wherever we’re headed now is probably just like festival side-stalls. Nothing special enough to impress anyone.”  

“Then where should I take you for you to be impressed?” He thought for a moment—and then recalled a picture postcard he’d once seen: the streets of a French town.

A dazzling white, solemn triumphal arch, the Versailles Palace adorned with glittering decorations, and the Eiffel Tower stretching straight up into the sky. A foreign landscape so unlike Japan it seemed otherworldly, as if it belonged not to this realm.

"Yeah, that’s right. If you took me to France, I’d be impressed all right."

He smirked inwardly, expecting to stump the other—but Seiroku, far from being flustered, only grew more smug.

"Well then, perfect timing."

Hours later, Kihachi was in France.

Before his stunned, uncomprehending eyes loomed a massive whitewashed gate reminiscent of Paris’s Arc de Triomphe.

Kihachi struggled to recall: just moments ago, hadn’t he definitely disembarked the train in Umeda, Osaka, and walked all the way here to Tennoji? As he retraced his memories, Seiroku beside him puffed out his chest proudly.  

"This is the venue of the Fifth National Industrial Exhibition."

The National Industrial Exhibition—Japan’s own version of a “World’s Fair,” held five times since the Meiji era.

Since opening its borders, Japan had been striving to stand shoulder-to-shoulder with Western powers, absorbing their culture and technology, steadily building the strength of a first-class nation. Yet compared to the Western nations, there remained things Japan had yet to achieve.

One of them was hosting a World’s Fair—commonly known as a “World Expo.”

Western countries eagerly hosted Expos to showcase cutting-edge technologies and promote industry, but for Japan, still possessing only rudimentary technology, organizing such an event was utterly impossible. So instead, Japan devised an alternative: its very own World’s Fair—the National Industrial Exhibition.

2.

The exhibitions grew increasingly popular with each iteration, and by the thirty-sixth year of Meiji (1903), the fifth National Industrial Exhibition—heralded as the largest in history—was being held on the grounds of Tennoji in Osaka.

But Sakamoto Kihachi only learned of this fact much later; at that moment, he felt as though he had been spirited away by gods or spirits.

Passing through the gates using tickets Seiroku had bought beforehand, they entered a grand avenue centered with flowerbeds and fountains. The wide, deep thoroughfare teemed with visitors, and along both sides stood white Western-style buildings adorned with intricate ornamentation.

"It seems all these buildings are exhibition halls. Let's head first to that Machinery Hall over there."

Upon entering the Machinery Hall, a deafening roar like the snoring of demons assaulted his ears. The stench of iron and oil nearly burned his nose, and before his eyes, gleaming black machines churned out soap and cigarettes. Most of the machinery here appeared powered by electricity. While basking in the breeze from electric fans that eased the hall’s oppressive heat, Kihachi could do nothing but stand in utter awe of the power of electricity driving all these devices.

Hardly had he begun to grow accustomed to the machines when, upon entering the next hall—the Hall of Wonders—Kihachi’s courage shattered completely.

The exhibits were one marvel after another: during a demonstration of wireless telegraphy, he trembled at invisible radio waves; during an X-ray demonstration, the sight of rays piercing human flesh to reveal bones beneath left him paralyzed with fear.

"I’ve had enough! It’s too scary—I’m going home!"

2.

In the breeze from electric fans that eased the hall’s oppressive heat, Kihachi could do nothing but stand in utter awe of the power of electricity driving all these devices. Hardly had he begun to grow accustomed to the machines when, upon entering the next hall—the Hall of Wonders—Kihachi’s courage shattered completely. The exhibits were one marvel after another: during a demonstration of wireless telegraphy, he trembled at invisible radio waves; during an X-ray demonstration, the sight of rays piercing human flesh to reveal bones beneath left him paralyzed with fear. "I’ve had enough! It’s too scary—I’m going home!"  

Hearing Kihachi whine like this, Seiroku dragged him into the adjacent theater, saying, “All right then, just stop by here at the end.”  

Amidst a restless crowd in the pitch-dark auditorium, just as Kihachi began to feel uneasy about what might happen next, light flooded the stage—and to the accompaniment of music, a Western woman in a white dress began to dance.  

She spun with her white dress flared like wings, and colored lights bathed her form, shifting the gown from blue to violet and beyond. This beautiful dance—like a butterfly or flower fluttering through the air—was the Denki Hi Odori performed by the actress Carmansera. In an instant, Kihachi was utterly captivated.  

“That dance was incredible! Let’s watch it again!”  

Even after the performance ended, Kihachi remained ecstatic and unwilling to leave, but Seiroku, wearing an uncharacteristically troubled expression, muttered, “We don’t have that kind of money,” and half-carried him outside. By then, Kihachi’s terror had fully transformed into fascination.  

At Canada’s exhibition pavilion, canned fruits and tins of sweets were piled high nearly to the ceiling.  

As Kihachi gazed longingly up at this tower of confections, Seiroku—who had been smiling wryly—bought him a tin of biscuits wrapped with a red ribbon. Popping one into his mouth immediately, Kihachi was overwhelmed by a rich sweetness that spread throughout his palate, accompanied by a satisfyingly coarse texture.  

The *Kaikai Kiki*, a merry-go-round where wooden horses spun endlessly in circles, was another exhilarating delight. Riding atop one of the horse platforms, Kihachi laughed aloud with childlike joy alongside other children and even grown adults, sharing the moment freely with Seiroku.

2.

Having thus thoroughly enjoyed every corner of the exhibition, the two finally arrived at the observation deck of the Dairin Kōtō.

From the lookout, they could see not only the entire fairgrounds bathed in twilight, but also Osaka’s cityscape dotted with factory chimneys like branching veins, stretching as far north as Minoo.

“What a view, Kihachi. You can probably even see all the way to Kyoto.”

“The scenery’s amazing, but I was even more stunned by that ‘box’ we rode up here in.”

At fifty meters tall, the Dairin Kōtō used a Western contraption called an “elevator” to transport people to the observation level, and Kihachi marveled at its convenience.

“Just like the machines on display, it runs on electricity too.”

“Electricity’s incredible. So it can carry people up somewhere this high?”

“Even so, Japanese technology still has a long way to go. We’re behind the Paris World’s Fair from three years ago.”

Seiroku spoke of the Paris Exposition Universelle held in Meiji 33 (1900), where moving walkways carried visitors and a Ferris wheel over a hundred meters tall had been the talk of the event.

“Ah, I’d love to go to Paris someday,” Seiroku sighed, gazing out at the horizon.

“I’d climb the Eiffel Tower just once—that’s all I ask.”

“You never change, brother—still obsessed with getting to high places.”

2.

In a corner of the evening sky, the faint moon floated into view, and Kihachi found himself recalling the moving picture he had just seen for the first time in his life. It was *A Trip to the Moon*, based on a novel by Verne, a French writer—about Westerners launched inside a giant cannonball fired toward the moon.

“That moon in the moving picture… didn’t have the rice cake-pounding rabbit, did it?”

“What’re you talking about? You still believe that nonsense about a rabbit pounding rice cakes?”

“You never believed in any of that stuff, brother. You don’t even believe in Paradise.”

“I’ve told you enough times. I don’t believe in anything I can’t see with my own eyes.”

“…Not even Paradise? Then if there’s no Paradise… where do people go when they die?”

Seiroku placed a hand on Kihachi’s head as he gave a brief, quiet reply: “I don’t know.”

“But me? I plan to go to the moon when I die. I’ll go to the moon—the highest place above this earth—and find out once and for all whether that rabbit is really up there pounding rice cakes.”

With that, Seiroku wore his usual smug smile. Yet Kihachi noticed the shadow across that smile seemed deeper than usual—just as the sun had dipped considerably lower, and the crimson hues in the western sky had deepened. Night would come soon.

Descending from the tall tower, the two set off toward the main gate to begin their return. A bell rang, announcing the closing hour, and visitors poured from the exhibition halls into the broad avenue. The bustling scene before sunset in this unfamiliar place only heightened Kihachi’s unease; he wanted to leave quickly. But beside him, Seiroku walked slowly, unhurried.

2.

Seiroku wore his usual smug smile. Yet Kihachi noticed the shadow across that smile seemed deeper than usual—just as the sun had dipped considerably lower, and the crimson hues in the western sky had deepened. Night would come soon. Descending from the tall tower, the two set off toward the main gate to begin their return. A bell rang, announcing the closing hour, and visitors poured from the exhibition halls into the broad avenue. The bustling scene before sunset in this unfamiliar place only heightened Kihachi’s unease; he wanted to leave quickly. But beside him, Seiroku walked slowly, unhurried.

As if piling on Kihachi’s growing impatience, Seiroku suddenly stopped in his tracks.

“Sorry, let me just go to the toilet.”

“Huh?” Kihachi nearly cried out—but not wanting to seem weak or afraid, he reluctantly nodded instead.  
Seiroku said, “I’ll be back in about five minutes,” and dashed off.

Kihachi sat down on a bench near the fountain tower and opened the tin he’d been given at the Canada Pavilion.  
He regretted losing the red ribbon that had tied the can shut—he didn’t even notice when it disappeared—but one bite of the sweet biscuit made his gloomy mood vanish entirely. As he nibbled carefully at the precious treat, those five minutes flew by in an instant.

“Is the toilet crowded or something?”

Finishing every last crumb, he waited—ten, fifteen minutes… then twenty, thirty, forty. Gradually, it dawned on him that something was wrong. He stood from the bench and scanned the crowd, but saw no familiar face among the throng.

The sun had now completely set. As the white buildings around him grew dimmer and took on a deathly blue hue, warmth drained from Kihachi’s body. His chest fluttered with anxiety.

“Brother!”

He forced himself to shout, but there was no reply—only rising panic. Realizing he was now utterly alone in this strange place at night, with no idea how to get home, his teeth began to chatter. Tears welled up and spilled from his eyes.

Would he never make it back home?  
No—that couldn’t happen.  
If only he could return safely, he’d gladly take any number of fist-bumps from his father.

2.

As something lurking in the darkness seemed ready to attack, Kihachi froze in terror and crouched down on the spot, weeping uncontrollably. With a tear-streaked face, he desperately chanted over and over again: “Namu Amida Butsu, Namu Amida Butsu, Namu Amida Butsu.”

Ise-san, Amida Buddha—anyone, it didn’t matter.

“Someone, please—blow away this darkness!”

　　　　　　　　　　II

At Inariyama in Fushimi, Kyoto, stood the renowned Inari Shrine, long celebrated as the god of prosperous business. Besides the main hall at the foot of the mountain, numerous subsidiary shrines and sacred mounds dotted the mountainside. Even in 1907 (Meiji 40), the local *o-tsuka* faith remained strong—people continued to revere the deities and spirits dwelling in these shrines and mounds as their household gods.

Along the winding pilgrimage path threading through the sacred mounds stood countless vermilion *torii* gates. Hundred Rivers Inako ascended the steep, mountain-like trail, enduring the fierce heat of August. She wore a dark blue *kasuri* kimono tied with a mustard-yellow obi, adorned with an obidome shaped like a mouse and a rice bag. Her braid, tied up in a single knot at the back, bounced with each step. Through the mountain air filled with cicadas’ cries, rustling leaves, and the distant sound of norito prayers recited at shrines and mounds, the sharp *karakoro* clatter of Inako’s *kiri* wooden clogs echoed.

From Yotsutsuji midway up the slope, she climbed a long flight of stairs, passed through a cluster of sacred mounds, and emerged from the scrub forest into a more open area. From there, she could see the Kyoto Electric Railway crossing the Kamo River on Kanshin Bridge, the barracks of the 38th Infantry Regiment in Fukakusa, and the Seven Roads train station below.

2.

This well-situated vantage point held a sacred mound dedicated to Akubi Ōmyōjin, a deity worshipped here. Part of the mountainside around it was bare rock, exposed and rugged, and pressed right against this cliff face stood a donation box and a modest pedestal—hardly more than an afterthought. Upon the pedestal were numerous offerings: small and large *torii* gates, votive items, and other devotional gifts.

Facing the sacred mound, Inako dropped her offering into the box, bowed twice, clapped twice, then drew in a deep breath.

"Please… this time, let me do it smoothly.  
Let me not be clumsy again. Let Father not get angry with me this time. May the string of my shamisen never snap during practice. May I become as beautiful as Onee-chan soon. And also—"

After thoroughly bowing until she felt satisfied, Inako began chanting the *Ōharae no Kotoba*, the Great Purification Ritual.

"Takamano-hara, where the divine spirits dwell, Emperor-ancestral deities Kumuromi and Kumurogi no Mikoto, by whose grace—"

The scent of candles drifted through the air. Her voice blended into the buzzing cicadas and rustling leaves, merging seamlessly with nature until she could truly feel it: this was a place where the gods resided, far removed from the ordinary clamor of daily life. A quiet warmth spread through her heart.

Then, as if harmonizing with her voice, the sound of *kagura* music began to drift from somewhere nearby.

While continuing her norito, Inako tried to locate the source of the music—was there a *kagura* hall close by? Yet strangely, the melody seemed unmistakably to come from the thick foliage atop the rocky cliff.

2.

Inako looked up just as the sound stopped, and an awkward “Ah!” escaped her lips.

The bushes atop the rocky cliff swayed violently—then suddenly, a boy tumbled down in a rush, sliding headlong.

The falling boy cried out, and after dramatically kicking over a donated *torii* gate with his backside, landed flat on his rear. Clutched to his chest was some box-shaped machine.

Inako stood frozen, stunned by the sudden turn of events—until she abruptly clapped a hand over her mouth in realization.

“He’s finally descended…! The deity has truly come!”

Joy surged hotly within her, and clasping her hands together, Inako began frantically bowing before the boy.

“Akubi Ōmyōjin-sama, Akubi Ōmyōjin-sama, please, I beg you to grant my wish—”

“Ya dumbass.”

The boy cheerfully whacked Inako on the head with a horn-like part he’d detached from the machine.

“Ow! What was that for?”

“That’s *my* line. Who just bows to a human who fell off a cliff without even checking if they’re hurt?”

Regaining her composure, Inako stared intently at the boy. He wore a cotton *kasuri* shirt layered under a *kogura* hakama—utterly ordinary attire for a mere youth. Realizing this, she quickly lowered her head in apology.

“Forgive me, please! I honestly thought my devotion had been answered and the god himself had appeared!”

“How in the world do you even come up with something like that?”

The boy sighed, reattaching the horn to his device. Inako watched him with rapt curiosity.

“First time seeing a phonograph?” the boy asked.

2.

The boy placed the phonograph on the base of the shrine and grasped the side handle to wind the spring. Once wound, the cylinder mounted horizontally at the center began spinning rapidly. The moment he touched a slender needle attached to the bell—later, she would learn this part was called a “horn”—to the cylinder, *kagura* music burst from the horn.

“That’s the *kagura* you just heard. Amazing, isn’t it? But how does sound come out all by itself?”

When the boy stopped the phonograph, he pulled out a tube shaped like a rolled-up fish cake and gently placed it into Inako’s palm.

“This is called a *roukan*—a wax cylinder. See the fine grooves carved into its surface? A needle traces these grooves, and the vibrations reproduce the recorded sound. Since this one has singing on it, it costs just over one yen.”

Startled by the price, Inako nearly dropped the cylinder. The boy barely caught it before it hit the ground, his face twisting in genuine frustration. “You idiot! I told you not to drop it!”

“S-Sorry… but that price is shocking! You could ride the Kyoto Electric Railway a hundred times for that!”

“It’s still cheaper than flat discs. And wax cylinders have some interesting features too.”

The boy took another *roukan* from his pocket and attached it to the phonograph, but this time, no sound came out.

“No sound? Is it broken?” Inako tilted her head.

The boy simply repositioned the needle to the beginning.

*“No sound? Is it broken?”*

Hearing her own voice echo from the phonograph, Inako flinched in shock. The boy grinned, clearly pleased with himself.

“This is what makes wax cylinder phonographs so great—anyone can easily record sound like this.”

2.

"There’s some amazing machinery indeed. But why on earth are you carrying something like this around?"

"I was entrusted to repair the phonograph at a local parishioner’s house nearby, and I was just testing it on the sacred mound before delivering it. Then I accidentally slipped and dropped it."

"Oh, so that’s what happened earlier—"

Just as the mood began to soften, Inako finally noticed the desecrated state of the sacred mound—the *torii* and offerings scattered in disarray—and cried out in outrage: "How could someone do such a blasphemous thing!"

The boy visibly grimaced at her anger, repulsed. He placed the lid with a handle onto the phonograph and hung it from his hand, then shot Inako a look of clear disdain.

"Sorry, but I don’t believe in things I can’t see."

As the boy turned to leave, Inako swiftly grabbed his obi. She pointed at the debris strewn across the ground.

"If you made the mess, you ought to clean it up—regardless of faith or superstition."

Gritting his teeth, the boy reluctantly restored the *torii* and other items to their proper places. Then he faced the sacred mound and clasped his hands together.

Inako started to think, *At least he knows to pay respects properly*—until she caught the look in his eyes, and her breath caught in her throat.

Even as he stood with hands joined, the boy glared at the sacred mound with pure hatred.

It was a terrifying gaze, like one reserved for a mortal enemy. Without bowing, he turned sharply on his heel and walked away as if nothing had happened, muttering, “Well then, that’s that.”

Snapping back to reality, Inako realized she needed to apologize to the sacred mound for the boy’s disrespect. Flustered, she hastily resumed chanting the *norito*.

2.

“At least he knows to pay respects properly”—until she caught the look in his eyes, and her breath caught in her throat. Even as he stood with hands joined, the boy glared at the sacred mound with pure hatred. It was a terrifying gaze, like one reserved for a mortal enemy. Without bowing, he turned sharply on his heel and walked away as if nothing had happened, muttering, “Well then, that’s that.” Snapping back to reality, Inako realized she needed to apologize to the sacred mound for the boy’s disrespect. Flustered, she hastily resumed chanting the *norito*.

“Great Yamato, land of the high sunrise—”

Perhaps because the boy’s fierce glare was burned into her mind, the words of the *norito*, which she had perfectly memorized, suddenly vanished. As she struggled to recall them—

“Having established this land as a peaceful realm—”

a voice rang out nearby. It was the boy, reciting the next line. He cast a single, scornful glance at a stunned Inako, then walked away without another word.

—What nerve, that boy!

Having finished her visit to Akubi Ōmyōjin, Inako descended the approach path with puffed cheeks.

Not only had her sacred mound been desecrated, but now she’d been “helped” by a faithless human. There could be no greater humiliation.

Still fuming, she passed through the forest and reached the foot of the mountain, where wooden fences lined the road. Leaning against one was a beggar. From his white garments, a single leg was visible.

Inako stopped before him. “Forgive me, but… your left leg—?”

“Lost it in the Russo-Japanese War.”

As Inako had suspected, the beggar was one of the *haibei*—a war-wounded veteran of the Russo-Japanese War.

“Were you with the Fourth Division, then?”

“Yes. During the general assault on Hill 203 three years ago, an enemy shell exploded nearby…”

Whether the memory of battle had resurfaced, his lips trembled, and by the end his voice was nearly a whisper. Seeing this, Inako immediately reached into her purse and pulled out her meager coin.

“I… this is little, but…”

“…Much obliged. Sorry to trouble you.”

2.

Before him, the beggar reached for Inako’s single sen coin—but suddenly let out a shrill cry: “Kiee!”

Startled, Inako gasped in shock. The beggar writhed violently, and with a *thud*, his supposedly missing left leg emerged from inside the fence. A centipede coiled around his ankle. Ignoring Inako, who stood frozen in horror, the man sprinted wildly down the approach path.

Inako turned to the wall where the beggar had stood. At low height, there was a hole just large enough for a foot.

“So he stuck his leg through here… pretending to be one-legged?”

“Exactly, you stupid fool.”

As she peered into the hole, suddenly a face appeared from the other side—making Inako shriek and fall flat on her backside. The boy from earlier grinned at her through the gap, then leapt over the fence with effortless grace.

“The Fourth Division never went to Hill 203. You were about to be perfectly duped.”

“You’re that boy from before—”

“I saw you on my way back from delivering the phonograph, listening intently to that suspicious-looking man. Just then, a centipede happened to be crawling on the ground, so I used it to set a little trap.”

She shuddered, recalling the centipede. Ever since being bitten as a child, she’d hated them.

“But anyway,” the boy continued, “I’ll take generous payment for this extermination job.”

Seeing the sly twist of his lips, Inako looked down at the single sen in her hand—and without hesitation, thrust it toward him.

“It’s all I have.”

Taken aback by her response, the boy faltered. “Don’t take me seriously—I was joking.”

“This is my thanks. You saved me from being deceived.”

---

2.

"One sen is already too much."

"Then consider it an offering to the gods. I was helped by a mischievous deity."

Eventually, the boy gave in and reluctantly took the coin. "Thanks... uh..."

"Huh? What's wrong?"

"Me? I'm Hundred Rivers Inako."

"...Grasshopper?"

"It's *Inako*!" She kicked his shin.

"Ow—what the hell! That’s rude!"

"After all that talk about being bitten by centipedes, who’s the rude one here? Now what’s your name?"

The boy, eyes welling with tears at the kick, still managed to keep his smug grin.  
"Sakamoto. Kihachi."

Since we were heading the same way, I ended up walking down the approach of the shrine together with the boy who called himself Kihachi.

He was slightly taller than Inako, about the same age—fifteen. He’d been born at a temple in Ōtsu, and while helping out at a relative’s shop, he attended a middle school in Kyoto. Right now, he was on summer break.

The conversation turned back to the earlier wax cylinder phonograph. When Inako tilted her head and asked, “Who’s this ‘Edison’ you mentioned?” Kihachi looked utterly shocked.

"You don’t know Edison? He’s the god of invention!"

"Eh? A god? What kind of blessings does he give? And where’s he enshrined?"

"Don’t go killing him off—he’s alive and well in America!"

"Well then," Inako said, and facing what she assumed was the direction of America, she clasped her hands together in prayer.

"Maybe if I pay my respects, I’ll receive some small blessing too."

2.

He was currently on summer break. The conversation circled back to the wax cylinder phonograph they’d been discussing earlier. When Inako tilted her head and asked, “Who’s this ‘Edison’ you mentioned?” Kihachi looked utterly shocked.  
“You don’t know Edison? He’s the god of invention!”  
“Eh? A god? What kind of blessings does he give? And where’s he enshrined?”  
“Don’t go deifying him—he’s alive and well in America!”  
“Well then,” said Inako, and facing what she assumed was the direction of America, she clasped her hands together in prayer. “Maybe if I pay my respects, I’ll receive some small blessing too.”

“If that’s the case,” Kihachi said, “why not visit Hieizan Yamashina Hachimangū? Apparently, Edison once used bamboo from around there as filament material for his incandescent light bulbs—and it led to great success.”  

“I don’t know anything about ‘filament’ or whatever, but it sounds like there’d be real benefits. I should pay a visit next time.”  
“You’ll pray, ‘Please let things go smoothly,’ then?” Kihachi asked with a wry smile.  
Inako flushed, realizing her earlier prayer had been overheard.  
“…If I have another disaster like today’s, maybe that’s exactly what I’ll ask for.”

Inako was the second daughter of Hyakugawa Shuzō, a sake brewery in Fushimi.  
Her father, Jin’emon, was a man who embodied the spirit of the old samurai. From childhood, Inako had been rigorously trained in etiquette and various traditional arts. While her elder sister mastered each discipline effortlessly, Inako—naturally clumsy in all things—repeatedly made blunders, each one drawing Jin’emon’s thunderous wrath.

The worst incident had occurred just that morning.

A prestigious guest was due to arrive that evening, so the entire family was cleaning. Inako had been diligently using a *hae toriki*—a fly trap consisting of a small metal basin filled with pungent lamp oil meant to lure and drown flies—when she suddenly remembered another errand. She left the device unattended in the hallway and walked away.

Moments later, a loud crashing sound echoed down the corridor. Alarmed, she rushed back—only to find Jin’emon waiting there, a grotesque sight: the overturned basin perched atop his head, oil dripping from his face along with trapped flies, his expression that of a vengeful demon.

2.

After being yelled at mercilessly, I was kicked out with the excuse that I’d only made more work for everyone by trying to clean.”

Sakamoto Kihachi was laughing so hard beside me he was nearly choking, but frustratingly, I couldn’t offer any defense.

The two of us descended the mountain and arrived at the back approach to the shrine. Along the path, souvenir shops displayed rows of the local specialty—Fushimi dolls—while teahouses lined up offering ice water and amazake.

Kihachi, perhaps feeling a bit peckish, took out a biscuit wrapped in paper and began nibbling at it daintily with his front teeth. He offered me one, but I politely declined—it looked cheap and low-quality just from a glance.

“Is it because your family enshrines Akubi Ōmyōjin at home that you visit that sacred mound, Inako?”

“No, that’s not it. Our family’s sacred mound is Goshendani. We visit Akubi Ōmyōjin’s mound because… if your faith is recognized, Akubi Ōmyōjin sends you a letter.”

Kihachi’s face twisted as he chewed, and he subtly edged a little farther away from Inako.

“I see… Yeah… I guess stranger things do happen.”

His eyes held a clear trace of pity. It was the unmistakable look of someone who thinks a woman has lost her mind.

“It’s true, really. When I was a child, my sister actually received a letter from Akubi Ōmyōjin.”

“I’ve heard of distant believers sending prayers by letter, but never of anyone actually receiving one from a god.”

Inako pouted, truly indignant that her words were being doubted.

2.

“Ōmyōjin sends you a letter.” Kihachi’s face twisted as he chewed, and he subtly edged a little farther away from Inako. “I see… Yeah… I guess stranger things do happen.” His eyes held a clear trace of pity. It was the unmistakable look of someone who thinks a woman has lost her mind. “It’s true, really. When I was a child, my sister actually received a letter from Akubi Ōmyōjin.”  
“I’ve heard of distant believers sending prayers by letter, but never of anyone actually receiving one from a god.”  
Inako pouted, truly indignant that her words were being doubted.  

“Anyway, that’s why I visit Akubi-san’s sacred mound. Besides that, I go to vent—I’m slow-witted, don’t have many friends, so I just talk to the gods. The offering money? That’s my consultation fee.”

“You actually believe a god listens to your complaints?”

“Believing is all I’ve got—that’s my one strength.”

She said it with utter confidence. Kihachi merely flicked biscuit crumbs from his fingers and muttered, “Huh.”

“Sakamoto-san, why were *you* at Akubi-san’s sacred mound?”

“Our household enshrines Akubi Ōmyōjin. My grandmother received the deity through training long ago.”

It’s said that those who train under a certain person named Odai at an Inari shrine can be granted the power of the sacred mound’s deity. Though Kihachi’s family belongs to a temple household, a special *hokora* containing the divided spirit (bunrei) of Akubi Ōmyōjin—granted to his grandmother—is enshrined in their kamidana. He’d been taught again and again, “The god will escape if you open it—never open this shrine.” As children, Kihachi and his older brother had called it “the forbidden shrine.”

Just as Inako began to admire him for dutifully making offerings, Kihachi laughed and denied it.

“The view’s great up there. I often sit on that spot and eat my lunch.”

At such irreverent words, Inako thought once again: *Yeah, I really hate this guy.*

When they reached the Kyoto Electric Railway stop, a single tramcar stood waiting—its appearance like a white-and-brown *uhiro* sweet. This tram, which chimed *chin, chin* as it ran, was affectionately known among people as the Keiden.

2.

A few years ago, a boy known as the *saki-bari*—a runner who dashed ahead of the tram—would race in front of the train, shouting, “Here comes the tram! Watch out, danger’s coming!” But due to the risks and heavy labor involved, the practice had been discontinued a few years prior, replaced instead by safety nets mounted on both ends of the tramcar to prevent accidents.

“Yeah. Since I’ve got it, might as well pass it along.”

Kihachi paused as he was about to board, then handed over an amuletic slip. It bore the Seven Lucky Gods and the shop name “Hōrai Butsugu-ten,” along with the phrase *“Mechanical Repairs Undertaken,”* an address—and unusually—also a telephone number.

“If any of your machines break, come to us. First time, we’ll settle it with a nice biscuit.”

Inako frowned, puzzled. A Buddhist goods shop offering mechanical repairs? What on earth was this? But just then, the bell chimed—*chin, chin*.

“If you’ve got complaints, bring a penny. I’ll listen in place of that god whose existence nobody’s sure of.”

With that flippant remark, Kihachi leapt onto the departing tram and rode away.

“Saying such disrespectful things again…”

Fuming, Inako looked down at the slip—and noticed writing on the back.

“‘Electrical Catalogue’…?”

Alongside that line, the reverse side was covered in hasty scribbles: terms like *Yoru Shirazu*, *Denpō*, and explanatory notes, even something resembling a rough diagram. Some parts were crossed out with diagonal lines, as if it were a personal memo. She wondered if she should have even accepted this, but with no way to return it, Inako reluctantly tucked the slip away, though it nagged at her mind.

---

2.

Leaving Inari Shrine and heading south through Fujinomori toward the Sumizome area, Inako arrived at the entrance of a small pleasure district.

Though far smaller than Gion or Shimabara, it was an old hanamachi where tales lingered of Ōkimi no Kurano-suke from *Chūshingura* having once frequented its teahouses. It should have been quiet during the day, yet today a lively chorus rang out:

“Kowa harakara o / Horobi yori / Sukawan tame no / Ikusa nari—”

“They’ve got the Salvation Army here.”

A group clad in navy-blue military caps and uniforms embroidered with “Salvation Army” marched down the street lined with brothels, playing trumpets and drums in time with the hymn *Kowa Harakara o*. 

Although human trafficking had been banned since the Prostitute Emancipation Edict of Meiji 5 (1872), countless women still ended up in the pleasure quarters due to poverty and unemployment. The Salvation Army, a Protestant Christian sect, stood at the forefront of the movement to abolish prostitution.

Despite its martial name, their work remained strictly peaceful. Right now, they were engaged in what they called *no*, *sen*, or field evangelism—distributing tracts and pamphlets like bullets to passersby.

Suddenly spotting a figure she thought she recognized at the front of the procession, Inako started forward—only for a bald man with a booming voice to bellow, “What d’you think you’re doin’, huh?!” as he stepped squarely into the path of the marchers. Startled, Inako ducked behind a stack of wooden barrels under a shop’s eaves.

“You scum! How dare you interfere with folks’ livelihoods, eh?”

---

2.

“We’ve come to save women who are suffering unjust treatment!”

The man at the front was noticeably larger and sturdier than the others—so much so that he almost looked Western.

“You are sinners just as we are. Unless you repent, you can never become true human beings. Wake up—quickly!”

“We’ve been wide awake for ages. You lot keep spouting nonsense, over and over.”

“The law permits prostitutes to freely abandon their profession, and forbids any interference. Come with us, and we’ll give you training to reintegrate into society.”

A window on the second floor of a brothel flew open, and a tobacco tray came hurtling down, striking the Salvation Army man directly on the head. His military cap tumbled off, revealing his closely cropped hair—but showing no sign of pain, he shot a sharp glare upward toward the window.

“It’s him… it’s definitely Kumi-san.”

That towering figure was Kumi Ken’go, someone Inako knew well. As Kumi brushed ash from his uniform, laughter erupted behind him, and one after another, men poured out from inside the brothel, quickly surrounding the Salvation Army group.

“Get ‘em!” the men shouted, lunging at the Salvation Army. Chaos erupted in the pleasure district.

Amid swirling dust, several men pummeled the missionaries, who offered no real resistance, only repeating with stubborn resolve: “Hear our words! Hear our message!”

As Inako worried for Kumi’s safety, the bald-headed man turned sharply toward her, fixing her with a piercing stare. She froze like a frog caught in a snake’s gaze.

In an instant, the bald man strode over. “Are you one of their accomplices?” he snarled, roughly seizing her wrist. Inako let out a small cry—and the moment she did, Kumi ceased his preaching, recognizing her at once.

2.

Kumi stood dumbfounded for a moment—but the instant he grasped the situation, his expression darkened sharply. As Kumi turned toward her, two men lunged at him simultaneously from both sides.

Kumi transformed into the wrathful visage of Fudō Myō-ō. With effortless ease, he seized their arms and flung them aside as if they were weightless.

The men staggered back in shock as their comrades soared over Kumi’s head. Charging forward like a storm, Kumi scattered the surrounding attackers with a single thrust, then rushed to Inako’s side. With one hand, he grabbed the bald-headed man by the collar and hoisted him into the air.

To Inako, who had collapsed onto the ground, Kumi spoke in an unexpectedly gentle voice:

“I didn’t expect you to be here. Stay right where you are.”

The bald man, hurled away by Kumi, plunged headfirst into a stack of wooden barrels, collapsing under the wreckage as they toppled.

Once more surging into motion, Kumi seized each assailant by the nape of the neck and hurled them one after another. Those who clung to his body were dragged like runaway locomotives as he charged wildly across the district, unstoppable in every direction.

“No wonder they call him ‘Roku-Jōki’—just like steam-powered force incarnate.”

During the Russo-Japanese War, Kumi had earned distinction in fierce battles at Nanzan, Fengtian, and elsewhere. His relentless, fearless charges—even against enemy Maxim machine guns—had drawn comparison to a locomotive, earning him the nickname “Roku-Jōki,” derived from the “Roku” in his name.

Now, no one in the pleasure quarter dared stand against Kumi. The attackers scattered like spiders’ young fleeing in all directions, vanishing into brothels. At last, calm returned. Kumi exchanged a few brief words with his companions, and the Salvation Army, now reformed into orderly ranks, departed the hanamachi—leaving only Kumi behind.

“Inako, are you hurt?”

2.

Watching the Salvation Army march away, Kumi approached, readjusting his military cap and brushing dust from his soiled uniform. The fury on his face had completely faded, replaced by his usual stern expression.

“Thanks to you… I’m truly grateful for your help.”

“As long as you’re safe, that’s what matters most. But we shouldn’t stand around talking here—let’s get going.”

Prompted by Kumi, the two left the hanamachi. Heading south, they soon reached the bridge spanning Sumizome Incline, where a single boat mounted on a cart was being hauled upward by steel cables beneath their feet.

“You got hit in the head with a tobacco tray earlier—were you alright?”

“That didn’t bother me, but what came after—that’s another matter. Forcing people into submission by brute strength… that’s not the proper way of spreading our faith.”

“But still, thanks to you, I was saved.”

“Was I? Acting without thinking—I always rush in headfirst. It’s my worst habit.”

“Oh, then—if you don’t mind—please come by my house. I’d like to thank you properly.”

At Inako’s invitation, Kumi made a grimace. “No, that won’t be necessary.”

“Your sister should be home too, I imagine.”

“She’s *exactly* why it would be a problem,” he muttered.

Inako frowned slightly, her brows drawing together. “Don’t be shy. Come on—we’ll have some good tea together.”

After the faintest twitch of his eyebrows, Kumi reluctantly fell into step behind Inako, who began trotting lightly ahead.

2.

“There was a strange machine at the Hyakugawa Shuzō main house called the *Ryōfūki*—a cooling device. It consisted of a rectangular box with a *uchiwa* fan inserted inside; when wound up, the fan moved up and down to blow air. In practice, though, every time it was used, it only sent out a faint, lukewarm breeze, making the room actually feel cooler once the spring had run down.

Yet perhaps this *Ryōfūki* had finally shown its power at last—the air in the sitting room now felt unusually chilly for summer.

Across from Kumi, seated at a rosewood table, sat his elder sister Noriko, back straight, in formal seiza posture.

Her pale skin and long, lustrous black hair hung straight down, a portion of it tied at the back with a red ribbon. She wore a high-quality summer kimono of *Ōmi jōfu* striped cotton, immaculately fastened with a purple *obi*.

As Kumi sipped the tea Noriko had poured, her previously closed eyes snapped sharply open.

“I was startled—what could possibly be the matter that you’d come so suddenly?”

“I was told to stay a while. I couldn’t very well refuse so rudely.”

“That odd uniform again—surely you’re meddling in some unnecessary business once more.”

“The Salvation Army established a Kyoto unit this year. A friend asked me to help out for a short time.”

“You’ve already spent the pension from your hard-earned war medal on all sorts of frivolous things, haven’t you.”

“It’s not for frivolity. Most of it goes toward charitable work.”

“How noble—chasing after fallen women. Truly admirable charity.”

“What are you saying—” “What is it?” Sparks flew between them in a fierce exchange of glances, and Inako pressed a hand to her forehead.

2.

It had been decided that Roku would be Noriko’s husband-to-be before the Russo-Japanese War even began. Roku was the second son of Riku Tsuneichi Shōten, a major rice wholesaler in Ōtsu who supplied the rice used in the Hyakugawa Shuzō brewery, and had been a familiar face to Noriko since childhood.  

Yet, problematically, the two were like cat and dog. Ever since they were young, Roku had always interfered in Noriko’s every move, and Noriko had responded with sarcasm and sharp-tongued remarks. Still, out of mutual obligation, they had continued to meet on formal terms—and though people had assumed they would marry after Roku completed his military service and was honorably discharged last December, there had still been no sign of any wedding preparations.  

Some whispered that Noriko had put the engagement on hold, frustrated by Roku’s obsession with charitable work. But when Inako asked for the full story, Noriko refused to say anything.  

This year, Roku would turn twenty-four, and Noriko twenty—yet both remained unmarried.  

Just as their glaring standoff reached its peak, the *Ryōfūki* stopped. Roku grimaced and glanced at the device.  

“I’ve been meaning to ask—why didn’t you just buy a battery-powered electric fan?”  

“Because this was cheaper,” Noriko replied dryly. “A classic case of *buy cheap, buy twice*, I suppose.”  

Roku nodded vaguely. “I see.” He drained the rest of his tea and set down his teacup.  

“Please,” he said curtly.  

Without a word, Noriko refilled his cup just as curtly.  

As Inako stood there, utterly exasperated by the pair’s behavior, the sliding door to the parlor slid open. “Roku, you’re here?” Her father, Jinbei’emon, peered in. Instantly, Inako’s stomach clenched tight.

---

2.

Dressed in matching *ōshima tsumugi* kimono and haori, Jinbei’emon had a composed demeanor, though his tightly closed mouth sported stiff Kaiser-style mustaches stretching sharply to either side. The moment he sat down and glanced at Inako, he said with unmistakable sarcasm,  
“So you’ve finally come back? Had your fill of gallivanting around, I suppose.”  

Jinbei’emon lit a Golden Bat cigarette and exhaled smoke without so much as a satisfied expression.  

“Mr. Jinbei’emon. I was told an important guest would be arriving soon.”  

“Good. Roku, you may stay as well. Our guest is Mr. Mishō Yōsuke—a man of the Mishō family, eldest son of Mishō Shōten. He’s just been appointed branch manager of their Fushimi branch and is coming to pay his respects.”  

Mishō Shōten was a wealthy merchant house headquartered in Matsusaka, long established as a liquor wholesaler. Since the Meiji era, they had followed the examples of Mitsui and Sumitomo, using their vast capital to expand into banking and coal mining operations.  

When the Battle of Toba–Fushimi devastated Fushimi, the Hyakugawa storehouse suffered partial damage, throwing their business into crisis. At that time, it was the Fushimi branch of Mishō Shōten that rescued them by distributing Fushimi sake to Tokyo—then an untapped market—as “downstream” sake, reviving their sales. Ever since, Mishō Shōten had remained a patron beyond repayment.  

“The previous branch manager was quite elderly. Does this Mr. Yōsuke have experience in trade?”  

“No need to worry on that count. After all, he is none other than the renowned Dr. Paikaru.”  

“Dr. Paikaru?” Roku exclaimed, eyes widening in surprise.

---

2.

During the Russo-Japanese War, there was a well-known professor at Imperial University who aggressively advocated that “Russia’s territory east of Lake Baikal should be ceded to Japan,” earning him the public nickname of “Dr. Baikal.”  

Around the same time, a strange figure emerged in the Kansai region. Though young, this man had traveled the world; upon returning to Japan, he had drunk every kind of liquor in every country he visited. He then established a new department for importing Western alcohol within his family’s store, and using the connections and distribution networks he had built overseas, rapidly expanded its business.

“I keep hearing rumors about this ‘Dr. Paikaru’—things like ‘he downed nearly two *shō* of wine in one go at a German village tavern,’ or ‘drank an entire *to* of liquor in Qing China and composed a hundred poems,’ or even ‘gets stronger in fights when drunk’—all of it sounds utterly dubious…”

His true nature remains largely unknown, overshadowed by outlandish and fantastical rumors. Yet one thing appears certain: at some point, he boldly declared,  
“Having tasted countless fine and rare liquors, I affirm that nothing surpasses Qing白酒—known also as *Paikaru*.”  

From then on, this man came to be spoken of alongside the aforementioned professor as “Baikal of the East, Paikaru of the West.”  

And this Dr. Paikaru—the very man who brought immense profits to Mitsue Shōten through imported spirits—was none other than their eldest son, Yōsuke.  

“He is, after all, the one destined to inherit the Mitsue household. …So, Inako. Be extremely careful not to cause any offense.”  

As Jinbei’emon fixed her with a stern glare, Inako stiffened—just as a maid called out from beyond the sliding door.

---

2.

“Fine daughters indeed. I’m nearly thirty myself and still unmarried—can’t say I understand the feelings of a parent with children, but it must be a joy to see such beauties grow up.”

“They’re still far from fully grown. Especially my second daughter—I’m at my wit’s end with how slow she is.”

“Did you say ‘Inako-chan’? How old are you, then?”

Startled by being addressed so suddenly, Inako stammered, “F-fifteen.”

Yōsuke rubbed his chin, watching her with keen interest. Seeing Inako fidgeting stiffly in formal seiza, Jinbei’emon seemed to fear she’d soon embarrass them all, and let out a sharp, deliberate cough.

“That’s enough greetings for now. Let me show you to the storehouse.”

The long, two-story sake warehouse stood adjacent to the main house, where brewing and storage operations were conducted. Sake was traditionally brewed in winter—called *kanzukuri*—when temperatures were ideal for fermentation, so at this time of year, the *kurabito* workers had returned to their hometowns to tend the fields, leaving the warehouse quiet and empty.

2.

“F-fifteen.” Yōsuke rubbed his chin, watching her with keen interest. Seeing Inako fidgeting stiffly in formal seiza, Jinbei’emon seemed to fear she’d soon embarrass them all, and let out a sharp, deliberate cough. “That’s enough greetings for now. Let me show you to the storehouse.”

The long, two-story sake warehouse stood adjacent to the main house, where brewing and storage operations were conducted. Sake was traditionally brewed in winter—called *kanzukuri*—when temperatures were ideal for fermentation, so at this time of year, the *kurabito* workers had returned to their hometowns to tend the fields, leaving the warehouse quiet and empty.

Jinbei’emon used his favorite bamboo-root-carved cane as a pointer, guiding Yōsuke through the storehouse. Nearby, Inako watched from a distance, while Roku stood beside her with arms crossed, silently observing. Noriko, who disliked the smell of the brewery, held a handkerchief to her pinched face.

“This is a magnificent storehouse,” Yōsuke said dramatically, spreading his arms wide. He had thought that Dr. Paikaru, who prided himself on having tasted every wine in the world, would naturally feel pride for his own country’s sake too—but then he added, “However,” sharply tapping his Western shoe against the floor.

“That is precisely why I am deeply uncertain whether Japanese sake can become a drink worthy of the new century.”

Inako could hardly believe her ears as Yōsuke spoke with solemn distress.

“Sake production depends on the grueling labor of *kurabito*, carries high risks of failure, and still relies mostly on human intuition rather than instruments. How can such an unstable drink, bound by the outdated notion of ‘tradition,’ possibly compete with foreign liquor?”

“What harsh words these are.”

Even as his own livelihood was being directly disparaged, Jinbei’emon merely smiled wryly. But Inako, whose insides churned with fury, raised her voice and stepped toward Yōsuke.

“That’s not right at all! The techniques of the *tōji* master brewers and the *kurabito* have been passed down and refined over hundreds of years. Without relying on thermometers, our brewers can judge the temperature of water with just their fingertips!”

Yōsuke, eyes widening in surprise, soon twisted his mouth into the mischievous grin of a child who has just conceived a prank.

2.

"But how many years does it take for an individual to master that skill? And even if someone claims to have perfected it, how can you prove it? I think sticking in a thermometer is much quicker."

"That’s…," Inako faltered, at a loss for words.

"Still, everyone works with care and sincerity. Sake is the drink of the gods, after all."

Yōsuke stared blankly for a moment, then burst into loud laughter that echoed throughout the vast brewery.

"I give up, I give up. You win. When someone drags the gods into an argument, there's no fighting back. ...But if it's such a sacred drink, then why does the sake spoil during brewing? If its quality depends entirely on the capricious will of the gods, no matter how devoutly you pray, believing in it is just a waste."

As Inako opened her mouth to retort, Jinbei’emon’s cane sharply tapped her arm.

"Hold your tongue. I told you not to meddle in sake-making."

Jinbei’emon gave her a cold, indifferent stare as Inako winced from the pain. She tried to protest again, but Roku gently grasped her shoulder, forcing her to fall silent.

"She’s quite spirited," Yōsuke said with a smile, his gaze clinging to Inako like syrup.

That night, having changed into their sleeping garments, Inako and Noriko stood together on the veranda lit by a paper lantern. Both wore plain white *chirimen* cotton yukata—Inako’s patterned with blue *fuki* (butterbur), Noriko’s with blue *ayame* (iris).

2.

Jinbei’emon’s cane sharply tapped her arm. "Hold your tongue. I told you not to meddle in sake-making." Jinbei’emon gave her a cold, indifferent stare as Inako winced from the pain. She tried to protest again, but Roku gently grasped her shoulder, forcing her to fall silent. "She’s quite spirited," Yōsuke said with a smile, his gaze clinging to Inako like syrup. That night, having changed into their sleeping garments, Inako and Noriko stood together on the veranda lit by a paper lantern. Both wore plain white *chirimen* cotton yukata—Inako’s patterned with blue *fuki* (butterbur), Noriko’s with blue *ayame* (iris).

Letting their hair down, Noriko continued playing a leisurely tune on the shamisen, while Inako sat on the veranda, swinging her legs and listening to the music. Through the space between the persimmon tree planted in the inner garden and the eaves, the starry sky peeked through, and now and then the smoke from a mosquito-repelling coil tickled Inako’s nose as she gazed upward.

“I was terrified when you suddenly defied Dr. Paikaru in broad daylight,” Noriko said, not pausing her playing.

“Well, he said something insulting, like we’re just dumb kurabito.”

“In situations like that, you just pretend not to hear. You don’t take everything so seriously.”

“You can say that because you’re skilled at everything.”

Inako had always felt that compared to her sister, there were far too many things she couldn’t do. Noriko was proper in conduct, elegant, and talented—flawless in everything she undertook. She played countless shamisen tunes, poured tea so refined it made guests hum “mumu,” and sewed garments fit for a wedding ceremony.

In contrast, Inako was clumsy and inept at everything. She had broken countless shamisen strings, poured tea so bad it made guests grunt “ugh,” and when she tried sewing, she produced a massive *teru teru bōzu* large enough to hang under the eaves.

“All I’ve got going for me is praying to the gods.”

“That’s not true at all. You’ve got plenty of things I don’t.”

Inako turned to look at her, skeptical, but Noriko kept playing the shamisen with a composed expression. The instrument’s body rested against her right thigh as she sat in seiza, and Inako laid her head on Noriko’s left thigh, sprawling lazily on her side.

2.

“Nako, you’re in the way,” Noriko said, offering a token warning, but didn’t stop playing.

Inako soaked in the soft warmth, gently combing her fingers through Noriko’s hair that spilled over her sleeping clothes.

Inako’s dark blue *kasuri* kimono was made of the same *Ōmi jōfu* fabric as Noriko’s white *kasuri*, but because Inako constantly snagged and frayed it, the cloth had become badly worn. That’s why every time she asked to borrow Noriko’s white *kasuri*, she was flatly refused: “If I lend it to you, it’ll come back looking like a dishrag.”

Noriko—with her refined beauty, long, lustrous black hair, and elegant white *kasuri*—was Inako’s ideal. Secretly, Inako rejoiced that her own hair, which she had painstakingly grown out, was finally nearing the same length as Noriko’s.

“Hey, Onee-chan… When are you and Roku-san going to make up?”

“What’s brought this on all of a sudden?”

“I keep trying to get you two together, but you just keep fighting.”

“Don’t go meddling. Think about your own future. You’re old enough to marry now, you know.”

Inako’s heart leapt. That’s right—she was at an age where marriage wouldn’t be surprising at all. She let her thoughts wander: what kind of man would she end up marrying? But then again, whether anyone would even have her was questionable.

“If I were to marry, I’d want someone like Roku-san. He’s strong, and dependable.”

“Oh really? Well then, I’ll give him to you anytime.”

Noriko said it calmly, without a trace of jealousy, then slightly twisted her lips into a mischievous smirk.

“Tell me, what would you do if your suitor turned out to be Dr. Paikaru?”

2.

Her heart leapt. That’s right—she was at an age where marriage wouldn’t be surprising at all. She let her thoughts wander: what kind of man would she end up marrying? But then again, whether anyone would even have her was questionable.  
“If I were to marry, I’d want someone like Roku-san. He’s strong, and dependable.”  
“Oh really? Well then, I’ll give him to you anytime.” Noriko said it calmly, without a trace of jealousy, then slightly twisted her lips into a mischievous smirk. “Tell me, what would you do if your suitor turned out to be Dr. Paikaru?”

“Absolutely not. Those sideburns look like centipede antennae—just looking at them makes my skin crawl.”

“Same here,” Noriko replied with a faint smile, and the two of them giggled together.

A week had passed. Since morning, the sun had been blazing, and the oppressive, steamy heat made it feel like a boiling cauldron from hell. Just as she was growing weary of it, Yōsuke arrived again. He seemed to have something important to discuss, and was engaged in a long conversation with Jinbei’emon in the guest room.

Since the maids were busy, Inako took the Ryōfūki to the guest room instead. Carefully supporting the device with both hands, she approached the guest room—and paused just outside, catching the conversation through the fusuma.

“That’s precisely why I believe the secret sake once brewed by Miss Hyakugawa is exactly the kind of drink suited for the twentieth century. Wouldn’t you consider brewing it again?”

*Secret sake?* Inako perked up, unable to help listening in.

“I don’t know what the previous branch manager told you, but that is certainly not a product capable of meeting Mr. Mitsue’s expectations.”

“As you know, we’re currently in a postwar depression, and frankly speaking, sales of Hyakugawa’s sake haven’t been favorable. It’s about time you produced something that would awaken the people’s interest.”

In response to Jinbei’emon’s silence, Yōsuke clicked his tongue.

“If you’re unable to do that… how about this—why not take this opportunity to switch over to a beer brewery?”

The Ryōfūki slipped from Jinbei’emon’s suddenly limp hands.

By the time Inako gasped, it was too late—the device crashed to the floor with a piercing clatter.

2.

“What’s this?” Jinbei’emon appeared from the guest room, and upon seeing the disaster, immediately glared at Inako.  
“Ah… um… it was hot, so I brought the Ryōfūki…”  

Jinbei’emon shoved past Inako and picked up the fallen cooling device lying on the floor. He reattached the detached *uchiwa* fan and wound the spring. Inako watched anxiously, praying for it to work—but the machine emitted only a grating noise, as if its gears refused to mesh. The fan remained lifeless, and her stomach twisted into knots.  

“Is this the best you can do?! Can’t even carry a simple thing properly, you fool!”  

As Jinbei’emon’s thunderous rebuke left her trembling to the core, Yōsuke strolled in, disturbingly unperturbed.  
“Haha. So ‘slow-witted’ really does apply.”  

“I deeply apologize. I’ve made a terrible spectacle of myself.”  

Ignoring Jinbei’emon’s bow, Inako timidly spoke up: “Um… that earlier talk about the beer factory… was that really true?”  

Yōsuke blinked once, then burst into genuine laughter.  
“Of course it was a joke. You actually believed me? How refreshingly gullible.”  

*A joke.* Inako had barely registered the relief when Jinbei’emon’s roar snapped her back to reality.  
“Mind your own damn business! And more importantly—what do you plan to do about *this* mess?!”  

Jolted by the outburst, Inako shrank down once more.  
“Y-yes… um… I know someone who could fix it…”  

She darted away like a fugitive and returned moments later, clutching the蓬莱仏具店’s signboard from her room.  

2.

How refreshingly gullible.” *A joke.* Inako had barely registered the relief when Jinbei’emon’s roar snapped her back to reality. “Mind your own damn business!
