Jinbei’emon took the signboard but eyed it skeptically. “A *Buddhist goods shop* doing mechanical repairs?”

“I wondered the same,” Inako replied, “but the person who gave me this seemed to know quite a bit about machines.”

“This smells fishy. There’s a phone number, but no way to verify it.”

“In that case, let me check it for you,” said Yōsuke, pulling a thick directory of Kyoto phone numbers from his large bag. “I carry it for work. Lucky timing.”

While Yōsuke looked up the number, Inako watched anxiously. Moments later, he looked up and smiled. “Found it.” A wave of relief washed over her.

“Though—it’s listed under a different name: ‘Rokkaku Dry Goods Store.’”  

At Yōsuke’s next words, Inako froze, her hopeful expression still fixed on her face.

Jinbei’emon let out a deep, weary sigh, as if wrung from every fiber of his body.  

“To embarrass yourself in front of customers, only to end up duped by some shady character whose identity we can’t even confirm… Just how much must you disgrace the Hyakugawa name before you’re satisfied?”

“Father, please listen—I never meant—”

Jinbei’emon swung the cane he was holding and brought it down sharply on Inako’s head. A searing pain shot through her skull, blurring her vision with tears. Yōsuke gave a small chuckle. “Now, now, Mr. Hyakugawa…”

“Inako-chan is an honest girl. She’s the type who’d say sake is the drink of the gods without thinking.”

“No, she’s just dull-witted. …Go back to your room. Excuse us, gentlemen—we’ll continue our discussion later.”

With heavy, furious breaths, Jinbei’emon strode back into the reception room.

2.

“How much more must you disgrace the Hyakugawa name before you’re satisfied?”  
“Father, please listen—I never meant—”  
Jinbei’emon swung the cane he was holding and brought it down sharply on Inako’s head. A searing pain shot through her skull, blurring her vision with tears. Yōsuke gave a small chuckle.

With heavy, furious breaths, Jinbei’emon strode back into the reception room.

When Yōsuke was about to return the signboard with a muttered, “What a disaster,” he paused, catching sight of the writing on its reverse side.  
“What’s this?” he said, scanning the back—and instantly, the smile vanished from his face.  
“*Denki Mokuroku*…”  
His voice was one of utter disbelief. Noticing Inako’s gaze, Yōsuke quickly handed the signboard back. “Well then,” he said, forcing a smile before stepping into the parlor.  
In the now-quiet hallway, Inako traced the phone number on the signboard with downcast eyes. Then her gaze was suddenly drawn to the address scribbled beside it.  
It read: Shin-Kyōgoku.  
A faint glimmer of determination flickered in Inako’s eyes as she stared at the signboard.

That same afternoon. Carrying a *ryōfūki* wrapped in a furoshiki, Inako rode north along Kawaramachi-dōri on the tram.  
It had been years since she’d last come this way—since childhood, when she’d visited the Great Buddha at Hōkō-ji. As she gazed out the window, a sudden sight startled her: a pointed roof and a brick Western-style church. A woman beside her explained it was the newly built St. John’s Church, completed this year. Its pristine, majestic appearance moved Inako so deeply that she instinctively clasped her hands and bowed.

Continuing along Kitayama-dōri, where lush willows lined the banks of the Takase River, she disembarked at Shijō-Kobashi stop. She walked west along Shijō-dōri, a narrow street cutting through the city from east to west, and arrived at Shin-Kyōgoku-dōri.

Shin-Kyōgoku-dōri, established in the early Meiji era, was Kyoto’s liveliest district. Along the thoroughfare stood hat shops, *geta* stores, and theaters, bustling with housewives accompanied by maids, elderly men with shaved heads, and students. Under the harsh summer sun, nearly everyone frantically waved fans or *uchiwa* to cool their faces.

2.

Every tea shop along the way displayed signs reading “Ice Shop.” Workers sat on benches beneath the eaves, savoring ice sprinkled with sugar or ramune, while girls in hakama raised playful cries outside the Komachikurenai boutique. Glancing at them, Inako arrived at the address marked on the signboard—but found no likely shop in sight.

“This should be the area…”

As she wandered uncertainly back and forth, she accidentally bumped into a passing boy. He tumbled over, spilling a large quantity of canes he’d been carrying in a basket on his back across the ground.

“Sorry! I wasn’t paying attention!”

“No, no, don’t worry about it. Getting knocked over by such a beautiful lady is more than worth it.”

The boy, his kimono tucked up at the hem and wearing a happi coat labeled “Yagura Takeyukibuchi Craft Shop” at the collar, sprang to his feet with a flippant remark and grinned brightly, his face smeared with grime.

He looks like a smaller version of Kihachi, Inako thought. As she handed him the picked-up canes, the boy noticed the signboard and let out a soft “Oh?”

“Hey, big sis—could it be you’re looking for Hōrai Butsugu-ten?”

“Huh? Y-yes… I got lost…”

“In that case, I’ll show you the way. I was heading there myself anyway.”

Without waiting for her reply, the boy started walking off. Inako hesitated—unsure what else to do—but since she had no other leads, she decided to follow his small back.

“You’re acquainted with Mr. Sakamoto?”

“My name’s Yajiro Yamazaki. You here to see Big Bro Kihachi?”  

“Yeah. I need him to fix my *Ryōfūki*.”  

“Heh, he’ll be thrilled—loves mechanical repairs. See? Right there.”  

Hōrai Butsugu-ten was nestled inside a narrow alleyway. Passing through the entrance beneath a large signboard, the dim interior displayed an array of Buddhist goods: candles, incense, rosaries, and other ritual items neatly arranged on shelves.  

Yajiro called toward the back, “Excuse me—this is Yajiro.”  

“Big bro, I brought a customer! Uh…”  

He glanced at her briefly. “Her name’s Hundred Rivers Inako.”  

“*Kinako*-san?” Yajiro offered.  

“It’s *Inako*!” she snapped, correcting him sharply.  

From deep within the earthen-floored workshop, someone burst into loud laughter.  

“What’s this, Yajiro? Back from your sales run already?”  

Emerging still chuckling, Kihachi appeared unchanged—his smug grin as ever present.  

“I was out showing product samples to customers. Then on the way back, I ran into Kinako-san here.”  

After exchanging a few quick words with Kihachi, Yajiro cheerfully declared, “Well then, see you at the next Night Study Meeting!” and bounded off. Watching him go, Kihachi turned back to Inako, still unable to suppress his amusement.  

“Sorry for keeping you waiting, uh… *Kinako*-san,” he said, grinning.  

Without hesitation, she kicked him hard in the shin.  

“Ow! What the hell was that for?!” he protested.  

Baring her teeth in a sharp, furious grimace, Inako shot back,  
“How dare you give me such a ridiculous phone number! Thanks to you, I nearly ended up in serious trouble!”

---

2.

“Nonsense?” Kihachi tilted his head, puzzled—just as the telephone in the neighboring house began to ring shrilly.

Soon, someone answered, and the bell fell silent. After a brief pause,  
a white-haired old man suddenly burst into the shop, shouting, “Is Bunshichi there?!”

“Uh…?” came a drowsy voice from the back. A middle-aged man wearing glasses and a happi coat emerged, scratching at his unruly hair. Flakes drifted from his scalp—he was almost certainly Bunshichi.

“Ah, Mr. Kimono Shop, good day,” he said lazily.

“It’s not ‘good day’! That damn phone rang again—another call for *me* at *your* place!”

“Sorry ’bout that… So, uh, what’d the caller say?”

“I hung up right away! Now hurry up and erase that phone number from your signboard!”

As I watched the exchange, I turned to Kihachi. “What’s going on?”

“Well, see, writin’ a phone number on the sign makes customers trust you more. But we can’t afford the annual fee, so my uncle went and borrowed the number from Rokkaku Kimono Shop next door.”

Kihachi looked exasperated, while Bunshichi calmly placated the old man.

“Don’t worry, we pay our share for the trouble, don’t we?”

“Then pay me nuisance damages too! Calls meant for *me* keep coming nonstop!”

As the two bickered, Inako leaned close to Kihachi and whispered,  
“That kimono shop guy’s got guts, answering the phone like that. I’d be too scared.”

“What, you nervous about talking to an operator?”

---

2.

“If the other person’s got cholera, what then? I heard you can catch it through the phone.”

“No way that’s possible.”

Kihachi pressed a hand to his temple as if dealing with someone decades behind the times, when Bunshichi interjected, “Now, now, we’re in front of customers,” and gently led the still-fuming kimono shop owner toward the back.

“It’s fine. Just leave it be.”

“Yeah, don’t worry. That Mr. Kimono Shop’s actually a chatterbox—he uses wrong-number calls as an excuse to shoot the breeze.”

As Inako watched them disappear, wondering if this was truly harmless, she met Kihachi’s eyes—he stood with arms crossed, gaze unreadable.

He quickly looked away. “...Well… sorry about that. Should’ve explained properly when I gave you the signboard.”

“Forget it. I came all this way myself and now I know your shop really exists.”

“You believed me enough to come all this way?”

“Believing’s my specialty. Besides, I *had* to get this fixed.”

She pulled the Ryōfūki from its furoshiki wrapping. Kihachi leaned in sharply. “A Ryōfūki?”

After dropping it, it had stopped working. “Here—this is partial payment.”

“Oho. Biscuits. And high-quality ones at that.”

He helped himself to a couple from the snacks kept for guests. Taking the package from Inako, he immediately plucked one out and began nibbling cheaply at it with his front teeth.

“Might be fixable quick. Wait here a moment, would you?”

She nodded—then suddenly, a sharp bell rang out. Inako flinched, her shoulders jerking violently.

2.

Glancing at the wall clock, she saw it was already three o’clock. Kihachi placed the Ryōfūki on a workbench in the corner and immediately began disassembling it. The bench was cluttered with various types of mantel clocks, as well as unidentifiable machines and parts.

As Inako gazed at these objects—clearly distinct from the other Buddhist paraphernalia—Kihachi suddenly announced, “Done.”

“Eh? Already fixed?”

“Fixing mechanical things that don’t use electricity? That’s child’s play. The gear had just come loose, that’s all.”

Amazed by his astonishing speed, Inako watched as Kihachi wound the spring; instantly, the Ryōfūki’s *uchiwa* began to spin cheerfully, delivering the long-awaited lukewarm breeze.

“Wow, thank you so much,” Inako said, her hand resting over her heart in relief—but Kihachi stood with arms crossed, looking dissatisfied.

“Even if it’s working, this lukewarm wind… I can’t honestly say I’m happy with it.”

As Inako wrapped the Ryōfūki back into its furoshiki, the same sharp bell rang out again.

“Perfect timing. Look, the tea’s ready.”

Kihachi pointed at a machine on the workbench. Puzzled, Inako turned to look—and fell speechless. A kettle, propped up on the machine, was pouring boiling water all by itself into a teapot.

“You’re surprised, aren’t you? I built this based on an English automatic tea maker. It’s connected to the alarm clock I installed—when the time comes, the alcohol lamp underneath ignites automatically, and then, just like a karakuri puppet, it pours the boiled water into the teapot filled with tea leaves.”

2.

Back into its furoshiki, the same sharp bell rang out again. “Perfect timing. Look, the tea’s ready.” Kihachi pointed at a machine on the workbench. Puzzled, Inako turned to look—and fell speechless. A kettle, propped up on the machine, was pouring boiling water all by itself into a teapot.  

“You’re surprised, aren’t you? I built this based on an English automatic tea maker. It’s connected to the alarm clock I installed—when the time comes, the alcohol lamp underneath ignites automatically, and then, just like a *karakuri* puppet, it pours the boiled water into the teapot filled with tea leaves.”

Kihachi poured tea from the *kyūsu* into prepared teacups and handed one to Inako. The flavor wasn’t quite as refined as Noriko’s tea, but surprisingly pleasant. As a confection to accompany it, Kihachi added a single biscuit.

“This here’s a Buddhist goods shop, right?”

“That’s the main trade. But since I can handle simple mechanical repairs, I take repair orders, and if they cover material costs, I’ll build things to request.”

“Wait—so this tea-brewing alarm thing… is it for sale?”

When Kihachi nodded while chewing a biscuit, Inako made a grimace.

“Don’t make that face. Before delivery, I test everything myself.”

Still bewildered at how casually he operated, Inako let her eyes wander over the machines on the workbench.

“Got any other alarm clocks? If there’s one that makes it hard to oversleep, I might buy it.”

“Oh, plenty. I’ve even got some prototypes I tinkered with myself—they’ll wake you up all right.”

At the word *prototype*, Inako remembered and showed Kihachi the signboard she’d brought.

“This signboard—you wrote ‘*Denki Mokuroku*’ on the back. Was it important?”

The cheerful expression on Kihachi’s face darkened slightly.

“…Crap. Did I accidentally hand over the signboard I was using as a notebook?”

“If it’s instructions for making something, maybe I should give it back.”

“No need. It was just me idly designing a prophecy machine I thought up long ago.”

“Prophecy?”

“A book I once wrote called *Denki Mokuroku*—it lists twenty things electricity will achieve within the next hundred years.”

2.

“Wake you up all right.” At the word *prototype*, Inako remembered and showed Kihachi the signboard she’d brought. “This signboard—you wrote ‘*Denki Mokuroku*’ on the back. Was it important?”  

The cheerful expression on Kihachi’s face darkened slightly. “…Crap. Did I accidentally hand over the signboard I was using as a notebook?”  
“If it’s instructions for making something, maybe I should give it back.”  
“No need. It was just me idly designing a prophecy machine I thought up long ago.”  
“Prophecy?”  
“A book I once wrote called *Denki Mokuroku*—it lists twenty things electricity will achieve within the next hundred years.”  

Watching Kihachi blush shyly, a mischievous impulse stirred in Inako.  

“There’s other prophecies, right? Let me see that book for a sec.”  

Anyone would feel embarrassed having childhood scribbles shown to someone else.  

“…That’s ancient history. I lost it ages ago.”  

Inako sighed, disappointed. “I see.” She sipped the now-lukewarm dregs of her tea.  

“Guess you’ve run out of things to tease me with.”  
“N-no, I don’t think anything like that!”  

Startled by the direct hit, Kihachi flinched. As they idly chatted and munched on biscuits, a sudden burst of laughter erupted from the back of the shop. Just as Kihachi had said, the kimono merchant, his anger apparently cooled, was now deeply engaged in casual talk with Bunshichi. Inako glanced at the wall clock—already, far more time had passed than she’d realized.  

“It’s this late already. I’d better head home.”  

Kihachi offered to walk her to the nearby Keiden stop, so the two of them left the shop together. They strolled down the evening-tinged Shin-Kyōgoku, descended the gently sloping Tatarazaka, then turned right onto Sanjō-dōri, famed for its brick buildings and post office.  

Glancing briefly at the clock tower of Ibaye Tokuya Clock Shop, bathed in the setting sun, Inako asked Kihachi a question.  

“Mr. Sakamoto, are you planning to work in the electrical field someday?”  
“More electricity than machinery. I want to go to university, study, and then join an electric power company or a firm that makes electrical products. Ideally, one like ‘Japan’s Edison’—that’d be perfect.”  

2.

So the two of them left the shop together. They strolled down the evening-tinged Shin-Kyōgoku, descended the gently sloping Tatarazaka, then turned right onto Sanjō-dōri, famed for its brick buildings and post office. Glancing briefly at the clock tower of Ibaye Tokuya Clock Shop, bathed in the setting sun, Inako asked Kihachi a question.  
“Mr. Sakamoto, are you planning to work in the electrical field someday?”  
“More electricity than machinery. I want to go to university, study, and then join an electric power company or a firm that makes electrical products. Ideally, one like ‘Japan’s Edison’—that’d be perfect.”  

The man known as Japan’s Edison, who ran a physical and chemical instrument manufacturing business in Nijō, was apparently an exceptionally insightful individual. When he was the same age as Kihachi, it was said he had replicated a Western electrostatic generator just from looking at a single illustration.  
Afterward, he succeeded in domestically producing storage batteries; these were used in the navy’s new wireless devices and greatly contributed to the Combined Fleet’s victory over Russia’s Baltic Fleet in the Battle of Tsushima.  
“They say his workshop is now developing a medical X-ray apparatus.”  

While half-listening to Kihachi’s complicated explanations, Inako suddenly spotted ahead of them the circular stained-glass window of Saint Francis Xavier Cathedral. Instinctively, she clasped her hands toward the stark white church.  

Perhaps annoyed at having his story interrupted, Kihachi pouted his lips in displeasure.  
“You… you believe in Christianity too?”  
“No, I don’t. But Jesus-san is a great god, isn’t he? I heard from Mr. Roku that he healed the sick and even rose from the dead. It doesn’t hurt to bow and receive some blessing.”  
“Sounds like a pretty fishy story to me. How come civilized Westerners believe such tall tales?”  
“Sakamoto-san, you were born in a temple. How can you speak so disrespectfully about other people’s faith?”  
“That’s precisely why I can. I’ve seen up close monks chanting scriptures they don’t understand, charging money for dharma names and other things that bring no real benefit. Religion is just about scaring believers with talk of hell and divine retribution to make a profit.”  

As Kihachi looked up at the cathedral, his eyes took on that same resentful glare Inako had once seen before the sacred mound.

---

2.

“An age of blindly believing in gods and Buddha is over. Just as X-rays have made visible what the eye could not see, electricity will now expose one by one the true nature of miracles and curses.”

*Exposing God.* Those words sent an unpleasant throb deep within Inako’s chest.

“Instead of doing nothing gods, electricity will power machines and enrich people’s lives, enrich the world itself.”

Kihachi raised his eyes to the sky and declared loudly, his face brimming with confidence.

“The Twentieth Century is the century of electricity.”

“…There’s no way electricity can replace God.”

Taken aback—perhaps not expecting his words to be so directly challenged—Kihachi wore a stunned expression.

“God exists. That’s what Mother always said. She told me there are countless invisible gods in sake. If you cut corners, the gods spoil the brew; but if you honor them, dedicating yourself wholeheartedly to the craft, the gods within the sake lend their hands and make it delicious. No matter how powerful electricity may be—it can never replace God.”

“A load of nonsense.”

Inako’s argument was cut short by Kihachi’s cold retort.

“Now we can see even the tiniest things through electric microscopes. With tools like that, we’ll soon uncover the true identity of these so-called gods. The Twentieth Century is an era where intuition and tradition—the inefficient ways of craftsmen—are replaced. Even sake brewing will be done by electricity. We won’t need to pray to any gods—we’ll still be able to brew delicious sake.”

At that moment, the mocking face of Yōsuke, who had ridiculed the brewery work, flashed in Inako’s mind, and her irritation surged violently.

“Saying ‘sake made by the power of God’—that sounds suspicious, all right. Sounds just like a swindler’s sales pitch.”

2.

He sneered mockingly through his nose, as if deliberately provoking her.  

“Mrs. Locust—ain’t you just a swindler?”

Her head, boiling with irritation like bubbling water, had finally reached its limit.  

“It’s electricity that doesn’t matter whether it exists or not!”  

“…What’d you say?”  

“What do you mean, ‘the Century of Electricity’? Phonographs and clocks run on springs, and most shops don’t even have electric lights. How many people in this day and age actually depend on electricity to live? Lamps give plenty of light, trains are faster than electric trams, and you can boil water just fine with gas. Electricity makes no difference—it’s the same either way!”  

For once, Kihachi was at a loss for words. This time, it was Inako who scoffed at him through her nose.  

“How ridiculous you look, Sakamoto-san, so infatuated with electricity. You’re saying the exact same things as those monks you despise. Clinging desperately to the power of electricity—which doesn’t matter whether it exists or not.”  

Deep wrinkles gathered sharply between Kihachi’s brows, and his clenched fists began to tremble slightly.  

“That *Denki Mokuroku*—it’s just a prophecy book written by a con artist.”  

Ding, ding—the hollow, foolish chime of a bell rang out. They hadn’t noticed, but they’d already arrived at the Keiden tram stop.  

“Enough. Go home.”  

In a low voice, Kihachi spoke quietly. Without even glancing back at him, Inako leapt onto the moving train.  

Once seated, she slumped forward and leaned her forehead weakly against the *Ryōfūki*.  

  

Unpleasant things always come in succession. When she returned to Fushimi, she ended up having dinner with Yōsuke, who was still at home.

2.

At the table where the meal was laid out, Yōsuke reached for a mackerel sushi without particularly looking pleased.  
“Is it tasty?” asked Jinbei’emon, pouring him more sake.  
“It’s excellent,” Yōsuke replied—safe, noncommittal words.

“Mr. Yōsuke, do you have any favorite fish?”

At Jinbei’emon’s question, Yōsuke paused in thought, his face already flushed from drinking.

“Pacific saury.”

“I see. Indeed, autumn saury, rich with fat, are delicious.”

“No—I prefer saury caught just *before* their peak season. I once had sashimi of saury at that time. It was truly remarkable. Delicately sweet—and above all—”

His eyes snapped toward Inako, sharp and unblinking. She froze as if struck by paralysis.

“The firm, crisp texture of the flesh—the tautness you never get in fully matured saury—that is what I find utterly irresistible.”

A sensation like thousands of centipedes crawling all over her body surged through Inako. She quickly averted her gaze.

She simply couldn’t stand this man Dr. Paikaru. Ever since dinner began, time had felt terrifyingly slow, and a tight, queasy weight pressed on her chest.

Back at Hōrai Butsugu-ten, when they were drinking tea, time had flown by in an instant.

Remembering Sakamoto Kihachi’s scoffing smirk, Inako set down her chopsticks as if to shake off her melancholy, gave a slight bow to those around, and left the room. Wandering aimlessly through the house, she eventually found herself in the kitchen.

2.

Like thousands of centipedes crawling all over her body, a crawling sensation surged through Inako. She quickly averted her gaze. She simply couldn’t stand this man, Dr. Paikaru. Ever since dinner began, time had felt terrifyingly slow, and a tight, queasy weight pressed on her chest. Back at Hōrai Butsugu-ten, when they were drinking tea, time had flown by in an instant. Remembering Sakamoto Kihachi’s scoffing smirk, Inako set down her chopsticks as if to shake off her melancholy, gave a slight bow to those around, and left the room. Wandering aimlessly through the house, she eventually found herself in the kitchen.

The maids were all out, and the pot of water heating sake on the *okudo* was boiling. Seeing it, Inako rolled up her sleeves and gently dipped her index finger into the simmering water.

Just hot enough to trace the character *no* on the surface with her fingertip.

The temperature for pasteurizing sake—her mother had taught her this long ago. If it were any hotter, the flavor of the sake would be ruined; any cooler, bacteria would multiply and spoil the brew.

Finding it too lukewarm, Inako added a few more logs to stoke the fire.

“Does that sort of gathering make you uneasy?”

Yōsuke’s voice came softly from behind her shoulder, and Inako flinched. She turned and stepped back, only to find herself half-pressed against the *okudo* as Yōsuke, his neck flushed bright red from drinking, let out a hoarse hiccup.

“Was that a pasteurizing practice just now? Why on earth is Inako-chan doing something like that?”

“Sometimes, when I want to clear my head, I practice it. It calms me down.”

“You’re still the same baffling girl,” Yōsuke sneered with a mocking smile.

“But you know… from now on, you won’t need to do things like that anymore.”

Yōsuke suddenly leaned in. Inako, half-trapped with her back against the hearth, felt the heat at her back.

“What are you talking about?” she asked, her face stiff with tension.

Yōsuke’s mouth twisted into a leer, his breath reeking of alcohol, so close she could feel its warmth.

“You’re going to be my wife, after all.”

A dry, cracking sound split the air—the sound of firewood splitting.

She didn’t understand what he had just said. Had she misheard?

2.

Yōsuke sneered with a mocking smile. “But you know… from now on, you won’t need to do things like that anymore.” Yōsuke suddenly leaned in. Inako, half-trapped with her back against the hearth, felt the heat at her back. “What are you talking about?” she asked, her face stiff with tension. Yōsuke’s mouth twisted into a leer, his breath reeking of alcohol, so close she could feel its warmth. “You’re going to be my wife, after all.” A dry, cracking sound split the air—the sound of firewood splitting. She didn’t understand what he had just said. Had she misheard?

“Um… aren’t you too drunk? I’ll bring you some water.”

“This is serious. I’ve taken a liking to you. You *will* become the bride of the Mitsue family.”

Yōsuke’s hand settled on Inako’s shoulder, pressing down as if crushing the last flicker of hope still lingering within her.

All the blood drained from her body. Her feet no longer felt grounded—she was adrift in a sickening daze. Her head throbbed and swayed, a hollow ringing filling her ears. She tried to speak, but no words came out.

“If you become my wife, you won’t have to waste your time on foolish distractions anymore. Jewels, fine kimonos, steak, cake—you’ll live in such luxury, you won’t even have room to worry.”

*Paradise*, Inako’s lips moved silently—but she violently shook her head, rejecting it.

“T-That’s very kind of you, but I can’t possibly imagine someone like me becoming Dr. Paikaru’s wife…”

“But you must. Your father and I have already made an arrangement.”

Inako could no longer think straight. Her mind felt like a lotus root—hollowed out, full of holes.

“The Mitsue shop has done everything for the Hyakugawa family. We’ve even provided financial support from our bank when needed. But Mr. Hyakugawa refuses every request we make—says he doesn’t want to brew new sake. That’s simply unreasonable… Still, I’m not a monster. So I made the suggestion half in jest:”

Yōsuke raised his index finger sharply.

“If you, Miss Inako, agree to marry me, I’ll ensure he’s allowed to keep brewing exactly as he does now.”

“…So you threatened my father with that?”

2.

“No wonder a girl who’s never known hardship, always relying on others, turns out this way. Think about it—your slow-witted daughter gets a husband, and the brewery is saved too. For the Hyakugawa family, isn’t it nothing but gain?”

“So my feelings don’t matter at all?”

“Feelings?” Yōsuke snorted derisively, as if he found the very idea absurd. “Do you really think someone whose only value is becoming my wife has the right to be picky?”

A sharp pain pierced Inako’s heart, while Yōsuke’s smile twisted into something vulgar.

“Well, just suppose you refuse this marriage… then this morning’s little joke might just become reality.”

——They’d force the family to abandon sake brewing entirely and convert the brewery into a beer plant instead.

Blazing fury surged through her, scalding her insides, and Inako glared fiercely at Yōsuke.

“If you think we’re such worthless people, then why choose me in the first place?”

Suddenly, Yōsuke’s palm slid slowly, slickly over Inako’s right cheek—and she froze, every limb locking in shock.

“That timid little act of yours—it excites a man. No pretense, so honest… and above all—”

Just as she felt a revolting sensation like centipedes crawling across her skin and was about to scream, a maid burst into the earthen floor area, face drained of color. “Miss! Get away from him!”

For one fleeting moment, Inako rejoiced—*help has come*—but in the next instant, the maid shoved her aside and shrieked, “Fire!”

Inako stumbled and fell. When she scrambled back up, she saw blazing fire erupting where a log had slipped and landed just before the *okudo*.

“What’s all this ruckus?!”

Rushing in, Jinbei’emon took in the scene at a glance and barked at the maid, “Use the water from the *mizubīru*!”

2.

Seeing Inako still rolling on the earthen floor, Jinbei’emon’s eyes widened in shock.

He stepped down into the dirt-floored area—and to his astonishment, saw her lunging toward him, clutching a kitchen knife taken from the sink.

—I’m going to die. The moment the thought struck, her legs gave out. She dropped onto her backside and scrambled backward across the floor. But Jinbei’emon closed the distance effortlessly and seized Inako’s braid—the one tied in a single knot at the back—with brutal force.

“Please, forgive me! I beg you!”

The blade he raised glinted dully.

As she squeezed her eyes shut, it was the hair that screamed.

Somehow, her head felt strangely light. Trembling, she opened her eyes—only to see Jinbei’emon standing over her, holding a severed lock of hair dangling like the limp neck of a dead bird.

Her hand flew unconsciously behind her back—but grasped only air. Her hair was gone.

The braid, which had nearly reached the same length as her sister’s, had been cut short at the nape of her neck.

“Why… why…”

Tears blurring her vision, she looked up at him. Jinbei’emon stared back with cold, unyielding eyes.

“…Now maybe you’ll think twice. Every time, without fail—you go doing senseless things.”

“But I—I only wanted to practice fire-starting, just to pass the time, using an empty pot…”

“I haven’t the faintest idea what you’re talking about…”

Jinbei’emon pressed a hand to his forehead and twisted his mouth in utter dejection.

“For heaven’s sake… can’t you please start acting normal, just once?”

2.

That was precisely what Inako had always wished for. Jinbei’emon’s anguished expression pierced her heart far more deeply than any shouting ever could. Behind him, Yōsuke was covering his mouth, quietly laughing.

“There’s no need to worry—hair will grow back, you know. It’ll be just fine.”

“I’m sorry you had to see such an unsightly sight. If I’ve upset you in any way, I sincerely apologize.”

“My feelings haven’t changed at all, so please rest assured. Then, I’ll head back ahead of you.”

Inako stared blankly at Yōsuke’s retreating figure as he cheerfully walked away, then weakly opened her lips.

“Is it true… that Yōsuke-san wants to marry me?”

“He seems to have taken a liking to you since the moment you met. He wants to officially register the marriage right after his appointment as branch manager.”

“So soon…?”

“There’s going to be a celebration next week for Yōsuke’s promotion to branch manager. At the last minute, they’ve decided to combine it with your wedding ceremony. Since there’s no time to prepare proper bridal furnishings, it’ll be a simple affair—just registering the household registration for now.”

Jinbei’emon looked down at Inako, still sitting numbly on the earthen floor, with a puzzled expression.

“What’s with that face? You’re not actually dissatisfied with getting married, are you?”

“Am I… just a tool for business?”

“…What did you say?”

“They’re rushing through my most important day like it’s nothing but a formality?”

“Shut up!” Jinbei’emon roared.

“You don’t improve at housework or your training, you bring shame upon this family’s name, and yet you dare talk back to your father?”

Tears spilled from Inako’s eyes. “No, that’s not it—that’s not it at all…”

2.

“The fire just now—was it because of Yōsuke-san—”

“Now you’re blaming others? Dull-witted people are always quick with excuses.”

Out of the corner of her eye, she saw the maid who had witnessed the scene glaring at Inako with undisguised contempt.

“Acupuncture! Ito! Get in here!”

Yanked roughly by Jinbei’emon, Inako was dragged down the corridor and shoved into the dark storehouse at the edge of the garden. Dust swirled into the air, and she coughed involuntarily.

“You are nothing but a *Ryōfūki*,” Jinbei’emon declared solemnly.

“A useless thing, only trouble to everyone.”

The heavy door slammed shut behind her, and darkness swallowed the storehouse whole.

Jinbei’emon probably assumed, like most parents would, that the storehouse frightened Inako. But to her, this place felt as safe as being wrapped in a futon. As long as she remained inside, she wouldn’t be scolded further, wouldn’t burden anyone, and wouldn’t suffer shame.

Useless—only trouble. A fact she understood all too painfully.

Why did she keep failing? Why could no one ever understand her?

And anyway—if there truly is a Paradise after death, then why must she remain trapped in this hellish world? Her mind spun with memories: every past blunder, Jinbei’emon’s shouts, the maid’s look of utter disappointment, Yōsuke’s unsettling smile—until, finally, thinking about it all became too tiresome.

She was simply tired of trying.

“I want to die.”

That feeble whisper was undeniably Inako’s truest heart.

2.

She was simply tired of trying. “I want to die.” That feeble whisper was undeniably Inako’s truest heart.

＊

Kihachi, carrying his luggage, arrived in front of Hyakugawa Shuzō under the searing midday sun.

A dark brown wooden fence stretched imposingly across the property, and just before it, a boy who appeared to be an apprentice had been lingering restlessly. Watching the scene from the corner of his eye, Kihachi passed through the gate and stood before the entrance.

“Excuse me, I’m from Hōrai Butsugu-ten.”

A voice replied, “Yes,” from within, and soon the door opened to reveal someone who made Kihachi blink in surprise.

At first glance, she resembled Inako—but only momentarily. He quickly realized this was another person entirely. Taller than Inako, fuller in figure, with slightly narrower eyes. Could this be Noriko, the older sister Inako had mentioned?

The woman—Noriko—flinched slightly, her eyes flickering with unease at the sight of the unfamiliar man.

“Um… I’ve brought something for Miss Inako and came to deliver it.”

“…And your name is?”

Her voice was thin, delicate—a timbre he wasn’t used to—and Kihachi’s own voice trembled in response: “S-Sakamoto Kihachi.”

Noriko studied him intently, as if assessing his worth, then swung the half-open door fully wide.

“Please come inside.”

As he followed her down the corridor, an indescribable sweet fragrance drifted from Noriko ahead of him, making Kihachi shiver involuntarily. Her long hair swayed gently, along with the red ribbon tied in it, innocent and carefree.

Soon they reached a guest room, where they sat across a table from each other. Noriko offered a faint smile.

“My name is Hyakugawa Noriko. My younger sister Inako has spoken often of being indebted to you.”

2.

“It’s not like I did much,” Kihachi replied modestly, yet his eyes were irresistibly drawn to the biscuit plate perched at the edge of the table. Noriko let out a small snort of laughter. “Go on, eat,” she said, pushing the plate toward him.

“Much obliged,” he said, unhesitatingly biting into a biscuit.

“So you came here to see Inako today?”

Kihachi choked slightly on the biscuit and hastily shook his head, then began placing a selection of clocks from his bag onto the table.

“Inago—Miss Inako mentioned wanting a wake-up clock, so I just brought some samples.”

“Hmm,” Noriko murmured, narrowing her eyes as she poked one of the clocks with her finger.

“And where is Inako now?”

“She’s in the storehouse. The girl got into trouble again last night, so she’s been locked inside. Sorry you came all this way for nothing. I’ll keep the clocks for you for now.”

“No, that’s not—” Kihachi stammered, faltering. Noriko covered her mouth, laughing.

“Thought so. You really came here because you wanted to say something to Inako, didn’t you?”

Her smile said it all—she saw right through him.

“Did Inako tell you about the fight yesterday?”

“Fight? No, I hadn’t heard. Ah, so you came to make up.”

He instinctively started to deny it, then stopped himself—afraid of being teased again—and simply fell silent.

“But it looks like you won’t be able to see her. That girl… I think she’s going to be locked in the storehouse until her wedding.”

“…Wedding?”

It was news to him, completely out of the blue. Kihachi stared at Noriko as she told him about Dr. Paikaru—and about Inako’s impending marriage.

2.

“Didn’t you?” Her smile said it all—she saw right through him. “Did Inako tell you about the fight yesterday?”  
“Fight? No, I hadn’t heard. Ah, so you came to make up.” He instinctively started to deny it, then stopped himself—afraid of being teased again—and simply fell silent. “But it looks like you won’t be able to see her. That girl… I think she’s going to be locked in the storehouse until her wedding.”  
“…Wedding?” It was news to him, completely out of the blue. Kihachi stared at Noriko as she told him about Dr. Paikaru—and about Inako’s impending marriage.

As they spoke, a faint itch crept over his body, yet strangely, he felt none of summer’s usual heat. In the corner of the room, the recently repaired *Ryōfūki* hummed softly. Had it always been this cool?

When the conversation ended, Kihachi’s voice emerged hoarse from a parched throat.  
“So the apprentice boy out front—is he keeping watch to make sure Inako doesn’t escape?”  
“That’s right. Look, you can see one in the garden too.”  

Peering through the slightly opened *shōji*, he spotted a maid idly patrolling in front of the storeroom, its heavy bolt firmly in place.  

“What should I do? Should I come again?”  

Kihachi fell silent for a moment. Then, tidying the alarm clock on the desk, he stood up smoothly.  
“I see. It seems complicated. In that case, I’ll take my leave now.”

　　　　　　　　　　＊

Whenever Inako thought back to her mother, Naeko, the memory always came with the scent of sake—intertwined with Naeko’s smile.

The image most deeply etched in her mind was of her mother with a hand towel wrapped around her topknot, wearing a simple cotton *kasuri* kimono and a *tasuki* sash, giving orders to the *kurabito*.  

Naeko had been something rare—a female *tōji*. In nearly any other brewery, such a thing would have been unthinkable.  

A *tōji* was the master brewer, the head of the entire sake operation. More than just possessing deep knowledge and refined instincts about brewing, the role included mediating among the *kurabito*, organizing labor, and overseeing every aspect of production. When Naeko’s father—the previous *tōji*, and Inako’s grandfather—retired, he recognized his daughter’s extraordinary talent and made the unprecedented decision to appoint her as successor, elevating her to the position of *tōji* despite her gender.

2.

For young Inako, who was at the age of craving affection, the brewery where Naeko worked was her favorite playground.

The *kurabito* doted on her too, letting her help carry loads during *yamadori* while singing *motosuri* songs together, and allowing her to observe each stage of sake production—teaching her the basics of brewing alongside Naeko.

“Inako,” Naeko would say, as if it were a mantra, “there are countless invisible gods dwelling inside the sake.”

She believed that when these deities took residence in the fermenting brew, fine sake would result. But if they didn’t, the batch would spoil—a condition known as *fuzō*. Even if benevolent gods first entered, if malevolent ones slipped in later, the sake would fail anyway, an affliction called *hidachi*. These evil spirits seemed vulnerable to heat, which made the pasteurization process crucial.

No one truly understood why *fuzō* or *hidachi* occurred. All humans could do was pray to the gods, beseeching them not to let harmful spirits near.

And so, every day, Inako joined Naeko in clasping her hands before the *kamidana*, praying for the safe making of the sake.

Then one year, a sudden infestation of rats broke out in the brewery.

While the family and workers panicked, Inako alone remained calm—catching rat after rat with ease. Watching her, Naeko chuckled merrily: “Well, looks like Inako’s quite the expert at rat-catching, isn’t she?”

2.

However, due to this very incident later known as the “Hyakugawa Rice Riots,” nearly all of the raw rice had to be discarded, resulting in massive losses. To make up for it, Naeko threw herself into sake brewing with even greater intensity during the winter of the following year—Meiji 33 (1900)—driving her body to the limit.

Yet no matter how hard Naeko pushed herself, there was one process she simply could not interfere with: *seigiku*, the making of koji.

This was the step where steamed rice was carried into a special room called the *kōjimuro*—a room strictly forbidden to women.

Why women were prohibited, she didn’t fully understand, but it was a common practice at virtually every other brewery as well.

“In the *kōjimuro*, the sake deity takes residence within the rice,” Naeko explained. “But the sake deity dislikes beautiful women, so if a beauty is present, He gets shy and refuses to inhabit the rice.”

Though that was the reason Naeko gave for the ban, Inako harbored a growing desire deep inside.

She wanted—just once—to see the invisible god with her own eyes.

“I’m not beautiful like Onee-san, so it should be fine,” Inako reasoned, searching for a loophole. And so, one early dawn, seizing a moment when no one else was around, she quietly slipped into the *kōjimuro*.

But the tightly sealed room was pitch dark, and she could make out nothing clearly; no sign of any godly presence appeared before her. Within less than five minutes, fear overcame her, and she fled the *kōjimuro* as if running for her life.

It had been wrong to enter—but surely, since she’d only stayed a brief moment, the god would overlook her transgression.

Carrying a tangled mix of relief and unease, the year turned, and the Twentieth Century arrived.

The subsequent brewing and pressing proceeded without incident, and just as Inako began to let her guard down—

2.

“There’s no use—this one’s spoiled.”

Tanesako, inspecting the stored sake, quietly declared.

Seeing the murmuring ripple through the *kurabito*, Inako trembled with dread.

—It’s because I entered the *kōjimuro*. I angered the god.

And misfortune continued. Soon after the sake had spoiled, Tanesako fell ill with pneumonia.

Sake brewing was grueling labor from dawn to dusk. As *tōji*, Tanesako bore a heavy burden. On top of that, her mother-in-law disapproved of Tanesako’s involvement in brewing, harshly scolding her: “For a bride, you do nothing about the household!” Tanesako had long since reached her physical and emotional limits.

“You don’t have to come visit me like this every single day.”

When Inako came to her bedside, Tanesako spoke between coughs. Her cheeks, thinned from lost weight, were deeply wrinkled; her once-lustrous hair, tied simply at the back, hung painfully over the futon.

“I’m praying properly to the gods, so it’ll be fine. Mother will surely get better.”

Tanesako smiled, then said, “Ah, let me show you something good,” and handed Inako a postcard. In the photograph, the sea stretched toward the foreground, with Mount Fuji, capped in snow, rising in the distance.

“You’ve always wanted to see Mount Fuji, haven’t you? This photo was taken from Satta Pass in Shizuoka.”

Seeing Mount Fuji in a photograph—for the first time, not in a painted scroll—stirred Inako’s heart against her will.

“How tall is Mount Fuji, exactly?”

“Much, much taller than Mount Hiei or Mount Atago.”

“Is Mount Fuji really this gray-colored mountain?”

2.

“Mother will surely get better.” Tanesako smiled, then said, “Ah, let me show you something good,” and handed Inako a postcard. In the photograph, the sea stretched toward the foreground, with Mount Fuji, capped in snow, rising in the distance. “You’ve always wanted to see Mount Fuji, haven’t you? This photo was taken from Satta Pass in Shizuoka.” Seeing Mount Fuji in a photograph—for the first time, not in a painted scroll—stirred Inako’s heart against her will. “How tall is Mount Fuji, exactly?”  
“Much, much taller than Mount Hiei or Mount Atago.”  
“Is Mount Fuji really this gray-colored mountain?”  

“The grayness is because photographs have no color. In truth, it’s a beautiful blue mountain just like in *nishiki-e* prints. …Back when we’d just gotten married, I went to Tokyo on business with Jinbei’emon-san, and we stopped by this very spot while we were there. The scenery was so breathtakingly beautiful that I bought this postcard as a souvenir.”

It was an unexpected revelation. At home, there had rarely been anything resembling conversation between the couple; all she could recall was her mother standing quietly beside the taciturn Jinbei’emon, smiling gently.

“Do you still not get along with your father, Inako?”

“…He’s scary when he gets angry, and besides—he’s cold even to you, Mother.”

“He’s the kind of man who seldom speaks his thoughts aloud. Even when he’s strict with you, Inako, it’s his own way of caring for you. So please forgive him.”

Her mother’s hand gently stroked Inako’s head. It was thin, clearly weakened by illness, but still warm.

“Mother’s illness will get better soon, right?”

“Of course it will. You’ve been praying to the gods for me, haven’t you?”

Inako pressed her tear-filled eyes against her mother’s chest.

“That trip with Jinbei’emon-san was such a joy. Is there anywhere you’d like to go, Inako?”

At Tanesako’s words, Inako sprang up. “O-Ise-san!”

“I’ll pray to the highest of gods so Mother can get well!”

Then, in a quieter tone, she added, “And… I also need to apologize to the gods for entering the *kōjimuro*.”


2.

Inako decided to confess everything to Moneko, who knew nothing of the situation. She expected to be scolded—but to her surprise, after hearing the whole story, Moneko giggled.

“Maybe the gods were flustered because Inako is so beautiful.”

“But I’m not as pretty as Onee-chan.”

Moneko pinched both of Inako’s cheeks with her hands.  
“It’s your smile. When you smile, no one’s more beautiful than you.”

“Won’t Okaa-san be angry? The sake turned bad because of me…”

“Angry? Why would I be? My proud daughter was called beautiful by the gods themselves.”

Inako didn’t know whether it was the pain in her chest at feeling how weak Moneko’s grip had become, or the warmth of those gentle words—but tears welled up in her eyes. Moneko gently wiped them away with her fingertips.

“There’s… somewhere I want to go even more than Ise-san,” Inako said, her voice trembling.

“I want to see Mount Fuji. I want to see if it’s really a mountain as grand and beautiful as in the pictures.”

“But it’s far away,” Inako added with a resigned smile. Slowly, Moneko shook her head.

“You know, from here in Kansai, you can actually see Mount Fuji.”

“What? See Mount Fuji?”

“Yes. Not too far by train—there’s a place where you can see the real Mount Fuji.”

“Really?” Inako’s face lit up instantly, leaning forward eagerly.

“Then when Okaa-san gets well, you’ll take me there, right?”

“All right. I promise.”

2.

“Pinkie swear?” Moneko raised her little finger. Inako started to extend hers too, when suddenly a maid peeked in from outside. “Excuse me—the doctor has arrived.”

“There now, Inako,” Moneko said gently. “That’s enough for today. Go back to your room.”

Inako was ushered out of the room and, feeling small and uneasy, turned back to look at Moneko.

“You know, Inako… you seem to think you’ve got nothing special about you. But your smiling face—really, it’s truly adorable. It’s like the sun itself, brightening people’s hearts.”

“Okaa-san?”

“I’m sure your smile will bring happiness to countless people.”

With that, her mother smiled—and then her face disappeared behind the sliding door as it closed shut.

The next day, Moneko’s condition took a sudden turn, and she passed away peacefully, as if falling into sleep.

At the funeral, Jinbei’emon and Noriko wore stiff, solemn expressions. Condolences, the monks’ sutras—none of it felt real. As soon as the ceremony ended, Inako found herself standing inside the brewery without knowing how she’d gotten there.

“It’s all my fault.”

She had entered the kōjimuro—the sacred fermentation chamber—where women were forbidden. She had angered the gods. The spoiled sake, her mother’s death—everything, every single thing—was punishment from the gods, divine retribution brought on by her.

The gods truly exist. They are always watching. She must never anger them again. She must remain grateful, and continue praying, so that just a fraction of their grace might be bestowed upon her.

Inako burst into sobs. The brewery, usually so full of life, was eerily quiet, and her weeping echoed sharply through the space.

Somehow, Inako had fallen asleep. Now, she woke to the oppressive heat of the earthen storehouse.

2.

Weakly lifting her head, Inako looked around with bleary eyes. Amid shelves, chests, and piles of junk, she saw a fist-sized shadow darting swiftly beside a large jar.

Fully awakened now, Inako pulled out a handkerchief and crept forward cautiously. She stood still, patiently staring at the shadowed area behind the jar. Soon, a rat darted out—and she snatched it up with her hand through the cloth.

“Five sen earned.”

Plague had long raged across Japan as a dreaded epidemic. Just last year, when cases surfaced in Fushimi Town and caused an uproar, a government program was launched to buy one rat for five sen, since rats were known carriers of the disease.

Inako, having been repeatedly locked inside storehouses, had grown accustomed to rats and knew exactly how to catch them. Last year, hoping to help her family’s finances, she had captured them in large numbers—but suspicion arose that she might be breeding them, so the authorities refused to buy, turning her into the neighborhood’s laughingstock. In the end, she was even beaten with a cane by Jinbei’emon.

As she wondered whether the rat-buying program was still running, her stomach let out a loud growl.

“Ugh… I’m so hungry…”

The moment she muttered this, a small, round light appeared on the floor, and Inako leapt up, still clutching the rat.

The circular light pulsed and writhed across the floor. When Inako timidly tapped it with her free hand, the light raced up her arm, surged through her body, and her vision flared with blinding brilliance.

Blinking against the glare, she saw Sakamoto Kihachi peering in through the latticed window, holding a glowing tube, utterly stunned.

“You… You weren’t planning to *eat* that, were you?”

Inako, flustered, hastily dropped the rat at the sight of Kihachi’s twisted, horrified expression.

---

2.

The moment she muttered this, a small, round light appeared on the floor, and Inako leapt up, still clutching the rat. The circular light pulsed and writhed across the floor. When Inako timidly tapped it with her free hand, the light raced up her arm, surged through her body, and her vision flared with blinding brilliance. Blinking against the glare, she saw Sakamoto Kihachi peering in through the latticed window, holding a glowing tube, utterly stunned. “You… You weren’t planning to *eat* that, were you?” Inako, flustered, hastily dropped the rat at the sight of Kihachi’s twisted, horrified expression.

“No way! I’d never eat a rat!”

She approached the lattice window, indignant, then pointed at the tube. “What *is* that thing you’ve been shining all along?”

“This is a flashlight. In Japanese, it’s called a *denchūdentō*.”

Kihachi fiddled with the knob, and the bulb at the end of the tube flickered.

“This time, it’s not spring-powered. It’s real, genuine electricity.”

At those words, Inako remembered yesterday’s argument and grew awkward, turning her eyes away from Kihachi.

Kihachi, perhaps thinking the same thing, stiffened. “Well… uh, that…”

“You were worried about that alarm clock yesterday, right? So today, I brought a sample. Then Sister Inago told me where you were, so I came over the back wall.”

Only then, as he finished speaking, did Kihachi finally notice. “Wait—you… your hair…” He gasped.

Inako gave a weak smile and ran her fingers through the shortened ends.

“Pretty bad, huh? I thought I could finally catch up to my sister now, but instead… I’ve fallen even further behind.”

“But you’re getting married soon, right? Why cut such precious hair?”

“Oh, so you heard about the wedding too,” said Inako, and when she began explaining yesterday’s misstep, Kihachi asked seriously, “So… does that mean Inago is going to marry that Dr. Paikaru?”

“I’m someone whose only strength is believing in others, so I have no right to be selfish. If marrying him would help our family… then I will…”

Her lips trembled as she tried to continue. She knew full well she wasn’t in a position to choose. But.

2.

“I’ve been trying, in my own way, to grow up fast—swallowing down things I wanted to say, always reflecting, carrying every grudge and complaint straight to God. …But no matter what, nothing ever works out.”

Household duties, practice—all half-done. In the end, she’d only caused endless trouble, disappointing everyone around her. Maybe, just by breathing, she was dragging the world down.

“…Really, what’s the point of me even being born?”

Suddenly, Kihachi opened the cap of his flashlight and pulled out a cylindrical object from inside.

“This is a dry cell battery. Invented twenty years ago by a man named Yai—the first in the world. But apparently, it didn’t sell at all at first. Back then, there just weren’t enough machines in the world that could use dry batteries.”

Puzzled, Inako listened to Kihachi, unsure where this was going.

“Back then, telephones used liquid batteries. But during the Sino-Japanese War, those liquid batteries froze in Manchuria’s cold. So they started using dry batteries instead. That story made the newspapers, and for the first time, people realized how powerful dry batteries could be.”

Kihachi paused. “It’s the same with you,” he said, then continued.

“There’s definitely a reason you were born, Inago. It’s just that neither the world nor you yourself have realized it yet.”

Inako lowered her head and bit her lip. If she didn’t, everything inside her might collapse.

Kihachi’s words were kind. But that very direct kindness struck her heart mercilessly.

“So, Inago—”

“What do *you* know about it, Sakamoto-san!”  

2.

For a moment, the storehouse fell silent. Outside, as if remembering something, the cicadas began their shrill song once more.

“My head’s not right—I’m sure of it. I’ve got some unnamed illness eating away at my mind. But since no one else can see it, I have to live just like everybody else. I’d actually be relieved if a doctor would just tell me outright I’ve got a sickness in my head… Then I could accept it. Then I’d have an excuse.”

She had tried to endure—but failed. Large drops fell from Inako’s eyes, sliding down one by one.

“Believing is my only strength. Yet I can’t even believe in myself.”

“Then you’ll stay here forever? If you shut yourself in this dark place, you’ll never find any meaning in living.”

“Then where *should* I be—?”

And as she asked herself that, she suddenly realized—there was one place, and only one, truly suited for her.

How had she never noticed before? It was so simple.

“Well then,”

she said, lifting her swollen face, managing only a faint smile with her lips.

“Will you die with me, and take me to Paradise?”

Kihachi’s eyes widened. He understood—she was dead serious.

“There, at least, I won’t trouble anyone. I think we could be happy.”

“Paradise…”

Kihachi turned his face away. Without another word, he simply walked off. Once his figure vanished from sight, Inako slumped weakly against the wall.

He must think I’m mad, saying such things, Kihachi probably thought, stunned.

And that was fine. Her thoughts were never meant to be understood by anyone.

2.

Leaning dazedly against the wall, listening to the distant clamor from outside and the chirping of cicadas, Inako suddenly heard a deafening toll of a temple bell that drowned out all other sounds.

“What—fire?”

The urgent clanging carried the panic of her household—the sound of people scrambling in confusion. Just as Inako began to push herself up, the storehouse door burst open, and Kihachi stepped inside.

“Well then, let’s go.”

Backlit by the outdoor light, Kihachi reached out his hand to her.

“I’m takin’ you to Paradise right now.”

“…To where?”

“To Paradise. Ain’t that what you wanted to see?”

Inako gaped, unable to comprehend what he was saying.

“What on earth are you talking—”

“Hurry. Let’s go before they notice.”

Grabbing her arm, he pulled her along without waiting for an answer, dragging her out of the storehouse.

Once outside, they ducked beside the building to survey the surroundings. The bell still rang without pause, and the maid who had been watching the engawa was now running through the house with a look of terror.

“W-where’s the fire even happening?”

“Ah, that bell? That’s my alarm clock I just set up under the floorboards.”

“An alarm clock? Isn’t it way too loud? You’ll bother the neighbors!”

“Well, there *is* ‘Track Eleven: Thunder Summoner’ in the *Denki Mokuroku*—that one jolts you awake with pure current—”

“Scary! Don’t say any more!”

2.

An answer, dragging her out of the storehouse. Once outside, they ducked beside the building to survey the surroundings. The bell still rang without pause, and the maid who had been watching the engawa was now running through the house with a look of terror. “W-where’s the fire even happening?”  
“Ah, that bell? That’s my alarm clock I just set up under the floorboards.”  
“An alarm clock? Isn’t it way too loud? You’ll bother the neighbors!”  
“Well, there *is* ‘Track Eleven: Thunder Summoner’ in the *Denki Mokuroku*—that one jolts you awake with pure current—”  
“Scary! Don’t say any more!”

They slipped out through the back door, careful not to be seen by anyone in the house, then peeked onto the main street. Just then, firefighters arrived in a rush, hastily assembling a hand-pump engine in front of the house. Inako began feeling dizzy at the chaos unfolding around her, but Kihachi grabbed her again, trying to pull her away.

“Inako-kun!”  

A sharp voice rang out as Roku emerged from the crowd of onlookers.

Today he wasn’t wearing his Salvation Army uniform; instead, he wore Western clothes—a black shirt and trousers. Roku’s eyes locked onto Kihachi gripping Inako’s arm. Something snapped in his expression—he instantly flared with anger.

“You! What do you think you’re doing?!”

“Damn it—run!”

They bolted, but Roku charged after them like a locomotive, rapidly closing the gap.

Gasping for breath, they strained their legs to run faster, but the wooden clogs hampered their pace.

Just as Roku closed in behind them, reaching out toward Kihachi—

*Ding, ding.* A bell chimed.

From the side, a tram surged forward, blotting Roku from view.

Unaware, the two had already crossed the tracks of the Kyoto Electric Railway. Like scooping goldfish, Roku was caught mid-stride by the tram’s emergency net. The net sagged under his weight—unable to hold him—and his backside scraped across the ground through the mesh as he was dragged along. “It burns! It burns!” he cried out, carried off by the moving vehicle.

Inako and Kihachi watched, hearts pounding, until the tram finally came to a stop. Slowly, Roku crawled out from beneath the net.

2.

*Ding, ding.* A bell chimed. From the side, a tram surged forward, blotting Roku from view. Unaware, the two had already crossed the tracks of the Kyoto Electric Railway. Like scooping goldfish, Roku was caught mid-stride by the tram’s emergency net. The net sagged under his weight—unable to hold him—and his backside scraped across the ground through the mesh as he was dragged along. “It burns! It burns!” he cried out, carried off by the moving vehicle. Inako and Kihachi watched, hearts pounding, until the tram finally came to a stop. Slowly, Roku crawled out from beneath the net.

As passersby on the street halted in confusion, the two scrambled into a nearby rickshaw. Kihachi urged the puller: “Just get us away from here. You’d better move faster than that tram!”

Perhaps stung by the insult to his professional pride, the rickshaw man grunted, “Understood,” then set off with fierce determination.

“Roku-kun, wait up!”

But the rickshaw sped on violently, and Roku’s pained figure—clutching his rear—vanished swiftly into the distance.

　　　　　　　　　　Three

“Wow, you’ve really torn it up nicely again.”

Laying Roku’s trousers flat on the tatami mat in the guest room, Noriko struggled desperately to suppress her laughter.

“If you’re going to laugh, just laugh already,” Roku snapped, now changed into a kimono, his cheeks twitching in irritation.

“Laugh? *Fufu*. I was just thinking—you’re still the same, recklessly charging ahead like a fool.”

“Gah! Don’t laugh!”

“But you just told me to! Anyway, you’ve been standing this whole time—why don’t you sit?”

“How can I?! My backside feels like it’s been rubbed raw with sandpaper!”

Though there was no bleeding, he could clearly feel the thin skin on his buttocks peeling away in strips; sitting on the floor was utterly unthinkable.

“And with all this commotion, I can’t even settle my poor rear in peace.”

After being run over by the tram, when they arrived at Hyakugawa Shuzō, firefighters were just packing up their hand pump and leaving. At the entrance, Jinbei’emon stood in a towering rage, berating maids and apprentices alike, his fury making his hair seem ready to burst from his scalp.

2.

“My backside feels like it’s been rubbed raw with sandpaper!” Though there was no bleeding, he could clearly feel the thin skin on his buttocks peeling away in strips; sitting on the floor was utterly unthinkable. “And with all this commotion, I can’t even settle my poor rear in peace.” After being run over by the tram, when they arrived at Hyakugawa Shuzō, firefighters were just packing up their hand pump and leaving. At the entrance, Jinbei’emon stood in a towering rage, berating maids and apprentices alike, his fury making his hair seem ready to burst from his scalp.

“Jinbei’emon-san was told, but someone saw a boy taking Inako-chan away. Do you know anything about it?”

“Well… maybe they eloped.”

“Eloped? To me, it looked more like a fire-scene thief making off with the loot!”

“A thief, sure—but more like a Robin Hood of thieves. You’re just too rigid-minded, Roku-san.”

“And why must you always put things so pointedly… Never mind. Aren’t you worried about your sister?”

“I’m so worried I can’t even swallow my rice,” said Noriko, crunching on a biscuit as she spoke. When she finished eating, she brushed the crumbs from her fingers, opened the sewing box beside her, and began threading a needle.

“You’re… going to mend it?”

“Of course. Even if it’s only provisional, I’m your fiancée, aren’t I?”

Just as Roku opened his mouth to reply, Noriko burst into laughter.

“Still, those holes in your pants—walking around with your butt completely hanging out!”

“Don’t say it!”

Roku turned bright red, steaming like a pressure cooker letting out *shuposhupo* bursts of vapor.

　　　　　　　　　　＊

“This… is Paradise?”

Inako narrowed her eyes into slits, gazing up at the Hōrai Butsugu-ten signboard glowing in the evening sun. Following reluctantly behind Kihachi, she stepped into the shop, where Bunshichi sat on a stool smoking a cigarette and reading the newspaper.

“Oh, Inako-chan, welcome—wait, what’s with your hair?”

Bunshichi widened his eyes behind his glasses. Inako patted her head and replied, “It’s… been quite a day.”

2.

The shop was unusually lively, and in one corner, four children were chatting and laughing. Among them was Yajiro, the boy who had shown her around the store the other day, grinning broadly with a dirty face. "Ah, Kinako-nee, welcome back!"

"What's with that weird hairstyle?" asked a girl with bangs parted in the middle and long hair hanging down.

"Miyaji-chan, that's rude!" The girl smacked Yajiro lightly on the head.

"What'd I do?"

"You're such an idiot, Miyaji-chan. Think before you speak."

At the bold girl’s remark, a younger-looking boy in striped clothes and a bespectacled girl with her hair tied back in a simple knot both nodded vigorously in agreement.

"Ito, Mitsu. Sorry, but could you two fix Inago’s hair out front?"

After exchanging a few words with Kihachi, the two girls grabbed Inako by the sleeve.

"Then let’s go. Uh… Kinako-san? Inago-san?"

"It’s *Inako*—*Hundred Rivers Inako*!"

As Inako was pulled outside, Kihachi said, "Yajiro and Sadakichi, come help me with some prep work," and led the remaining boys toward the back.

Inako sat on a bench, newspaper draped over her shoulders like a barber’s cape, while Ito snipped at her hair with scissors.

"Hmm… so you ran away with Kihachi-niisan, huh?"

Inako tried to protest, but Ito pressed her hand on her head. "Stay still."

"That’s not it. He said, ‘I’ll take you to Paradise,’ and I ended up here."

"Eh?! You mean… we’re dying?" Ito dropped her scissors.

---

2.

“It’s not that!” Inako cried, but even to herself, her words sounded exactly like someone confessing to elopement—only now did she realize it, and she flushed with embarrassment. Meanwhile, Mits held up a mirror, gazing at Inako with a wistful, almost pained expression, muttering under her breath, “Elopement… double suicide…”

“Here we go again. Mits always gets fired up over stories like that.”

“You should try indulging in a little romance yourself, Ito-chan? I hear *Golden Yaksha* is good.”

“I’d rather something funny. Nothing beats listening to rakugo at a yose theater.”

“Oh dear me. So Ito-chan doesn’t understand matters of the heart at all, does she?”

Mits teased in an affected feminine drawl. “Whatever,” Ito shot back, voice tight. “I’m just the girl who gets mocked by Yajiro all the time for having ‘no charm.’” She slashed off a stray lock with sharp emphasis.

“S-so anyway,” Inako interjected, fearing her ears might be next, “do you all come to Sakamoto-san’s house often?”

“Yeah. We get free lessons from Kihachi-niisan. We gather at night after finishing chores—call it the Night Study Meeting. Seems like lots of other households run similar gatherings too.”

“Well, Kihachi-niisan’s meetings do tend to veer off-topic pretty quickly…”

“Right. If anything comes up, it’s always ‘electricity, electricity’ this and that.”

Inako found this unexpected side of Kihachi strangely endearing—and couldn’t help bursting into laughter.

All the while, Ito made her final snips. After shaking off the newspaper, Inako peered into the mirror Mits held up. The uneven, flyaway strands were now neatly trimmed, sloping gently forward along the collar of her kimono.

---

2.

“It looks like I’ve turned into a kokeshi doll…”

Inako, on the verge of tears, was comforted by the two women: “You look adorable,” “It really suits you.” The sun had set during her haircut, and lamplight now spilled from inside the shop.

Staring at that lonely glow, she felt an unbearable restlessness welling up inside. What kind of commotion must be happening at the house in Fushimi right now? And how on earth should she face them when she returned?

Her anxieties were endless—especially with her wedding to Yōsuke just days away. One bizarre event after another had unfolded so rapidly that she felt as if she’d been swept far away from reality.

When she looked up at the night sky, the lamp inside the shop suddenly went out, plunging the surroundings into complete darkness.

Ito and Mits were nowhere to be seen. Inako peered into the darkened interior of the shop.

“Sakamoto-san?”

She called out, but there was no reply. At the back, she spotted the faint flicker of candlelight.

Swallowing hard, Inako stepped hesitantly into the eerily quiet shop, her legs heavy with dread.

Candles were lined up along the narrow earthen floor at the rear of the shop. Following the light, she saw they led from the earth-floored area into a side room, then onward from the room toward the staircase cabinet in the innermost part of the building.

Inako slipped off her clogs, stepped into the room, and called again softly from beneath the stairs:

“Sakamoto-san?”

Still, no answer. The second floor above was pitch black, invisible.

Gathering her courage, Inako ascended the staircase cabinet and entered the second-floor room. As expected, it was completely dark—she could make out nothing. Shivering under the strange, oppressive atmosphere—

*“So you’re Inako-chan, aren’t you?”*

2.

A boy’s voice, unfamiliar to her, echoed in the darkness, and Inako screamed.

“Don’t be so scared. I heard you wanted to go to Paradise—so I thought I’d take you there.”

“What… what do you mean?” Inako asked into the dark.

“I’ve already called Amida-sama. He’ll be coming to welcome you any moment now.”

“Paradise? Wait—I didn’t die, did I? When did I die?”

Just as she was completely overwhelmed by fear, a brilliant light suddenly flared outside the window.

“Now we set forth on our joyous journey to the Pure Land of ten trillion Buddhas! How splendid the path ahead!”

From within the light emerged Amida Nyorai, shining in radiant gold.

Surrounding the Buddha were countless bodhisattvas, swaying merrily atop clouds. Faced with a scene straight out of a *raigozu* painting she had once seen, Inako’s legs gave way entirely.

With a lively *don-chan don-chan*, music evoking Paradise drew nearer along with the approaching figures.

“Please spare me! I’m not ready yet—just a little more time!”

Inako cried out desperately, nearly shrieking, “Mannamanchan-an, Mannamanchan-an!”

“…I never thought she’d be this gullible.”

A voice came from behind. “Huh?” She whirled around—only for laughter to erupt all at once.

Sakamoto Kihachi, standing behind her, pulled off the black cloth he had been wearing. From inside the closet, Yajiro and Sadakichi emerged carrying a phonograph that had been playing the celestial music. Up from the foot of the stairs, Ito and Mitsu burst forth, laughing.

Still dazed and unable to comprehend what had happened, Inako stood frozen. Kihachi, sweating slightly at the temples, pointed to a box beside him.

“This. This is it—the magic lantern.”  

2.

On the stand sat a tin box shaped like a fish cake. Attached to the box was a tube, and from its forward lens, light stretched straight out the window, projecting an image of the Coming of Amida onto the broad white wall of the neighboring house.

The magic lantern was a device that worked by inserting translucent slides—called *genjōban*—between the lens and the light source; when illuminated, the painted images on the slides were enlarged and projected. Multiple slides could be combined, and by manually shifting them—as had been done just moments ago with the bodhisattva—it was possible to create the illusion of movement in the picture. Now that she understood the truth, Inako collapsed inwardly, all tension draining away.

“Big sister, you were really startled!”

Sadakichi, who hadn’t spoken a single word when they first met, smiled shyly. “So *you* were the one making that voice,” Inako said, gently pinching his cheeks. Just then, the projector flickered and went dark, plunging the room into blackness.

Yajiro swiftly struck a match and lit the oil lamp.

“Kihachi-niichan, your flashlight’s busted again, looks like.”

“I could’ve sworn I had plenty of dry cells… Maybe the brass plate’s acting up?”

Frowning slightly, Kihachi removed the flashlight from the magic lantern and replaced it with the oil lamp.

At that moment, Bunshichi arrived carrying a tray with rice balls and takazuke pickles. Watching the children leap at him with cheers and immediately stuff their mouths, Inako murmured quietly to Kihachi:

“So ‘Paradise’… that was just the magic lantern, huh.”

“Well, I *did* show you Paradise. Didn’t lie about that. Got a problem with it?”

Inako let out a deep sigh and tugged at Kihachi’s sleeve.

“…Are there more tricks like this?”

2.

When the *raigō-e* [image of Amida’s descent] ended, various magic lantern slides appeared on the wall—foreign landscapes, comical scenes of a mouse leaping into a sleeping man’s mouth, and more. During the theatrical scene from *Yoshitsune’s劝進帳 (Kanjin-chō)*, Yajiro and Sadakichi improvised voices, and Inako, clutching a rice ball in one hand, thoroughly enjoyed the magical world of the lantern.

Once the projections concluded, Inako gave a lecture to the children on how to catch mice. She had started it merely as a gesture of thanks, but to her surprise, the children loved it. As soon as Inako finished, Kihachi seized the still-warm momentum and launched into an impassioned explanation about the prophecies of the *Denki Mokuroku*.

“No way, that’s nonsense—lemons can’t possibly replace batteries!”

Itō laughed as she objected.

“No, apparently lemon juice works as an electrolyte. That’s why I tried building my own version of the *Denki Mokuroku*’s ‘Fifteenth: Lemon Bomb.’ It’s a celebratory timed bomb powered by lemons—when it goes off, confetti flies everywhere.”

“And just where would you use something like that?” asked Yajiro.

“Hold on, let me finish. Last month, a new bookstore opened on Sanjō Fu-yamachi. On opening day, I set the ‘Lemon Bomb’ right on top of their display stack of books, then hid behind a nearby shelf to watch.”

“And did it explode?” Sadakichi asked.

“No, not at all,” Kihachi replied flatly, shaking his head.

“It didn’t go off—instead, some shop clerk came along and just bit into the lemon… My hard-earned money spent on those lemons, and they just—”

Dejected, Kihachi slumped. The children burst into laughter. “Well, clearly people would rather have the lemon than your stupid bomb,” Mitsue said, wiping tears from the corners of her eyes.

2.

“What are you talking about? The lemon battery component here is removable—it can even power other machines—”

As Kihachi continued explaining, the children jeered from a short distance away: “Think up something more useful!”

“Well then, there’s this item listed in the catalog: ‘Thirteen—Electric Catfish.’ It’s a machine shaped like a catfish; you put dishes or pots into its mouth, and using electricity, it vibrates the water inside to clean off grease and burnt residue in just seconds.”

Inako, watching Kihachi’s enthusiastic explanation from a little ways off, was bewildered, thinking to herself, *Such strange contraptions…* But beside her, Yajirō narrowed his eyes and said, “Still, Brother’s serious about it.”

“Kinako-nee, have you seen the tea maker?” When Inako nodded, Yajirō pointed at Ito, who was quietly giggling.

“Ito’s family has lots of younger brothers and sisters. Brother gathered materials and built that tea maker specially for her, so they could reduce the housework a bit.”

“He’s not satisfied with it though, since it doesn’t run on electricity,” Yajirō added.

“It was Brother who suggested the kimono shop next door buy a telephone too. The owner loves to chat, but his legs and back are weak. Brother said, ‘With a phone, you can talk to friends without having to go out.’”

As she listened to Yajirō, Inako gazed absentmindedly at Kihachi’s lively profile as he spoke warmly to the children.

“Brother always finds ways to make us happy.”

Inako had dozed off. Into her drowsy ears came the uneven sounds of sleeping breaths. She opened her heavy eyelids and saw the Night Study Meeting children piled atop one another, fast asleep across the tatami floor.

2.

She sat up. Outside the window, it was still dark, and Kihachi was fiddling with something at the desk lit by a lamp. Trying not to make a sound, she approached and peered over his shoulder. Kihachi was making the flashlight in his hands flicker on and off.

“Did you fix the flashlight?”

Startled by her sudden voice, Kihachi flinched visibly. “You were awake?”

“The spring inside was just weak. It’s an old flashlight, after all.”

Inako tucked a short strand of hair behind her ear and sat down beside Kihachi. He stared at her blankly, and when she tilted her head slightly in response, he quickly averted his gaze.

“Is something wrong with my hair?”

“…No. I was just thinking—how women don’t need to go to much trouble to wield their weapons. A simple gesture, and you catch a glimpse of the pale nape beneath the hair. Once you’ve seen that, even the toughest man is done for.”

Kihachi glanced briefly at Inako’s figure, then shook his head with an air of regret.

“But with a body like a soft *neri yōkan* sweet, maybe the nape is the only weapon you’ve got.”

Infuriated by the insult, she pinched him sharply in the side. Kihachi let out a pitiful yelp.

“I don’t care about my nape. I just wanted to grow my hair as long as my sister’s.”

“So you’d wear a schoolgirl’s *hishagami* topknot? This length is perfect—no need to tie it up every day, easy to wash. It’s the rational hairstyle of the new century.”

“Alright!” Kihachi slapped his hands together.

“From now on, this style shall be known as the ‘Twentieth Century Hair’!”

---

2.

“It makes no sense at all,” Inako said, utterly baffled—yet a moment later, she let out a small snort of laughter. “But anyway, thanks.” She smiled. Though she’d been feeling down since her hair was cut short, this was probably the first time she’d genuinely laughed.

Kihachi turned his face away, looking awkward, a single bead of sweat trailing down his temple—whether from the heat or embarrassment, it was hard to say. Inako pulled out a handkerchief, reaching to wipe the sweat from Kihachi’s brow, but he flinched back sharply.  
“What’s with you all of a sudden?”

“You were sweating so much, I thought I’d wipe it for you.”

“Didn’t you just use that handkerie to catch a mouse?”

“Oh yeah, I did,” Inako said, sticking out her tongue. The two burst into laughter at once.

“Still, I can’t get used to Kyoto’s muggy heat. I’d kill for a refrigerator right now.”

“A refrigerator?”

“A machine they displayed four years ago at the National Industrial Exhibition in Osaka. An entire building—just one giant machine for cooling things down. Inside, it was so cold I nearly froze to death, even though it was summer. At the entrance, there was a model of Mount Fuji, and the snow on its peak was made from ice chilled by the refrigerator itself. Now that was poetic.”

Kihachi lit the lamp of the magic lantern and slid in a new幻燈板. Outside the window, a colored photograph of Mount Fuji draped in snow shimmered into view, and Inako’s heart leapt at its beauty.
