“These days we rely on the *Ryōfūki* and electric fans, but someday, we’ll have refrigerators that can cool entire houses. The kind of sweltering heat that makes Kyoto feel like hell will be nothing but a memory.”

And it’s not just that, Kihachi thought, glancing at the sleeping children.

2.

“Instead of lamps, if we use electric lights, there’ll be no need to clean the soot-stained firebox. Once we can easily boil water with electricity, people will be able to bathe every day. Then children won’t have to spend so much time helping out—they’ll be free to study or do what they love.”

“Can electricity really do all that?”

“—Electricity’s something you can live without anyway.”

Hearing those words—the very ones she’d once flung at Kihachi—Inako flinched.

Kihachi said with a wry smile, “I used to think the same way, y’know.”

“I didn’t understand electricity at all. When I was a kid, I even thought Asakusa’s *denki buran* was just fuel for electric lamps. Now that I think about it, what on earth does sake have to do with electricity, right?”

And with that, Kihachi let his smile soften.

“It was at the exhibition four years ago that I first came to understand the power electricity holds.”

“Did you see something like this magic lantern again?”

“No,” Kihachi shook his head. “Something far greater.”

“I saw Paradise.”

　　　　　　　　　　＊

The day he’d been taken by his older brother Seiroku to the Osaka exhibition. Seiroku had said, “I’ll be right back,” and gone to the toilet—but never returned. As dusk fell over the grounds, Kihachi remained alone, teetering on the edge of despair.

The exhibit halls that had enchanted him during the day now loomed as black silhouettes, seeming like giants intent on trapping him here forever. Or perhaps a kidnapper would emerge from the dispersing crowd and carry him off somewhere unknown. Kihachi crouched low on the ground, trembling, waiting endlessly for someone—anyone—to save him.

2.

That was the day he’d been taken by his older brother Seiroku to the Osaka exhibition. Seiroku had said, “I’ll be right back,” and gone to the toilet—but never returned. As dusk fell over the grounds, Kihachi remained alone, teetering on the edge of despair. The exhibit halls that had enchanted him during the day now loomed as black silhouettes, seeming like giants intent on trapping him here forever. Or perhaps a kidnapper would emerge from the dispersing crowd and carry him off somewhere unknown. Kihachi crouched low on the ground, trembling, waiting endlessly for someone—anyone—to save him.

Then, suddenly, the world beyond his tightly closed eyelids grew bright.

A warm, powerful light, rivaling the sun itself, spilled outward. From the surrounding crowd came a rising wave of murmurs and gasps of wonder.

What on earth was happening? A faint spark of curiosity stirred within Kihachi, and hesitantly, he began to open his eyes.

And at the sight that unfolded before him, Kihachi was utterly speechless.

Kihachi stood within a world of light.

Though night had fallen, the area was bathed in brightness so intense it felt like midday.

He rose to his feet and surveyed the grounds. The exhibition halls encircling the main avenue shimmered in golden radiance. Statues such as the Willow and Willow观音 and fountains cast off multicolored beams like rainbows. In this brilliant illumination, the ecstatic expressions of the crowd were clearly visible. Beside Kihachi, a middle-aged man, overcome with emotion, murmured softly:

“So this is the grand spectacle of the exhibition—*illumināshon*.”

Illumination.

Upon closer inspection, countless electric bulbs were affixed along the outer walls of the exhibition halls and main gate. It was the combined glow of these countless bulbs that had conjured this golden world.

As he stood dazed by the brilliance, an elderly woman nearby folded her wrinkled hands and reverently intoned, “Namu Amida Butsu,” bowing before the shimmering lights.

At that moment, everything Kihachi had seen and heard at the exhibition surged vividly through his mind:

The divine, heavenly breeze generated by the electric fan. The exquisite music played by the phonograph. The *Denki Hi Odori*, dazzling and radiant as celestial nymphs dancing.

2.

Bulbs were affixed along the outer walls of the exhibition halls and main gate. It was the combined glow of these countless bulbs that had conjured this golden world. As he stood dazed by the brilliance, an elderly woman nearby folded her wrinkled hands and reverently intoned, “Namu Amida Butsu,” bowing before the shimmering lights. At that moment, everything Kihachi had seen and heard at the exhibition surged vividly through his mind: The divine, heavenly breeze generated by the electric fan. The exquisite music played by the phonograph. The *Denki Hi Odori*, dazzling and radiant as celestial nymphs dancing.

And then—turning night into day—the boundless radiance of electricity.

“Ah… So this is Paradise.”

He was standing in the very realm of Paradise, a world he had never been able to imagine no matter how hard he tried.

“The twentieth century is the age of electricity! An age when even Paradise itself can be created with electricity!”

An inexhaustible vitality welled up from deep within his body. He had never felt anything like this before.

“I will create Paradise with electricity! If the world ahead lies in darkness, I’ll illuminate it with electric lamps!”

From his breast pocket, Kihachi pulled out the *Denki Mokuroku* and raised it toward the illumination display. Above the glowing grounds, the searchlight atop the Murai Brothers’ *Kōtō* swept the night sky in wide arcs.

Just as Kihachi was about to put the *Denki Mokuroku* away, a figure glimpsed through the crowd caught his eye. Instantly, his joy evaporated. Without a word, Kihachi dashed off.

Panting, he reached the flowerbed—and there, seated leisurely on a bench, Seiroku casually raised a hand. “Hey.”

“What… are you doing?”

“When I came out of the restroom, the illuminations had just started. They’re beautiful, so I stayed here to watch.”

“But you said you’d come right back…”

“Oh? Were you lonely?”

Seiroku showed no remorse. Kihachi rushed forward and pounded his thin chest fiercely with both fists.

Tears spilled from Kihachi’s eyes. He couldn’t tell whether they came from frustration, anger, or the overwhelming relief of no longer feeling alone.

2.

That very day, upon returning from the exhibition, just as expected, Enki’s fists rained down dozens of blows upon Kihachi’s head.

Yet Kihachi was so brimming with unprecedented vitality that not even Enki’s blows could faze him.

From then on, he threw himself into his studies at upper elementary school with renewed vigor, and thanks to the welling spring of imagination now surging within him, the catalog—previously half-empty—had already been filled up to entry number nineteen by the end of summer.

Just one left. One day, while carefully pondering what prophecy should occupy the final twentieth slot, Kihachi was summoned by Enki to discuss his future plans following his graduation from upper elementary school next year.

“…I’d assumed that after finishing upper elementary school, you’d take over the temple duties.”

Enki scratched his closely cropped hair impassively as Kihachi voiced his desire to attend middle school.

“As I’ve said before, we simply don’t have the money to send you to middle school.”

Kihachi had previously brought up his wish to continue his education, even before visiting the exhibition.

But last year, repairs to the main hall—which had suffered severe damage—ended up costing far more than anticipated. Offerings from parishioners hadn’t come in as hoped, forcing the temple to exhaust nearly all its savings. There was no longer any leeway to fund further schooling, and so the matter had been dropped.

“The temple will be mine eventually—I’ll inherit it. But that’s exactly why I want to study electricity. Electricity can make people’s lives easier. And for our parishioners who fear the afterlife, I can use electricity to create a vision of Paradise and show them what it’s truly like. That’s why I need to learn more—”

2.

“Now, what did you just say?”

Up until then calm, Enki’s demeanor abruptly shifted as he cut Sakamoto Kihachi off mid-sentence, his tone sharp and severe.

“Do you not believe in Amida-sama?”

Caught off guard by this unexpected reaction, Kihachi was flustered.

“I’m asking you—do you doubt the power of Amida? Answer me, you fool!”

Enki’s enraged voice shook the air itself, and Kihachi shrank back in fear.

“If you truly believe, Amida will surely lead all to the Pure Land. I’ve told you countless times: trying to save people through your own strength is doubting Amida’s power. And now—you dare speak of constructing Paradise with electricity?”

Enraged, Enki seized Kihachi by the collar and lifted him up.

“The very idea that you could utter such foolishness… Amida-sama, our ancestors, Akubi Ōmyōjin—they are all watching over you! How can you so calmly turn your back on them?”

In the force of being shoved away, the *Denki Mokuroku* slipped from Kihachi’s breast pocket. Before he could retrieve it, Enki snatched it up and flipped open its pages. As he leafed through it, his face began to twitch uncontrollably.

“So this is all you’ve been thinking about—such meaningless nonsense…”

Grabbing Kihachi roughly by the nape, Enki dragged him down the corridor into the kitchen. He shoved aside Kihachi’s mother Kei, who had been preparing lunch, and stood before the hearth. Realizing what Enki intended, Kihachi struggled desperately—but Enki showed no hesitation. He flung the *Denki Mokuroku* straight into the blazing fire beneath the stove.

“No—!”

His cry rang out in vain. In an instant, flames engulfed the *Denki Mokuroku*.

---

2.

Without a moment’s hesitation, Kihachi plunged his hand into the fire and snatched out the *Denki Mokuroku*, slapping the flames to extinguish them. But the booklet was already scorched in places, covered in ash, and parts of it had burned away entirely.

“You’ve gone too far this time—Kihachi doesn’t deserve this!”

“He’ll never wake up unless I do something drastic like this!”

As his parents argued, Kihachi felt their voices as if from some distant place, staring blankly at the ruined *Denki Mokuroku* in his hands. The searing pain in his burned fingers throbbed relentlessly, gnawing at him.

A few days later, Seiroku visited the house and, upon seeing the state of the *Denki Mokuroku*, muttered, “What a mess.”

“Hey, Kihachi. Mind if I take this catalogue off your hands for a while?”

“To you?” Kihachi asked dully.

“Yeah. If you keep holding onto it, your old man might just burn it again. Let me hang on to it until things cool down—and in the meantime, I’ll fix up the charred parts so they’re readable again.”

When Kihachi frowned skeptically, Seiroku gave a wry smile. “Trust me.”

Honestly, given what he knew of Seiroku up till now, Kihachi wasn’t sure whether he should hand it over so easily. Yet the thought of continuing to look at the damaged *Denki Mokuroku* pained him deeply.

“…Alright then. I’m counting on you. But make sure you return it. I still haven’t fulfilled the final prophecy yet.”

As Kihachi passed over the *Denki Mokuroku*, Seiroku flashed a grin, revealing his white teeth.

“Well, well. You’ve really gotten serious about this, huh?”

“I’m going to build Paradise with electricity,” Kihachi declared proudly.  
“I’ll bring every prophecy in the *Denki Mokuroku* to life—and prove to you, my own brother who doesn’t believe in Paradise, that it truly exists.”

2.

Seiroku looked taken aback, but quickly flashed a provocative grin.

“Go ahead then—if you can do it, do it.”

　　　　　　　　　　＊

Having finished speaking, Kihachi pulled several scraps of paper from the stack on his desk and handed them to Inako with a quiet “Here, read these.” Just like the previous *Yoru Shirazu*, the sheets were covered with diagrams and names of prophecies.

“*Number Seven: Denyaku Seisho*… A phonograph that automatically translates spoken foreign languages. *Number Fourteen: Denki-shiki Hakkudoku-mizu*… Water imbued with various flavors by electricity.”

As Inako skimmed through the prophecies, she suddenly felt a sense of unease.

“Didn’t you say the *Denki Mokuroku* was a bound booklet?”

“These papers I just gave you are only copies of the catalog… The original one hasn’t been returned yet.”

Inako instinctively raised her head. “Why not?”

“My brother—”

Kihachi hesitated mid-sentence, as if weighing his words. After a brief pause, he forced out the rest.

“My brother disappeared somewhere… taking the catalog with him.”

The lamplight flickered in Kihachi’s eyes. Inako wondered what could have happened between the two brothers, but sensed it was something she shouldn’t pry into, and held back from asking further.

“…My brother always hated the idea of succeeding the temple. Maybe he thought that if I kept going to school, he’d end up being forced to take over, so he took the catalog to make me give up my path with electricity.”

“But even if he disliked it, would he really do something so selfish?”

2.

“He was the kind of person who’d laugh mockingly every time he read the catalog. When I told him, ‘I’m going to create Paradise with electricity,’ he looked like he wanted to say, ‘There’s no way that’s possible.’”

Kihachi fixed a serious gaze on the copied *Denki Mokuroku* spread out on the table.

“It was so long ago—some of the prophecies must’ve slipped my mind. But if the catalog ever comes back, I’ll write down the final twentieth prophecy and complete it. Then I’ll fulfill every single one, just to prove myself to my brother.”

“Ahh, I see,” Kihachi murmured, as if coming to a private understanding, then gave Inako a strong, resolute smile.

“Creating Paradise with electricity… maybe that’s what gives my life meaning. Someday, I’ll fill this still-dark country with nothing but the light of electric lamps.”

His eyes shone with such brilliance that Inako couldn’t bear to look directly at them. She turned her gaze toward the window instead. Unnoticed, the pitch-black sky had begun to tint with blue; gradually, the outside world brightened, and the silhouette of the magic lantern’s Mount Fuji grew fainter, dissolving into the dawn.

“…I said I wanted to go to Paradise, but actually… there’s another place I really want to see.”

Her whisper was nearly inaudible, but Kihachi heard it. “Oh? Where’s that?”

“I’ve always wanted to see Mount Fuji once. My mother used to say there’s a spot nearby where you can see the real Mount Fuji.”

As Inako glanced sideways at the fading image of Fuji, Kihachi spoke hesitantly:  
“…About Inago’s mother… I’m sorry. Calling her a fraud back then… I shouldn’t have said that.”

2.

As the silhouette of the magic lantern’s Mount Fuji grew fainter, dissolving into the dawn, “I said I wanted to go to Paradise, but actually… there’s another place I really want to see.” Her whisper was nearly inaudible, but Kihachi heard it.  
“Oh? Where’s that?”  
“I’ve always wanted to see Mount Fuji once. My mother used to say there’s a spot nearby where you can see the real Mount Fuji.”  

As Inako glanced sideways at the fading image of Fuji, Kihachi spoke hesitantly:  
“…About Inago’s mother… I’m sorry. Calling her a fraud back then… I shouldn’t have said that.”  

Inako looked surprised. Kihachi puckered his lips and muttered, “I’d been meaning to apologize, but kept missing the chance.” Finally understanding why Kihachi had gone out of his way to come all the way to Fushimi, Inako smiled faintly.  

“You know… maybe when my mother told me about Mount Fuji, she wasn’t lying—maybe she just wanted to cheer me up.”  

Kihachi stared blankly, waiting for her to continue.  

“She always explained sake brewing in a way even children could understand… by using gods.”  

Mother had often told her: *There are countless invisible gods in sake.*  

These “gods” were none other than the essential koji mold and the yeasts dwelling within the brewery. In the kōjimuro, the koji mold was cultivated on steamed rice to make koji; through motosuri, yeast was nurtured. The “evil gods” that spoiled the sake were, in truth, unwanted microbes intruding during production. Among them, the bacteria known as *hi-ochi kin* were known to cause *hi-ochi* spoilage—but even now, its exact origin and reliable countermeasures remained poorly understood.  

“Even knowing the ‘gods’ behind sake are microbes, there’s still so much beyond human control. In the end, we can only believe in the power of the gods. Asking for more would anger them. Because my faith isn’t strong enough, Father and Onee-chan and all the kurabito suffered last time—I don’t want to burden them again.”  

“Inago…”  

“I may be slow, but I can finally be of use to the family. So I’ll stay here with Dr. Paikaru—”  

“Inago!”  

Suddenly, Kihachi thrust a one-sen coin right before Inako’s eyes.

---

2.

Staring wide-eyed at the coin, Inako blinked rapidly.

“When you helped drive off that fake *haibei*, you gave me this—consider it your offering money,” Kihachi said.

“The power of that offering still holds.”

 ——Instead of a god whose existence I can’t even be sure of, at least someone listened to my complaints.

Inako suddenly remembered Kihachi’s words at the Inari shrine—and her eyes slowly widened.

“I… I thought you were joking back then…”

“You can treat me as your god now. Before me, say whatever you want—no limits.”

“—No…”

She pressed her hands over her eyes, but couldn’t hold it in any longer. The flood surged forth all at once.

“No. I don’t want to. I don’t want to go back home. I don’t want to be with that person ever again.”

Plop. Plop. Heavy tears struck the tatami, one after another.

“I try every single day. I want to be good at housework and lessons like Onee-chan. But I can’t. No matter how hard I push myself, nothing turns out right. There’s something wrong with my head. Don’t say I’m ‘slow’—you don’t have to say it out loud. I know it better than anyone. *I know.*”

Outside, daylight crept stronger, and the image of Mount Fuji projected on the white wall began to fade.

A *genjō* cannot be seen in bright places.

“I hate this time of day. I never want morning to come. I don’t want anyone seeing me like this—looking foolish, being laughed at. I wish I could stay locked away in darkness forever. Because no matter what I do outside… I’ll only fail. Others will just find it funny and laugh. Or worse—they’ll only feel disappointed in me.”

---

2.

Inako’s face, flushed crimson, was soaked with tears, and her sobbing, along with the tears themselves, poured forth without end.

“I want to die. I don’t believe I can ever live properly again. I just want to disappear.”

Mount Fuji had completely vanished now, leaving only a blank white wall stretching across the space.

“I want to die, I want to die.”

Inako kept hurling those same words at the vanished mountain, over and over.

　　　　　　　　　　＊

Morning came. The members of the Night Study Meeting woke one by one, quickly ate a simple breakfast, and began cleaning up from the previous day.

Sadakichi silently polished the magic lantern; Itō scolded Yajiro for carelessly cleaning the phonograph; Mitsue swept listlessly, eyes half-dreaming as she gazed at Kihachi and Inako; Bunshichi smoked a morning cigarette while minding the shop.

Amid everyone going about their usual routines, Inako alone worked in silence, her face sunken and dark.

Kihachi had brought her here hoping it might lift her spirits even slightly—but the shadow she carried was so deep that not even a flashlight could illuminate its depths. Could he really send her back home like this?

As Kihachi agonized, an incongruously cheerful male voice cut through his thoughts: “Excuse me!”

Wondering who on earth would visit so early, he turned—only to see Inako freeze, her face turning deathly pale.

“That’s Dr. Paikaru’s voice.”

Startled, Kihachi raised a finger to his lips, signaling silence. He turned to Yajiro, who had just approached holding the phonograph, and whispered, “Brother, put that away somewhere,” before stepping down from the dais and peering cautiously from the living room into the shop area.

---

2.

In the sales area, a man wearing a high-crowned hat was speaking with Bunshichi.

When Bunshichi turned toward him, he beckoned: “Kihachi, this man has something for you.”

Could he have come to pick up Inako? Cautious, Kihachi stepped into the shop, only to see the man bow deeply and courteously.

“You must be Sakamoto Kihachi. My name is Mitsue Yōsuke, branch manager of the Fushimi branch of Mitsue Shōten.”

At Yōsuke’s sly, ingratiating smile, Kihachi asked coolly, “Are you here about a mechanical repair?”

“Mechanical? Ah, yes—I remember now. Your signboard says ‘Mechanical Repairs Undertaken.’”

“It’s true I can fix most machines, so people often come asking me to do repairs.”

“I see,” said Yōsuke, stroking his chin. “Just as one would expect from the owner of that extraordinary book, the *Denki Mokuroku*.”

“Huh—?”

Out of nowhere, those utterly unexpected words tumbled from Yōsuke’s mouth.

“The matter I’ve come about,” he continued, “is precisely that *Denki Mokuroku*.”

Bewildered, Kihachi could only stare as Yōsuke went on, saying something even more unbelievable.

“I’ll speak plainly. Would you sell the *Denki Mokuroku* to me?”

Kihachi froze, mouth agape. The words passed through one ear and out the other; only belatedly did their meaning sink into his mind. “Wha…?” he managed, his voice hollow and drained of air.

“The *Denki Mokuroku*. That prophecy you wrote, correct?”

“What are you talking about? That’s just some childish doodling.”

“No need to be modest. For business reasons, I absolutely must know what’s written in that catalog.”

---

2.

“is precisely that *Denki Mokuroku*.” Bewildered, Kihachi could only stare as Yōsuke went on, saying something even more unbelievable. “I’ll speak plainly. Would you sell the *Denki Mokuroku* to me?” Kihachi froze, mouth agape. The words passed through one ear and out the other; only belatedly did their meaning sink into his mind. “Wha…?” he managed, his voice hollow and drained of air. “The *Denki Mokuroku*. That prophecy you wrote, correct?” “What are you talking about? That’s just some childish doodling.” “No need to be modest. For business reasons, I absolutely must know what’s written in that catalog.”

Yōsuke deepened his contemptuous smile and leaned in. “To be precise,” he said, “it’s *Number Twenty* of the *Denki Mokuroku*. The prophecy written there—that’s what matters.”

“…Number Twenty…?”

Kihachi struggled to make sense of the situation, his mind reaching the peak of confusion.  
He’d thought Yōsuke had come to reclaim Inako—but instead, he was here to buy the *Denki Mokuroku*, something only his family should know about.  
This man claimed he needed to know the contents of the twentieth prophecy—a prophecy Kihachi didn’t even remember writing—for business purposes.  
Rather than clarifying anything, the situation only grew more bewildering. Kihachi almost laughed. What on earth was going on?

“Even if you spring this on me out of nowhere… besides, I don’t have the *Denki Mokuroku* on me right now.”

“No?” Yōsuke’s face, still fixed in a smile, visibly darkened with displeasure. “Don’t lie. I know for a fact that you have it.”

“It’s not a lie. And anyway, how do *you* even know about the *Denki Mokuroku*?”

“That’s none of your concern. You just need to accept what we’re asking.”

His condescending tone made Kihachi instantly understand why Inako had refused to marry him—after only a few minutes together, it was clear.

“In any case, asking for something I don’t have is unreasonable.”

The matter of the *Denki Mokuroku* troubled him, but Kihachi had no desire to get further involved with this man. And if Yōsuke caught on to Inako’s presence, things would become complicated. He wanted him gone—fast.

“Fine. I’ll come again. Be ready with a proper answer next time.”

Just as Yōsuke turned on his heel, Kihachi began to let out a sigh of relief—

2.

“By the way,” Yōsuke suddenly turned back, making Kihachi’s blood run cold.

“You know Hundred Rivers Inako, don’t you? Didn’t you meet her yesterday?”

“…No, I didn’t. Is something wrong with her?”

“She ran away from home yesterday and hasn’t returned. She should be making contact by now.”

“Do you have any idea why she might’ve run away?”

When Kihachi subtly implied Yōsuke’s marriage to her, Yōsuke frowned in clear annoyance.

“No idea. Who can understand the thoughts of someone so bizarre?”

In that instant, the last trace of Kihachi’s polite customer-service demeanor vanished completely from his face.

“…Weren’t you the one supposed to marry her?”

Yōsuke’s eyes widened. “You know about that?”  
Kihachi instantly regretted speaking—had he said too much?

“I overheard it recently. Is it really proper to speak so ill of the woman you’re going to marry?”

“Well, think about it. Who else but me would willingly marry a useless woman like that?”

Yōsuke exaggeratedly shrugged his shoulders. It was meant to be a joke, but Kihachi didn’t even crack a smile. On the contrary, a slow, creeping heat began to rise behind his temples.

“This marriage is practically an act of charity. Don’t you think I’m admirable?”

Throbbing, throbbing—the image flashing through his increasingly heated mind was Inako’s face:  
Her furious expression when she desecrated the sacred mound. The look of stunned disbelief as she chased off the fake *haibei*. Her oft-seen, narrow-eyed stare of utter exasperation. And the profile she made when tucking short hair behind her ear—a face reminiscent of Noriko.

2.

“Thank you,” her smiling face.

And then, the face that wept, sobbing, “I want to die.”

“Inako-chan only *seems* like a good girl—truth is, she’s surprisingly arrogant. For someone so useless, she’s awfully picky about men. When I could’ve picked from any number of candidates, I went and chose her specially.”

This conversation must have reached the living room where Inako was sitting. Along with Yōsuke’s words, laced with malice.

What kind of expression did Inako wear right now?

“Oops, I’ve chatted too long. Well then, I’ll be off,” Yōsuke said, turning away again.

Kihachi wasn’t acting on any clear thought. His head burned too fiercely to think at all. The next moment, he shouted impulsively, “Wait just a minute!”

Yōsuke turned back with an annoyed look. Kihachi naturally clenched both fists tightly.

“The *Denki Mokuroku* isn’t gone—it’s simply not in my possession right now.”

When Kihachi glared, voice sharpened, Yōsuke twitched his eyebrows slightly.

“It’s being kept at my family home in Ōtsu. I’ll go retrieve it and deliver it to Dr. Paikaru myself.”

“Oh? How generous. Then payment will be handed over upon successful receipt—”

“No,” Kihachi cut in sharply. “I don’t want money.”

Yōsuke tilted his head, puzzled.

“Instead, call off your engagement to Hyakugawa Inako.”

Yōsuke’s face twisted openly. “What did you say?”

“I think the marriage has nothing to do with you. …Unless, perhaps, you’re—”

Yōsuke smirked defiantly, then broke off. “Never mind. Saying it would be in poor taste.”

2.

“So what will Dr. Paikaru do about it?”  
Yōsuke studied Kihachi carefully, then nodded. “Fine.”  
“If you can prepare the *Denki Mokuroku* by the wedding ceremony five days from now, I’ll cancel my engagement to Inako-chan.”  
He sneered mockingly, as if to say, *Are you satisfied now?*  
“Then by that deadline, I will deliver the *Denki Mokuroku* to Dr. Paikaru.”  
“I’ll hold you to it. Well, I’m looking forward to it.”  
With those condescending words, Yōsuke swiftly turned and left.  

Soon after, the members of the Night Study Meeting tumbled noisily out from the back of the living room. “What’s with that guy, with his sideburns,” “Mountain-high hat,” each grumbled in irritation—except Inako, who wore a troubled expression.  

“Well then…” Kihachi cheerfully slapped his palm with his fist.  
“What should we do next?”  
The children chorused, “Huh?”  

“You made such a promise ’cause you’ve got some idea where it is, right?”  
“Just bluffing. If I really knew, I’d’ve gone and gotten it ages ago.”  

Kihachi could only offer a wry smile at the impasse—when suddenly, Bunshichi cut in.  
“Well then, how ‘bout this—if you actually went back to Ōtsu? It’s about time for the *yabuiri* season, so I’ll give you time off.”  

Bunshichi raised one eyebrow expectantly. Kihachi grimaced.  
“…You want me to just waltz back into the house I ran away from three years ago, like nothing happened?”  
“It was three years ago, wasn’t it? They might welcome you back without much fuss. And besides—maybe Seiroku had a reason to hide the catalog somewhere inside the house.”  

2.

Kihachi folded his arms and grunted. No matter how hard he racked his brain, he couldn’t figure out why Yōsuke had known about the *Denki Mokuroku*, nor what this twentieth prophecy—something he’d never even written—could possibly be.

Then again… Could Seiroku, who had once kept the catalog, be somehow involved?

“…I’ve no intention of being tossed around blindly without knowing a thing.”

Kihachi exhaled deeply, set his face firmly, and spoke to Bunshichi, who was smirking faintly.  
“Uncle, I’m heading to Ōtsu right now—for *yabuiri* season.”

“You don’t need to do that.”

Inako cut in, her voice sharp, fists clenched tightly at her sides. The corners of her eyes were red and damp.  
“You don’t want to go back home. Don’t force yourself for my sake.”

“But Inako-san,” Mitsue said, her face as if facing the end of the world, “if the catalog really exists, the wedding will be called off. Isn’t that what you want? You hate the marriage, don’t you?”

“If I don’t endure this, it won’t just be the household—I’ll bring shame upon Sakamoto-san too.”

There was a painful stiffness in Inako’s words, like someone forcing themselves to walk on an injured leg.  
“If I keep acting selfishly… divine retribution will strike.”

　　　　　　　　　　＊

After Itō, Mitsue, and Sadakichi—children of Kyoto—had returned home, Inako, Kihachi, and Yajirō stood at the Keiden stop in Nijō-Kitayama. This was a transfer point between the Higashimawari Line and the Ōtō Line bound for Okazaki, and right beside it stood the workshop of “Japan’s Edison.”  

“Kinako-nee, are you really going back? Come with us to Ōtsu instead.”

2.

Inako did not answer. Instead, she gently stroked Yajirō’s head as the East Loop Line train arrived from the north. That train would take her back to Fushimi. She bowed deeply to Kihachi, who stood silently facing forward.

“Sakamoto-san… thank you for everything.”

When the train stopped at the platform, passengers began shuffling off one after another. Then Kihachi, who had been silent until now, spoke in a flat, toneless voice:

“You said earlier—‘divine retribution will strike,’ didn’t you?”

“If something terrible happens, who can prove it was the will of God or Buddha?”

“God exists. Even if we’re slow to understand, we must believe and wait. We can only pray and wait, hoping God will hand us rice cakes from the shelf like manna from heaven.”

No more passengers were getting off. Inako leapt onto the train as if fleeing.

“I think it’s better to reach up and grab the shelf yourself, rather than wait forever for a rice cake you might never receive.”

The train bell rang—ding, ding.

“If you look for yourself, you might find something good on that shelf besides rice cakes.”

Inako pretended not to hear. If she didn’t, she knew she wouldn’t be able to withstand his kindness. With a final, determined effort to break away, she called out, “Well then… I’ll repay you someday,” just as the train began to move.

“Inago’s mother didn’t lie.”

The East Loop Line train pulled away from the stop. Without realizing it, Inako had stepped back onto the platform. She now stood face to face with Kihachi, whose expression was utterly serious.

“There’s a place nearby where you can see Mount Fuji. I know it.”

“What do you mean?”

2.

“Besides rice cakes.” Inako pretended not to hear. If she acknowledged it, she knew she wouldn’t be able to withstand his kindness. With one final, determined effort to break away, she called out, “Well then… I’ll repay you someday,” just as the train began to move. “Inago’s mother didn’t lie.” The East Loop Line train pulled away from the stop. Without realizing it, Inako had stepped back onto the platform. She now stood face to face with Kihachi, whose expression was utterly serious.

“There’s a place nearby where you can see Mount Fuji. I know it.”

“What do you mean?”

「If you come with me, I’ll show you that spot. Doesn’t interest you?」

This time, the Kantō Line tram approached. It was the tram Kihachi and the others were meant to take.

“I’ll show you the Mount Fuji that Inago’s mother talked about.”

The metallic screech of wheels against the tracks carried a sound that somehow stirred urgency within Inako’s heart.

“…Why? Why do you care so much about me, Sakamoto-san?”

Kihachi suddenly leaned in close, his face nearly before hers, and Inako felt a ticklish sensation deep in her chest.

“Your eyes don’t have any light in them.”

“…Huh?”

“Inago shows all kinds of expressions, but her eyes stay dark. I told you before—I’m here to brighten the world. And I want to light up those eyes of yours.”

“Inako-kun!”

A firm, resonant voice thundered across the space, and from the far side of Kitayama-dōri, Roku came sprinting toward them.

“That guy definitely rubbed his butt on the tram earlier…” Kihachi murmured, stunned into silence. Trams moved at a speed humans could catch if they ran full tilt. At that distance, Roku would surely reach them.

But suddenly, right in front of Roku, a pile of lumber propped against a timber merchant’s wall toppled sideways.

“Sorry! We were playing and knocked it over!”

From the shadow of the building emerged Itō and Mitsu—supposedly already gone home. As they frantically bowed their heads to Roku, Sadakichi, standing beside them, delivered a perfectly convincing fake cry while secretly winking at Inako and the others.

The tram came to a halt at the stop. While Roku was occupied clearing the fallen timber, Yajirō boarded first, followed by Kihachi, who then turned and extended his hand toward Inako.

“So—will you join us, or not?”

2.

The hand that had pulled her out of the storehouse. But this time, there was no sign of anyone reaching for her from that side.

“Or,” Kihachi said, flashing a provocatively playful smile,  
“you don’t trust what I’m saying?”

Ding, ding—the bell chimed. The tram slowly began to move, and Kihachi’s hand slipped away.

“Trusting is what Inago’s good at, isn’t it?”  

Believing in the gods—that was her one defining trait. If she lost that, what would be left of her?

“I’m getting on!”

Inako took a bold step forward and reached her hand toward Kihachi.

Without hesitation, Kihachi seized her wrist and yanked her up forcefully. Stumbling a few steps inside, Inako stepped fully onto the tram and burst into lively laughter.

“Trusting’s my specialty. You won’t make a fool of me.”

“Fair enough,” Kihachi laughed back.

The tram steadily picked up speed, crossing the Kamo River lined with open-air summer dining platforms—*ryōbata*.  

Sunlight poured evenly into the car. Perhaps because she’d been shut away in the dark storehouse for so long, the scenery outside seemed brighter than ever before.

　　　　　　　　　　Four

As the tram approached Okazaki’s Nyōōmon-dōri, the vermilion-painted Ōtenmon gate of Heian Shrine came into view far to the left. Inako and Yajirō pressed their faces close to the window, gazing toward the city’s memorial zoo, opened four years prior.

Between Nyōōmon-dōri and the zoo flowed the Biwa Lake Aqueduct. To the east, it led to Nanzen-ji Temple’s boat basin. From the basin, the Keage Incline stretched straight upstream to the upper boat basin at Keage.

2.

“Huh, this is the first time I’ve seen an incline other than Sumizome.”

The Biwa Lake Aqueduct, constructed to link Lake Biwa and Kyoto with the aim of advancing water-powered industry, had been completed in Meiji 23 (1890). Utilizing the aqueduct, a hydroelectric power station began operations, supplying electricity that powered Japan’s first commercial electric railway, as well as industrial machinery and electric lighting. This became a major driving force behind Kyoto’s revival—a city that had been steadily declining since the capital’s relocation to Tokyo.

“Seeing the incline makes me think of the water chute at the National Industrial Exhibition.”

Kihachi narrowed his eyes fondly. The water chute had been one of the attractions at the exhibition—an amusement where boats carrying passengers slid down a steep slope and splashed dramatically into a pool below. However, the ride cost twenty sen, which was quite expensive, so Kihachi and Seiroku had apparently watched it from the precincts of Chaushiyama Temple.

The electric tram carrying the three of them climbed upward along the Ōtō Extension Line, which had just opened this month and ran alongside the incline.

On the adjacent incline, a boat mounted on a cart was being slowly winched upward by steel cables. A boatman, casually seated atop the cargo, leisurely puffed on his kiseru pipe. Along the mountainside, the dignified, hybrid Japanese-Western buildings of the Miyako Hotel stood in a row, their white walls standing out vividly against the surrounding greenery.

After disembarking, the trio boarded a *sosui* boat at Keage’s boat basin. The boat began to move forward slowly.

---

2.

The three of them climbed upward along the Ōtō Extension Line, which had just opened this month and ran alongside the incline. On the adjacent incline, a boat mounted on a cart was being slowly winched upward by steel cables. A boatman, casually seated atop the cargo, leisurely puffed on his *kiseru* pipe. Along the mountainside, the dignified, hybrid Japanese-Western buildings of the Miyako Hotel stood in a row, their white walls standing out vividly against the surrounding greenery. After disembarking, the trio boarded a *sosui* boat at Keage’s boat basin. The boat began to move forward slowly.

Unlike boats coming down from Lake Biwa, this one had to go against the current, so the boatman didn’t ride aboard. Instead, he walked along the boat-pulling path beside the aqueduct, the towing rope slung over his shoulder, pulling the vessel forward.

Kihachi had muttered, “I’m sleepy,” and then fallen asleep before they even entered the first tunnel. As the boat advanced along the mountain-foot canal, Yamazaki became the conversational partner.

“Our family are parishioners of Zenshoku-ji, so I’ve known your brother since we were kids. After he left the temple, we happened to meet again in Kyoto. When I mentioned I wanted to study English, he offered to teach me—just like that. That’s how the Night Study Meeting started, and later, Itsu and the others joined too.”

Yamazaki narrowed his eyes wistfully. “I wish I could’ve shown yesterday’s magic lantern show to Shōhei,” he whispered.

“Shōhei?”

“A kid from Ōtsu, same as me. He was the youngest in the Night Study Meeting. Such a lively boy… but he caught the flu and passed away. My brother gathered everyone and took us all to Ōtsu for the grave visit.”

“Sakamoto-san always makes sure to pay his respects.”

“He used to be more devout than most, actually. That’s why I was so surprised when I saw him again after a year. He’d changed completely—like something had snapped loose inside him.”

He glanced sideways. Kihachi still sat with arms folded, seemingly fast asleep.

---

2.

The boat passed through the boat basin and several short tunnels. A heron stood on each bank of the aqueduct, and as the boat approached, they flew off ahead like guides leading the way. Watching the scene, Yajiro let out a great stretch—revealing the characters "Yagura Takeyukibuchi Craft Shop" embroidered on the collar of his happi coat, which caught Inako’s eye.

“Come to think of it, Yajiro-kun, you make canes, don’t you?”

“Our family runs a bamboo root whipcraft shop. We also make handles for umbrellas and bags.”

“My father uses a bamboo root whipcraft cane. Says it’s sturdy and well-made.”

“Really?” Yajiro’s eyes sparkled.

Coming from someone who’s been whacked over the head with such canes for years, that was high praise indeed. They were annoyingly durable and long-lasting.

“You remember the incident when Tsar Nicholas II was attacked in Ōtsu, right? At that time, the Greek crown prince used a bamboo root whipcraft cane he’d bought as a souvenir to fend off the attacker. Word of that spread overseas, and lately we’ve even started getting orders from abroad.”

“Huh—foreigners too. So that’s why you’re trying to learn English, Yajiro-kun.”

She had thought of him as just a playful child, but clearly he was seriously thinking about the future in his own way.

“Exactly. The reason I’m studying English is to bring our country’s craftsmanship to the world, even if just a little—”

A finger shot out from the side and snapped against Yajiro’s forehead. “Ow!” he groaned.

Kihachi, who had apparently woken up without anyone noticing, pulled his hand back and wiped sweat from his face with bleary eyes.

---

2.

“We’ve even started getting orders from abroad.”  
“Huh—foreigners too. So that’s why you’re trying to learn English, Yajiro-kun.”  
She had thought of him as just a playful child, but clearly he was seriously thinking about the future in his own way.  
“Exactly. The reason I’m studying English is to bring our country’s craftsmanship to the world, even if just a little—”  

A finger shot out from the side and snapped against Yajiro’s forehead.  
“Ow!” he groaned.  

Kihachi, who had apparently woken up without anyone noticing, pulled his hand back and wiped sweat from his face with bleary eyes.  
--- 2.  

“Don’t believe everything Yajiro says. Once, a British family came by to see his shop, and he started learning English just so he could write a letter to the daughter—the girl he fell head over heels for at first sight—”  

“Don’t say it!” Yajiro cried, his ears turning bright red as he lunged to cover Kihachi’s mouth.  

Inako couldn’t help but let out a small laugh at such a thoroughly *Yajiro* sort of motivation.  

“Still,” Kihachi went on, gazing after a downstream boat gliding past, “in an age of trains and electric trams, the fact that *sosui* boats are still being pulled by people is downright outdated.”  

“Well then, what should we do about it?”  

“There’s something listed in the *Denki Mokuroku* called *Number Ten: Sosui Takarabune*. First, install overhead wires along the canal. Then, equip the boats with poles to draw power from the wires—just like trams. After that, going upstream would be effortless.”  

“But electricity—isn’t it too weak? Electric trams go so slow you could catch up if you ran hard.”  

“The fastest thing in the world today is the electric train. Germany’s electric locomotive has already exceeded two hundred kilometers per hour.”  

She didn’t quite grasp what “kilometers per hour” meant, but she got the gist—he was saying it was fast. So she simply nodded in silence. Yajiro also nodded dramatically, though he probably didn’t understand either.  

“If we put searchlights on the boats, visibility at night would be far better than with firelight—very convenient. And imagine them dashing through the darkness, their bulbs blazing like stars… truly the Treasure Ship of the Seven Lucky Gods.”  

“Trying to catch squid now, are we?”

2.

Inako was dumbfounded; Yajiro rolled with laughter. Apparently dissatisfied with their reactions, Kihachi sulkily crossed his arms and shut his eyes again, muttering, “I’m going to sleep.”

Ahead loomed a dark-mouthed brick tunnel. Just before entering, the boatman leapt from the boat-pulling path onto the deck, lit the forward lamp, and began hauling the vessel forward by gripping the iron chain fixed along the tunnel wall.

Inside the pitch-dark tunnel, it was cool. The ceiling shimmered with reflections of rippling water cast by the headlamp’s glow. The light at the far end appeared like a faint moon floating in the night sky.

After passing through the longest tunnel on the Biwa Lake Aqueduct, they had entered Shiga Prefecture.

After parting ways with Yajiro at Ōtsu’s boat basin, Inako followed Kihachi toward Mii-dera, a temple nestled at the foot of the mountain. They passed through an imposing gate reminiscent of Nanzen-ji’s Sanmon, then ascended the approach and stone steps.

Her wooden clogs clacked *karakoro* against the stones, echoing through the woods. The thick foliage shielded them from the harsh sunlight, and now and then a breeze—so refreshing it might have been called a wind from Paradise itself—swept through.

Kihachi strode past the Kannon Hall and bounded up the next flight of stairs two steps at a time. Inako hurried after him and emerged onto a bluff where stood a monument commemorating the Satsuma Rebellion.

Kihachi, already standing beside the monument, beckoned her over. “Now this is a fine view,” he said.

She stood beside him, calming her racing heart—and gasped at the vast scene spread out below.

“—It’s the sea.”

2.

The temple grounds, training fields, and rows of houses lay behind them, beyond which stretched a vast expanse of water so clear and boundless it seemed as though the entire blue sky had been poured onto the earth. Its depth extended far into the distance, where sky and water merged seamlessly. This was the first time Inako had ever seen, with her own eyes, a horizon.

As it neared the horizon, the color of the sky gradually lightened, becoming almost aquamarine where it met Lake Biwa. For the first time, Inako realized that the “blue” of the sky and the “blue” of the water were not the same at all.

“And there—that’s Mount Fuji.”

Kihachi pointed ahead, and Inako followed his gaze. To the north, the lake spread wide enough to reveal the horizon, but as it stretched southward, its width narrowed steadily. On the opposite shore, lush green plains dotted with small hamlets unfolded—and rising from that flatland stood a mountain shaped like a triangular rice ball.

“It’s called Mikami-yama,” Kihachi said. “But since ancient times, people have also called it Ōmi-Fuji.”

Compared to the ring of mountains enclosing Lake Biwa and its surrounding lands, it wasn’t particularly tall. Yet its proud, solitary presence jutting out from the flat terrain made it understandable why one might liken it to Mount Fuji.

As Inako gazed at the mountain—so earnest in its attempt to appear grand, like a child standing on tiptoe—she suddenly found it oddly amusing.

“Well now, Ōmi’s Mount Fuji is awfully cute.”

Seeing her smile, Kihachi teased, “Want me to tell you another name for Ōmi-Fuji?”

His tone was mischievous. “They also call it… *Mukadeyama*—the Centipede Mountain.”

*Mukade*. The mere three syllables sent a shiver of cold dread coursing through her entire body. Inako trembled involuntarily.

“W-why on earth would it have such a strange name?”

2.

The flat terrain made it understandable why one might liken it to Mount Fuji. As Inako gazed at the mountain—so earnest in its attempt to appear grand, like a child standing on tiptoe—she suddenly found it oddly amusing. “Well now, Ōmi’s Mount Fuji is awfully cute.”

Seeing her smile, Kihachi teased, “Want me to tell you another name for Ōmi-Fuji?” His tone was mischievous. “They also call it… *Mukadeyama*—the Centipede Mountain.”

*Mukade*. The mere three syllables sent a shiver of cold dread coursing through her entire body. Inako trembled involuntarily. “W-why on earth would it have such a strange name?”

Kihachi twisted his mouth into a sly grin and wiggled his fingers like centipede legs.

“There’s a legend that says a giant centipede once lived on that mountain—long enough to coil around it seven and a half times.”

Seven and a half coils. Just imagining a monstrous centipede wrapped around the mountain made Inako feel dizzy.

“Aw, don’t look so scared. Seven and a half coils ain’t all that long,” Kihachi said, mimicking the gesture of wrapping a *hachimaki* band around his head.

“It’s… i-it’s still a bit short for a proper coiling,” Inako stammered, narrowing her eyes sharply at him. “You’re just saying stupid things again,” she muttered, giving his shoulder a light punch.

On the tranquil lake surface, small boats with tall white sails glided lazily alongside steamships belching black smoke. Lost in the scenery, Inako was startled when Kihachi spoke up.

“Hey,” he said. “We’ve come this far already—wanna go explore around inside Mii-dera?”

Indeed, the temple housed numerous halls large and small, including the Kannon-dō. To pass them by would be a waste. Inako, who had secretly hoped to visit, readily agreed—a rare moment of appreciation for Kihachi’s surprisingly thoughtful suggestion.

They toured the Kannon Hall and the Three-Storied Pagoda before entering the Reishō-dō. Inside stood a massive *bonshō* bell, taller than a full-grown man. Its thick body was so broad that even if both of them threw their arms around it, they couldn’t have encircled it.

“You remember I told you about the giant centipede of Ōmi-Fuji earlier? There’s a legend that says the warrior Tawara no Tōta slew that great centipede, and as thanks, the local dragon god gave him this very bell.”

“This bell’s got scratches all over it when you look closely,” Inako murmured.

2.

They toured the Kannon Hall and the Three-Storied Pagoda before entering the Reishō-dō. Inside stood a massive *bonshō* bell, taller than a full-grown man. Its thick body was so broad that even if both of them stretched their arms around it, they couldn’t have encircled it.  

“You remember I told you about the giant centipede of Ōmi-Fuji earlier? There’s a legend that says the warrior Tawara no Tōta slew that great centipede, and as thanks, the local dragon god gave him this very bell.”  

“This bell’s got scratches all over it when you look closely,” Inako murmured.  

“That’s said to be the marks left by Benkei dragging it.”  

“Benkei—do you mean Yoshitsune’s retainer?”  

“When Benkei was still a monk at Enryaku-ji, during a conflict between Enryaku-ji and Mii-dera, he apparently seized this very bell and dragged it up the mountain. Later, the bell was returned unharmed, and since then it’s been called ‘Benkei’s Dragged Bell.’”  

Inako studied the *bonshō* again. Could there really have been a person capable of dragging something like this?  

“Ah… maybe Roku-san could do it.”  

　　　　　　　　　　＊  

A single *sosui* boat made its way upstream along the Biwa Lake Aqueduct, pulled by a boatman. On board were two men returning from peddling goods in Kyoto, idly puffing on Golden Bat cigarettes.  

“They say we won the war with Russia, but our lives haven’t gotten any better.”  

“If only we’d get some of that赔偿金… Instead, all we hear about is Karafuto and Mantetsu—stuff that doesn’t concern us at all.”  

“But I once saw Russian prisoners of war in Ōtsu—they were huge, built like Musashibō Benkei. Just the fact we beat men like that might be something to celebrate.”  

“If they’re Russians, maybe they could haul Mii-dera’s Benkei Dragged Bell without breaking a sweat.”  

“What nonsense. Now come on—that’s pushing it, even for you.”  

Just as their conversation reached a pause, the two noticed the boat was rocking oddly, in short, jerky motions. It seemed the large man seated ahead of them was fidgeting restlessly, swaying his body.

2.

“Hey, you up front—somethin’ wrong? You feelin’ sick or what?”

The large man in the black shirt—who’d been bouncing his leg—stopped abruptly and turned around sharply. His massive frame, which seemed to take up two seats, gave off an intimidating presence, and the two men immediately stiffened.

“No, not at all. I just have a rather urgent matter, and the boat is moving slower than expected. I suppose I grew a bit impatient.”

His manner of speaking was surprisingly gentle for someone so huge, and inwardly, they both relaxed.

“Well, that’s how it is when you’re goin’ upstream. Some say it’s faster just to walk.”

“Why don’t we give you a hand then? You’ve got the build for it.”

The big man nodded as if struck by a brilliant idea, then called out, “Boatman, let me pull!”—and with that, he leapt from the boat onto the boat-pulling path, shoved the protesting boatman back into the vessel, and took hold of the rope.

“He actually went and did it!”

“How can he think he can haul a *sosui* boat like this, no matter how big he is? It ain’t something you pick up overnight.”

Just as they began mocking him, a sudden, crushing pressure—like a sumo wrestler’s slap—slammed into them from the front. Everyone on board was thrown backward. The two scrambled to grab the gunwale, stunned by the torrents of water now spraying violently from both sides of the boat.

“Did we accidentally get on one of the fair’s water slides?”

Someone shouted. Pulled by the giant man, the boat surged forward at an impossible speed upstream along the aqueduct, far too fast for any normal *sosui* craft. The passengers clung to their seats, trembling in terror.

“If this guy can do *that*, draggin’ Benkei’s Bell would be a morning snack!”

“No doubt about it!”

---

2.

The giant man panted like a locomotive, sprinting along the aqueduct. This boat, racing upstream at a speed surpassing even descending *sosui* boats, was sighted in various places, becoming local legend—people would say, “A steamboat ran along the aqueduct!”

　　　　　　　　　　＊

Having finished their tour of Mii-dera, the two settled in a scenic spot by Kannon-dō and indulged in Benkei’s Strength Rice Cakes—square rice cakes generously coated with kinako.  
Inako was biting into a skewered cake, kinako clinging to the corner of her mouth.  
“Kinako…” Roku suppressed a smile.  
“What’d you say?” she shot back, glaring.  
“Still… are we really okay just lazing around like this?”  
“Mr. Roku? Even if someone were chasing us, they couldn’t catch up this fast.”  
“But still…” Inako wiped the kinako from her lips, lowering her eyes anxiously.  
“The warrior’s boat at Yabase may be swift, but haste makes waste—the long bridge at Setagawa.”

Sakamoto Kihachi suddenly recited the line, causing Inako to widen her eyes in surprise.  
“You know the name of the Setagawa Bridge, don’t you, Inago?”

The waters of Lake Biwa flowed out from its southern tip via the Setagawa River, changing names to Ujigawa and then Yodogawa before reaching Osaka Bay. The bridge spanning the Setagawa was the famed Setagawa Bridge, long known as a strategic crossing for both travel and battle.

The bridge spanning the Setagawa is the famed Setagawa Bridge, long known as a strategic crossing for both travel and battle.”

“This bridge,” he continued, “is apparently where the saying *‘If you hurry, go the long way round’* originated. In the old days, boats would cancel trips over the slightest issue, so it was actually faster to take the land route south, make a detour, and cross唐橋 than to try crossing Lake Biwa by boat. Though now, with the train running, the land route isn’t such a long detour anymore.”

Having finished his Benkei’s Strength Rice Cake, Kihachi stretched mightily.

“Isn’t your house somewhere around there, Sakamoto-san?”

“…No, it’s the opposite direction.”

“You’re still hesitating about going home, aren’t you?”

Kihachi stiffened, and Inako narrowed her eyes at him knowingly. “That’s what I thought.”

“I figured you were being unusually generous offering to guide us to Mii-dera…”

“It can’t be helped. I bolted out on my own—how am I supposed to face them now, after all this time?”

“Why not bring Benkei’s Strength Rice Cake as a gift?”

“That’s not something you can fix with sweets.”

“Well, maybe you don’t have to go back if you don’t want to,” Inako said, glancing toward Mikami-yama.

“…Still, though—what Mother mentioned about Mount Fuji. Could she really have meant Mikami-yama?”

Kihachi tilted his head. “Huh? I’m pretty sure she said, ‘You can see the *real* Mount Fuji,’ didn’t she?”

“Then it’s not Mikami-yama? What other Mount Fuji could there possibly be around here…?”

Dangling the skewer between his fingertips, Kihachi suddenly gasped.

“Oh, that’s right—I remember now. My older brother once said, ‘From Ise, you can see Mount Fuji.’”  

2.

“From Ise? That must be it!”

While Inako grew excited, Kihachi calmly shot her down: “Nah, that’s probably not right.”

“There’s no way you can see Mount Fuji from Ise. How far do you think it is?”

“So you’re saying my Okaa-san lied?”

Inako bristled, narrowing her brows. Kihachi exaggeratedly shrugged.

“Don’t go believing everything my brother says. You’ll end up getting burned.”

“Okaa-san doesn’t lie. I’m sure you *can* see Mount Fuji from Ise.”

“Oh? So *now* you say that,” Kihachi sneered, curling his lip as he stood up.  
“Well then—once we finish up this business with the *Denki Mokuroku*, let’s go to Ise and check for ourselves.”

Just as fiercely, Inako rose to her feet, puffing up defiantly.

“Fine! Let’s go!”  

As the two sparked against each other—

“Where exactly are you going?”

A low voice cut into their conversation. Startled, they whirled around to find Roku standing behind them, his face twisted in fury. His black shirt was soaked through, steam rising from his sweat-drenched body.

In an instant, Kihachi was seized by the collar, his feet flailing helplessly in midair.

“You damn little rat—finally caught you.”

“Roku-san… how did you—”

“I made inquiries at key places. A pair traveling together—especially Inako-kun, with that distinctive haircut—is hard to miss.”

Realization struck Inako; she touched her shortened hair. Roku glared at the limp form of Kihachi.

“You two heading for Ōtsu? I’ve already sent a telegram to Hyakugawa Shuzō about it. I’m taking you back to Fushimi, and you’re going to properly apologize to Jinbei’emon-san.”

Evening fell. When they arrived at Ōtsu’s Banba Station, Roku’s eyes widened at the sight of two men waiting in front of the station.  
“Jinbei’emon-san, you came all the way out here?”  
“I had business at your shop, so I came by while I was at it.”  
Beside Jinbei’emon, who kept his Kaiser-style mustache sharply pointed, stood Yōsuke, lazily fanning his face with a *uchiwa*.  
“Since Hyakugawa-san was coming to Ōtsu, I thought it’d be fun, so I tagged along.”  
Kihachi stared at Yōsuke’s carefree expression and couldn’t help but wonder: *Doesn’t the branch manager have anything better to do?* Seeing Kihachi’s look, Jinbei’emon trembled, gripping his cane tightly.  
“So this is the man who lured away that foolish girl?”  
Then, turning sharply toward Inako, Jinbei’emon raised his cane without hesitation.  
But immediately, Roku stepped between them, pleading, “There are people watching. Please, not here.”  
“Hyakugawa-san, I know a decent place nearby. We shouldn’t linger out in the open like this. Why not deliver your reprimand over a proper meal?”  
Jinbei’emon shot sharp glances back and forth between Yōsuke and Roku, then lowered his cane.  
“All right.”  

They walked up toward the hills. Deep within a secluded grove, where trees grew thick and dark, stood a restaurant called *Shokusanjin*.  

2.

As night deepened, only the dim glow of the *andon* lanterns lit the room where Kihachi, Jinbei’emon, and Roku sat around a low table. The faint light cast a damp, oppressive stillness over the chamber.

Inako was now alone with Yōsuke in another room. She longed to escape as soon as possible, but it seemed impossible so long as Roku was present. When Jinbei’emon set down his sake cup, he gave a slight bow toward Roku.

“My apologies, Roku-kun. I know you have your own business to attend to, yet here you are helping us with everything.”

“No, please don’t trouble yourself. My brother mainly runs the shop, so I’m free to do as I please. Besides, ever since returning from the war, I’ve been treated with such consideration. I’m living freely on my pension.”

“The Golden Cockscomb Medal. Truly, only someone who made his name thunder across battlefields as ‘Roku-Jōki’ could deserve such honor. I’ve heard you’ve been using that pension to support every veterans’ home and Red Cross facility you can find.”

“Should I perhaps refrain from that?”

“No, not at all. Rushing to aid those in hardship is itself a righteous cause—just continue as you are. And as for your marriage to Noriko, wait until your heart has settled. There are wounds from war that aren’t visible.”

Jinbei’emon drained his cup.

“But this,” he said, “don’t ever go saving those who take sweet bean cakes handed to them from others’ shelves as their due.”

“Ah,” Roku replied vaguely, nodding.

Jinbei’emon moved to pour him more sake, but Roku gently waved his hand in refusal.

“Oh, that’s right—you’re assisting the Salvation Army now. I remember you have a rule against drinking.”

Hahahaha,” Jinbei’emon chuckled, his shoulders shaking—but his eyes remained cold.

Inside the muggy chamber, the dim light of the *andon* lantern cast shadows across Jinbei’emon’s weathered face. Kihachi swallowed hard. If there truly was judgment before Enma after death, surely it would feel like this.

“It’s about time you told me your name, wouldn’t you say?”

　　　　　　　　　　＊

“The main branch of Mitsue is in Matsusaka, you see—but our ancestors originally came from Ōmi Province.”

In the empty sitting room, Yōsuke had already begun speaking freely, pouring himself another cup of sake.

According to Yōsuke, Ōmi had long been fertile land, but only the Setagawa River carried away the vast waters of Lake Biwa—and it frequently caused devastating floods, swallowing entire villages along its banks.

2.

Especially the flood eleven years ago was one of the largest in recorded history. Centered on farms whose rice crops had been completely wiped out before harvest, coastal villages suffered catastrophic damage—so severe that some people decided then and there to emigrate overseas.

Yōsuke’s relatives, whose business had been disrupted by the flood, resolved to move to Canada. At the time, Yōsuke was a young man, and due to family decisions, he left for Canada with his younger sister, accompanying the relatives to gain commercial experience and broaden his horizons.

In those days, traveling abroad was not particularly rare; many Japanese were migrating to Hawaii or California. Around the time of the flood, entering Hawaii required a substantial sum of one hundred yen, so most emigrants from Shiga Prefecture chose instead to settle in areas like Vancouver, Canada.

Unlike other migrants who worked in forestry or fishing, Yōsuke’s relatives had a certain amount of capital. They opened a shop in Vancouver selling goods imported from Japan: chirimen crepe silk, hemp cloth, bamboo-root whip (*takeyukibuchi*) crafts, kuzu crafts, and sake.

Like many Japanese laborers who went abroad hoping “to return home in glory,” the relatives eventually earned significant profits. After entrusting their store to an acquaintance, they returned once more to Ōmi. But Yōsuke did not return with them. Instead, determined to test his own mercantile talents, he boldly struck out into the wider world.

He would import Japanese goods popular among foreigners, sell them in the countries he visited, use the proceeds to purchase local alcoholic beverages, then ship and sell those in Japan. With the profits from that, he’d travel to another country, buy its local liquor, and repeat the cycle. For several years, he conducted this itinerant trade across the globe—and by the time he returned to Japan, he had amassed a considerable fortune.

2.

Once he finished speaking, Yōsuke downed in one gulp the sake poured into his small sake cup.

“The sake here is from Hyakugawa-san. And it’s delicious, too. I’ve always liked Hyakugawa’s sake—this sharp, dry flavor is irresistible.”

“Ah,” Inako murmured vaguely, nodding.

“Make sure you listen well to what I’ve said—and to my preferences. After all, you’ll soon be one of Mitsue’s people.”

Faced with this reality thrust upon her anew, Inako felt her stomach clench.

“Oh, that’s right—I had promised to have the *Denki Mokuroku* delivered before the wedding ceremony. Though I must admit, I laughed when that boastful fellow was so easily caught.”

“If you let Sakamoto-san escape from here, I can deliver the *Denki Mokuroku* to you.”

“That’s not a bad idea,” Yōsuke said, resting a hand on his chin. “If I keep Inako-chan right here beside me, he might come rushing to deliver the *Denki Mokuroku* in desperation.”

“…Why do you care so much about the *Denki Mokuroku*, Yōsuke-san?”

He paused, the sake cup halfway to his lips, and snorted derisively through his nose.

“The twentieth entry in the *Denki Mokuroku*. It contains information about a certain hidden sake.”

“A hidden sake?” Inako asked, incredulous. What on earth could be the connection between electricity and sake?

“A story I heard from the previous branch manager when I took over the position. Apparently, it’s a sake brewed using cutting-edge techniques rivaling even the national brewing research institute. …And the brewery that created this hidden sake is none other than Hyakugawa Shuzō.”

Inako was stunned—and suddenly recalled the conversation she had once overheard at home between Jinbei’emon and Yōsuke.

But never mind that, Yōsuke fumbled drunkenly into his pocket and pulled out a ring.  

“Look here—this ring uses pearls cultivated right here in Mie Prefecture, only a few years ago. In the West, there’s a custom of giving a ring to one’s betrothed. I thought I’d give it to you at our upcoming wedding ceremony. I don’t understand the value of jewels myself, but women do love their shiny things, don’t they?”  

Held aloft, the ring bore a perfectly round, lustrous pearl.

As Yōsuke stepped closer, Inako reluctantly leaned in to look—when suddenly, he grabbed her in an embrace.  

Pressing his nose to the nape of her neck, Yōsuke inhaled deeply.  

“Ahh… now this is the fragrance of genuine, natural perspiration. The raw essence has fermented beautifully.”  

A chill ran through her body as if her very life force had been siphoned away with the air. Her limbs trembled. Overpowered by the sharp stench of cologne, she felt her nose might fall off—while Yōsuke, without restraint, ran his hands over her back.  

“A taut, glossy skin, lean as a mackerel with not an ounce of wasted flesh… You know, I’ve always believed the most beautiful part of a woman’s body is her back. The moment I saw you, I knew—you embody the perfect aesthetic of the mackerel. …But now that I’ve touched you, I see you’re still too young.”  

2.

Even though it was summer, Inako’s teeth chattered uncontrollably. Still, she somehow managed to speak.  
“So… is that why you chose me as your bride? Just… just because of that…?”  

“That’s right. What other value could I possibly find in you?”  

Her nape, where her voice caught in her throat, was traced by Yōsuke’s fingertips, crawling like a centipede.  

“Your nape reminds me of a green peach—so fresh, so dewy. Irresistible. I thought your hairstyle was ridiculous, but now I see it’s rather alluring. Ah… I can already imagine running my tongue from this nape down along your back.”  

Gritting her teeth, Inako cried, “Let go of me!” and tried to push Yōsuke away. But no matter how hard she struggled, he wouldn’t release her. The terror of being unable to resist tightened her body into rigidity.  

“Honestly, with these modern women’s liberation movements—women have no real strength to rival men, yet they raise their voices so loudly. The most rational way of life for a woman is to quietly stay at home and devote herself to her man.”  

Overcome by the helpless fear and bitter frustration of being powerless against such force, tears streamed down Inako’s cheeks.  

“What the hell are you doing?!”  

Simultaneously with the sound of the sliding door bursting open, Roku’s voice rang out in fury. He seized Yōsuke by the collar, yanking him away from Inako, then twisted him upward with a single arm. Yōsuke’s face turned ghastly pale as his feet flailed helplessly in the air.  

“W-wait, calm down, please!”  

“Do you think this is a situation where I can stay calm?!”  

2.

“Is… is it really alright,” Yōsuke stammered, “to keep treating me like this? You and Noriko aren’t getting along, are you? And Miss Hyakugawa doesn’t seem too pleased about it either, deep down.”

“What of it?” Roku shot back.

“Oh, nothing in particular,” Yōsuke replied with feigned nonchalance. “Just that a single word from me might be enough to make the wedding talk disappear, that’s all.”

“What did you say—?!” Roku roared, his fury surging.

Tears streaming, Inako tugged desperately at Roku’s trousers. “Roku-san… that’s… that’s enough…”

“However,” Roku growled, hesitating for a moment under Inako’s pleading, but eventually yielding to it. With a grinding of teeth, he finally released Yōsuke. Yōsuke scrambled to his feet, straightening his clothes, then composed himself with an infuriatingly calm expression.

“Your head’s not as sharp as Inako’s, that’s for sure. Honestly, I’ve no taste for striking children. You ought to learn to take a joke more graciously.”

He flicked Roku sharply on the temple with his finger.

“How dare you speak like that—after making a girl cry who can’t even defend herself?!”

“Roku-san, it’s fine…” Inako whispered, still seated and sobbing quietly. “This centipede man… divine retribution will catch up to him sooner or later.”

Yōsuke glanced down at her, laughing with genuine contempt. “Divine retribution? What’s the point of groveling before gods who don’t even exist? Humans end at death—nothing more. What’s wrong with living however you please?”

He shot Roku one last glare, then immediately softened his features and reached for the sliding door.

“Well then, I’ll take my leave. Inako, make sure you get home safely. …And by the way, be sure to tell *him* about ‘that thing’ I mentioned.”
