Blinded by the sudden glare, Roku staggered with a low groan.

Seizing the moment, Kihachi snatched Inako’s wrist and bolted from the ryōtei.

They plunged along a dim forest path, utterly dark except for the beam of the flashlight, which flickered eerily across trees swaying in the wind. Kihachi’s breath came ragged ahead of her; his grip on her wrist was slick with sweat.

“Sakamoto-san, we should be safe by now… I’m gettin’ worn out…”

“We can’t… Not until we reach somewhere with light… Not before the torch dies…”

He slurred his words, tongue heavy, never once taking his eyes from the path ahead lit by the trembling beam.

2.

Groan. Seizing the moment, Kihachi snatched Inako’s wrist and bolted from the ryōtei. They plunged along a dim forest path, utterly dark except for the beam of the flashlight, which flickered eerily across trees swaying in the wind. Kihachi’s breath came ragged ahead of her; his grip on her wrist was slick with sweat. “Sakamoto-san, we should be safe by now… I’m gettin’ worn out…” “We can’t… Not until we reach somewhere with light… Not before the torch dies…” He slurred his words, tongue heavy, never once taking his eyes from the path ahead lit by the trembling beam.

But then—*crack!*—the flashlight suddenly emitted a popping sound and the surroundings were plunged into total darkness.

He’d said it was an old model. Probably another malfunction.

“There’s just a bit more to go until we reach a town with lights. Let’s walk slowly, Sakamoto-san.”

The darkness was undeniably unnerving, but having come this far, surely they’d be fine now. Fortunately, her eyes had begun to adjust.

“Sakamoto-san…?”

But Kihachi was frantically turning his head in all directions, his breathing growing even rougher.

“No light… There’s no light…”

The flashlight slipped from Kihachi’s hand and fell to the ground. The grip on Inako’s wrist slid away.

“There’s… no light…” he gasped in a broken voice, collapsing onto both knees where he stood.

Inako shook his shoulder, but his limp body went slack like a squid’s, boneless and soft.

“Sakamoto-san, snap out of it!”

She looped his arm over her shoulder and braced herself. Through her back she felt the heat radiating from his body and the violent pounding of his heart.

“We’re supposed to escape together, right?”

A limp human body was unexpectedly heavy. Even as she pushed forward, her feet only scraped against gravel without making progress.

“You said it yourself—we’re going to Ise-san to see if we can spot Mount Fuji, remember?”

Still, she dragged him forward with every ounce of strength. Inako’s breath grew ragged too, sweat bursting from her forehead.

Her foot slipped. She let out a small cry and collapsed onto the ground. Kihachi’s heavy body fell atop her, pinning her down so she couldn’t rise again. Gritting her teeth, Inako stretched out her arms and began crawling across the earth.

2.

A limp human body was unexpectedly heavy. Even as she pushed forward, her feet only scraped against gravel without making progress. “You said it yourself—we’re going to Ise-san to see if we can spot Mount Fuji, remember?” Still, she dragged him forward with every ounce of strength. Inako’s breath grew ragged too, sweat bursting from her forehead. Her foot slipped. She let out a small cry and collapsed onto the ground. Kihachi’s heavy body fell atop her, pinning her down so she couldn’t rise again. Gritting her teeth, Inako stretched out her arms and began crawling across the earth.

“Your eyes… you’ll light them up for me again, won’t you, Kihachi-san?!”

Inako too had reached her limit, collapsing under Kihachi’s weight, unable to move. Just as despair began to take hold, laughter and the footsteps of several people approached from ahead.

Soon, the lantern of the leading man illuminated Inako’s face—and immediately, cries erupted: “Hey, what’s wrong?” “There’s a kid collapsed here!” One of them suddenly exclaimed, “Isn’t this child Kihachi-kun?”

“Enki-san, it’s Kihachi-kun!”

Pushing through the group, a black-robed monk appeared. Looking down at the two figures sprawled on the ground, the man—his face stern and unyielding like stone—spoke in a solemn voice: “Kihachi.”

　　　　　　　　　　Five

Total darkness, where nothing can be seen, is terrifying.

Unlit hondō, attic spaces, the entrance to a pitch-black tunnel, telegraph poles lining a night road—

Ever since childhood, such things had unnerved her without reason; they filled her with pure, wordless fear.

“—Hachi… Kihachi… are you all right?”

When he came to, he saw the familiar ceiling above him, its wood grain resembling a turtle’s shell. Seiroku was peering down at him as he lay limp in his futon in his own room, morning sunlight filtering through the shoji.

“You passed out again,” Seiroku said, as if it were someone else’s problem. Irritated—*as if you don’t know whose fault this is*—Kihachi shoved aside the damp hand towel on his forehead and turned his gaze to the extinguished oil lamp.

2.

Her irrational dread had taken root—her aversion to darkness, once merely discomfort, had intensified since the fair into something so severe it could make her faint. She remembered last night, accidentally extinguishing the lamp: instantly, the memory surged—of being left behind in the fairgrounds at night, the crowd writhing like shadows, the black exhibition halls standing eerily still—and in that moment, her breath had seized, her heartbeat pounding painfully in her chest.

Kihachi lay there, a dull weight pressing against his skull, staring at Seiroku’s closely shaven head—the smooth, round pate he knew so well.

It was February of Meiji 37 (1904), half a year after the World’s Fair had closed. War with Russia had finally broken out over Korea and Manchuria. Since December the previous year, Seiroku had been enlisted in the 9th Regiment stationed in Ōtsu; mobilization orders could come at any time. Yet lately, on his rare days off, he’d been seen idling around town—some said they’d spotted him lingering at friends’ homes in Ōtsu, or loitering about magic lantern shops.

Seeing him back home after so long, Kihachi regarded him with exasperation, then glanced at the flashlight sitting beside his pillow.

“This damn thing hasn’t been any help at all, even at night.”

“That one from Santakurō?”

The flashlight had been given to him in December by a man named Santakurō, someone Seiroku knew. Not handed over in person, though—when Kihachi woke up that morning, it had simply been there, placed silently by his pillow during the night.

According to Seiroku, Santakurō made it his eccentric livelihood to deliver gifts to children every December. But he disliked speaking with people, so he always slipped the presents quietly beneath their pillows while they slept.

2.

This flashlight he’d received from Santakurō—its crucial battery was missing, rendering it a useless burden.

“I’d have preferred the *Denki Mokuroku* over this junk.”

It was an indirect jab at Seiroku: “Hand over the catalog you’re holding onto.” But Seiroku didn’t seem to take it to heart. “Well then, perfect timing,” he said with easy, guileless laughter.

“I’ve got good news. This December, Santakurō’s coming again. And this time… the gift he’s bringing is none other than the *Denki Mokuroku*—exactly what you’ve been waiting for.”

It felt like being kicked in the chest by muddy Western shoes.

“Lies, all lies!”

At Kihachi’s outburst, Seiroku hesitated slightly before scratching his head and chuckling.

“It’s not a lie. He really *is* coming. Don’t you trust me?”

“I don’t trust you!”

Seiroku looked genuinely taken aback by Kihachi’s immediate retort.

“It’s always been like this… Back when you said there was a giant centipede on Mikami-yama and dragged me up there, there wasn’t a thing—and I nearly got lost in the mountains. When you claimed some lake spirit lived in Lake Biwa and made me get into that boat, the hull had a hole in it, and I almost drowned. Every time I believed you, brother, I ended up suffering.”

After a brief silence, Seiroku spoke again.

“Well then… if Santakurō brings the *Denki Mokuroku*, will you finally believe me?”

“…Fine. I’ll believe you,” Kihachi shot back, glaring at Seiroku through tear-filled eyes.

“If you can do it, go ahead and prove it. Though I already know it’ll just be another lie.”

2.

My chest ached, but Kihachi forced the words out.  
“Is it really that unbearable for you to inherit the temple? You’d even take the *Denki Mokuroku* away from me?”  
“Now, now, what’s all this suddenly?”  
“Forget about electrical studies, forget middle school. Just like you want, after elementary school, I’ll work at the temple.”  

He slipped past Seiroku and left the room. Stepping into the earthen floor area, he felt the March chill. His mother, Kei, was washing dishes there. When she noticed him, she rushed over, relief spreading across her face.  
“Thank goodness—you’re awake.”  
“I was woken up by that brother of mine,” Kihachi said, his tone laced with sarcasm. To his surprise, Kei’s expression darkened.  

She crouched down until her eyes met his, placed both hands on his shoulders, and spoke soothingly.  
“…Stay close to him, just a little longer. I heard today that the 9th Regiment in Ōtsu has received mobilization orders.”  

Kihachi turned her words over and over in his mind, the weight of them sinking in.  
“Mobilization…? Is Brother going off to war?”  

He felt something cold and heavy settle deep in the pit of his stomach.  
Then, suddenly, Enki’s thunderous shout rang through the house:  
“What in the world are you doing?!”  

Startled, the two turned toward the sound.  

Inside the inner room, Seiroku stood on a footstool, closing the door of the *kamidana*’s forbidden shrine.  
That shrine—so often warned about, the one said to release the *bunrei* of Akubi Ōmyōjin if opened, bringing misfortune upon the household—Seiroku had opened it so casually.  

“It’s just… I couldn’t stand not knowing what was inside. I thought I’d take a look before going off to war.”  

2.

Kihachi stared, unable to process the reality before him. Seiroku flashed him a faint smile.

“I’ve just gone and seen the very god you trust more than electricity—face to face.”

Before Kihachi could grab him, Enki lunged and struck Seiroku down.

After pummeling Seiroku mercilessly, Enki finally calmed himself and turned toward the family gathered in the inner room.

“We’re going to Inari Shrine right away—tell Odai-san to perform a prayer. We must invite the deity back.”

Enki spoke with utter seriousness. Seiroku let out a dry, hollow laugh.

“Even if we do get the prayer done, how exactly would we know the god’s really come back? And besides, this *is* a temple. If you keep shouting about gods like this, you’ll scare off what few parishioners we have left.”

Ignoring Seiroku’s sarcasm, Enki turned bloodshot eyes toward Kihachi.

“Kihachi. You’re coming with me to Inari Shrine—right now.”

Kihachi froze in shock. If they left immediately, he wouldn’t be able to see Seiroku off when he returned to the barracks that evening.

“There’s no need for such farce. We’ve still got time before departure—why not go fishing at Lake Biwa instead?”

“Shut your mouth! Don’t you understand? Even a single day’s delay could bring unspeakable misfortune upon this household!”

Kihachi hesitated. This might well be the last day he ever spent with Seiroku.

But what if, by leaving the forbidden shrine unattended, divine retribution truly did strike?

What if that retribution took the form of Seiroku’s death?

2.

“Wait,” a dark voice whispered inside him. “Divine retribution from the gods isn’t necessarily limited to Seiroku’s death. Isn’t this misfortune something *he* himself sowed in the first place? After all he’s done, are you really going to forgive him so easily—and then go fishing together like nothing happened?”

It’ll be fine. As Enki said, if we redo the prayers properly, the gods will return and protect our household.

Kihachi steeled himself. “Alright. I’ll go with Father.”

He stood up, finished preparing, and had just slipped on his wooden clogs at the genkan when Seiroku called out from behind, “Hey, hey—today of all days, where do you think you’re going?”

Kihachi tried to keep walking, following Enki, but Seiroku grabbed his shoulder, forcing him to turn around reluctantly.

Seiroku grinned innocently.  
“Don’t cry if you trip along the way, okay?”

Still mocking me to the very end—

“Brother deserves to get hurt in war—teach him a lesson once and for all!”

Kihachi shook off his hand, turned his back on Seiroku’s unbroken smile, and walked toward Kyoto.

The prayer ritual at the Inari Shrine took half a day. By the time they returned to Ōtsu, Seiroku had already left.

As Enki clasped his hands before the forbidden shrine, wearing a look of deep contentment, “This should be enough. Seiroku will be safe now,” Bunshichi rushed into the inner room—his face unusually pale.

“Big brother! I heard Seiroku opened Akubi-sama’s shrine!”

“Ah. But we’ve already received purification from Odai-san, so the kami should have returned by now.”

2.

“…Is that so?” Bunshichi started to say something more, then closed his mouth. Though the kami should have safely returned, Kihachi still harbored a vague sense of unease.

In May, news arrived that Seiroku’s Second Army had captured Nanzen, a fortress on the Liaodong Peninsula. The village erupted in celebration, and Enki praised Seiroku’s valor.

Kihachi thought that when Seiroku returned, he’d grin proudly and say, “See? My prayers actually worked.” And if Seiroku sincerely apologized, Kihachi would promptly return the *Denki Mokuroku*, then follow him wherever he liked—fishing, or anywhere else. That was how Kihachi optimistically imagined things after Seiroku’s return.

So, even when official casualty reports arrived some time after the capture of Nanzen.

Even when Kihachi saw the name “Sakamoto Seiroku” listed among the dead.

At first, he thought it must be someone else—some stranger—and regarded it with detached indifference.

Only when he finally realized he knew no other Seiroku besides his brother did he begin to feel his body and mind dissolving into something insubstantial, unreal. In the end, he could no longer think at all.

Seiroku’s remains arrived from the battlefield. Hearing his mother’s weeping, seeing Bunshichi speak to the bones—“You’ve come back, Seiroku”—Kihachi still could not believe these fragments were Seiroku.

Seiroku was the one who, though slightly thin, had solid flesh on his bones, who grabbed Kihachi’s arm with his slender hands and dragged him everywhere, always grinning foolishly. These mere shards of bone could not possibly be Seiroku.

At Seiroku’s funeral, Enki, serving as chief mourner, spoke solemnly to the mourners.

2.

Seiroku had been a mischievous boy, an unruly child impossible to manage. But now, faced with the reality of his son’s death, I find myself suddenly remembering him as a beloved and admirable child."  

Kihachi heard Enki’s words from a distant corner of his consciousness.  

"Before departing for war, Seiroku committed the foolish act of opening the forbidden shrine of Akubi Ōmyōjin. In response, the deity imposed upon him the excessively severe punishment of death. I deeply regret that, before this came to pass, I failed to instruct Seiroku in the Buddha’s teachings or in the divine virtues of Akubi Ōmyōjin."  

What nonsense, a spark flared in Kihachi’s heart.  

Hadn’t they gone all the way to the sacred mound of Akubi Ōmyōjin at Inariyama, restored the deity, and brought divine presence back? Hadn’t they paid a hefty prayer fee, confidently declaring, “It’s all settled now”?  

As the congregation chanted the *Shōshinge*, the doctrinal hymn central to Zenshoku-ji’s sect, Kihachi drifted in a daze.  

Someone who’s never been to Paradise claims that chanting the Buddha’s name will take you there.  

They say that visiting a shrine brings divine favor, yet in the end, you must still strive on your own—gods will only nudge you forward. But if that’s the case, isn’t it the same whether gods exist or not?  

Offering *butsudan* rice or *shinsen* sacred food—no one actually eats it. Crafting a magnificent coffin brings no joy to the dead. How can anyone possibly know whether waving a great *ōnusa* purification wand truly brings divine grace?  

If gods and Buddhas truly exist, why don’t they just show themselves, even once?  

2.

Every emotion swelled to the breaking point, until finally something inside his head burst.

He stood, seized the incense burner beside him, and hurled it at the hondo. It shattered against the temple’s door, scattering ash across the entire floor. The chanting of the *Shōshinge* ceased, and Enki’s hair stood on end with fury.

“Kihachi! What in the world are you doing?!”

“If you’re really there, then come out!” Kihachi screamed toward the hondō.  
“If you truly exist, show yourself! I’ll beat you senseless!”

Kihachi bolted straight out of the main hall, fleeing through the village, then sprinting along the narrow field path.

Everyone’s a liar. Gods, Buddhas, and Seiroku too.

That bone claimed to be Seiroku—it’s a lie. It doesn’t resemble the brother he knew at all.

Then again—Kihachi suddenly stopped in his tracks.

If that bone isn’t Seiroku… and if there’s no Paradise waiting after death…

Then where, exactly, had Seiroku gone?

When Kihachi awoke, he saw the familiar ceiling above him, its grain resembling the shape of a turtle. *Ah,* he thought vaguely from under the futon, *I’ve come back.*

Kei peered down at him. “You’re awake.”

With an expressionless face, Kei ran a hand over Kihachi’s cheek and forehead. The hands, long worn from housework, were rough and cold. His *hishagami*-topped head looked even more gaunt than the last time they’d met.

“You didn’t eat yesterday. Breakfast is ready—come and have some.”  

---

2.

“It’s not Seiroku… and if there’s no Paradise waiting after death… then where, exactly, had Seiroku gone?” When Kihachi awoke, he saw the familiar ceiling above him, its grain resembling the shape of a turtle. *Ah,* he thought vaguely from under the futon, *I’ve come back.* Kei peered down at him. “You’re awake.” With an expressionless face, Kei ran a hand over Kihachi’s cheek and forehead. The hands, long worn from housework, were rough and cold. His *hishagami*-topped head looked even more gaunt than the last time they’d met. “You didn’t eat yesterday. Breakfast is ready—come and have some.”

Kei said this and briskly stepped out. Lying on his back, Kihachi realized he hadn’t found the right words for their reunion—after nearly three years apart. Then, suddenly, he grew anxious about Inako and immediately sprang up from the futon.

“Ah—”

Stepping into the wooden-floored room behind Kei, he locked eyes with Inako, who froze mid-chew, a piece of pickled vegetable still in her mouth.

Inako swallowed quickly, set her chopsticks neatly on her tray, and bowed awkwardly. “…I’ve come.”

“Ah, yeah,” Kihachi mumbled, smoothing down his bedhead as he sat beside her tray.

“Sorry about yesterday. Seems I caused you trouble.”

“We didn’t do anything. Someone nearby carried you here in a cart—it was Enki-san.”

Hearing his father’s name mentioned so abruptly, Kihachi felt a tightening in his stomach. Kei continued:

“Father happened to be passing by when he came back from the memorial service at the acupuncturist’s temple.”

Though surprised by Enki’s presence, Kihachi inwardly grumbled, *He could’ve just left me be.*

“And where’s Father now?”

The hearth room had only three trays laid out; there was no sign of Enki.

“He went out on business,” Kei said—and Kihachi breathed a quiet sigh of relief before picking up his chopsticks.

“I heard from Inako-chan yesterday—you came back looking for the *Denki Mokuroku*, right?”

Kihachi paused, his hand reaching for the soup bowl. Kei offered a faint smile.

“But before that… there’s something else we should take care of first.”

Prompted by Kei—“Go and pay your respects to Seiroku”—the two finished breakfast and set off for the graveside visit.

2.

The village where Kihachi was born lay halfway between northern Sakamoto Village and southern Ōtsu’s urban district, perched slightly uphill along the mountainside. From here, not only Lake Biwa but also the town of Kusatsu across the lake, its fields, and Mikami-yama could all be seen in the distance.

The weather looked uncertain, so Kihachi gripped an umbrella as he walked along the field paths. Inako followed a few steps behind, cradling funeral flowers in both arms, their fragrance occasionally wafting toward him on the breeze.

“I heard a lot from Kei-san yesterday—but I didn’t know my older brother had passed away.”

Seeing Inako grow downcast, Kihachi softened his expression. “I didn’t tell you, that’s all. Don’t worry about it.”

The graveyard stood at the edge of the village. On the Sakamoto family plot, wooden markers and stone stelae stood in rows for each generation, and nearest among them was Seiroku’s freshly erected grave marker.

“I’ve come. Now, hand over the *Denki Mokuroki*.”

Kihachi began rapping the umbrella against Seiroku’s grave marker as if striking a wooden mokugyo bell.

“Now, now—if you keep that up, your brother might come back as a ghost.”

“Good. If he’s around, let him show himself—I’ll beat him senseless.”

On the way here, Inako had recounted what had happened at yesterday’s ryōtei.

Yōsuke had said that entry twenty of the *Denki Mokuroku* contained information about the secret reserve sake of Hyakugawa Shuzō. The very idea that Hyakugawa could be connected to the *Denki Mokuroku* was completely out of the blue, and Kihachi still didn’t understand why Roku had reacted so violently at the mere mention of the *Denki Mokuroku*.

“Then explain yourself—come back as a ghost and clarify it,” Kihachi said, glaring at the grave while folding his hands in prayer.

“You’re still as disrespectful as ever.”  

2.

Inako placed flowers at the grave and closed her eyes, pressing her hands together in prayer. She remained that way for so long that Kihachi, left with nothing to do, turned his gaze toward the ashen surface of Lake Biwa beneath the overcast sky.

Below them, an old man wearing a *hokkamuri* cap labored in the fields, and occasional cool breezes softened the summer heat, carrying with them the calls of clam vendors and children’s laughter.

“…Now that I think of it, my older brother used to come up here often to look out over Lake Biwa,” Kihachi murmured quietly.

When Inako opened her eyes after finishing her prayers, she blinked rapidly in surprise.

“He liked high places. When he worked at the Buddhist goods shop, he apparently used to climb up to Akubi Ōmyōjin’s sacred mound all the time.”

“Huh? But Seiroku-san doesn’t even believe in gods, does he?”

“From what I heard, he didn’t go to pray—just to enjoy the view. Though that’s something my uncle told me.”

Inako glanced sideways at Lake Biwa, gently touching her hair as the wind tugged at it.

“Sakamoto-san, ever since you ran away from the temple, you’ve been living with your uncle at Hōrai Butsugu-ten, right?”

“Yeah, my uncle took me in. The shop probably didn’t make much money, but he still paid for my schooling. Thanks to him, I was able to attend middle school and keep studying electricity. …When I helped deliver items to Inari Shrines, I’d sometimes climb up to Akubi Ōmyōjin’s mound too, just staring off into the distance, wondering… ‘What exactly was my older brother seeing from here?’”

Only when he noticed Inako staring at him with wide, round eyes did Kihachi realize he had let himself sink too deeply into sentiment.

2.

“Well then, let’s hurry back and look for the catalog,” he muttered, quickly turning to leave the grave—only to lock eyes with someone approaching from the opposite direction. He froze in place.

The figure was draped in black robes, back straight and posture rigid, with a rock-solid, unforgettable face that bore an air of stern severity.

It was his father Enki—the first time he’d seen him in several years.

Under that piercing, sword-like gaze, Kihachi held his breath, bracing himself for a scolding.

But in an instant, Enki’s stern expression melted into crinkled warmth as he said with a chuckle, “So the little rascal’s come home.”

Stunned by this completely unexpected reaction, Kihachi stood dumbfounded, mouth agape.

“Inako-chan, thank you again for yesterday. Did you sleep well?”

“Yes. I’m truly grateful for letting me stay.”

As if entirely oblivious to Kihachi, Enki calmly exchanged greetings with Inako.

“By the way, Inako-chan, do you have any plans for today?”

Taken aback by the sudden question, Inako faltered: “For… today?”

“No? Thought so,” Enki declared, deciding for her, and seized both Inako’s and Kihachi’s arms, striding forward with long, determined steps.  
“You’re helping me with work now.”

——Had they been bewitched by a fox at an Inari Shrine?

Kihachi sat in seiza at the edge of the Buddhist altar room, surreptitiously spitting on his fingertip and dabbing it onto his eyebrow—a superstitious check to see if he was dreaming. Before the *butsudan*, Enki, clad in black robes and a five-striped *kesa*, struck the *hyōshigi* clappers and began chanting the *Hotoke Shō Amida Kyō*.  

2.

“Help me,” Enki had said—but ever since they’d arrived at this parishioner’s house for the memorial service, all Kihachi and Inako had done was carry a furoshiki bundle of Buddhist ritual implements. Now, crammed into the *butsudan* room, they endured the stifling heat of summer and the collective body warmth of the gathered crowd, reduced to standing stiffly in the corner beside Inako.  

When the *Amida Sutra* concluded, the bereaved family realized incense hadn’t been offered. Seeing Enki flash a sheepish grin and say, “Well then, let’s do it during the next *Shōshinge*,” Sakamoto Kihachi was stunned.  

Enki was strict—down to the type of candle used, down to the tiniest gesture. He’d once berated a parishioner so fiercely for placing prayer beads and sutras directly on the tatami that the man fled, terrified, never to return to Zenshoku-ji.  

Once the congregation had finished chanting the *Shōshinge* in unison, Enki turned to face them.  

“Ah, the gentleman we honored today with his third-year memorial—well, during his lifetime, I myself was greatly indebted to him. His family knows this well, I’m sure, but this man, let me tell you, loved his sake. If you offered just a single *sakazuki* cup of rice wine at his grave, you’d swear he’d pop right out and shout, ‘Not enough!’”  

Laughter rippled through the mourners, centered on the bereaved relatives, and the mood softened into warmth.  

“Now, every time I hold a service, I feel I must come up with some new story or another,” Enki continued, snapping his fan open and shut with a crisp *pat-pat* to mark the pause. “But even I, I must admit, am beginning to run out of material.”  

2.

“These days, even in a place like this, we’re beginning to hear the term ‘Twentieth Century’ more and more. With the advancement of machines and technology, it’s a world crowded with gods and buddhas—countless *yaoyorozu* deities, Amida Buddha, Jesus-sama… truly, the divine is packed in tight. Some faiths impose strict precepts and traditions, but fortunately for us, our teaching is wonderfully easy for priests: simply uttering ‘*Namu Amida Butsu*’ once guarantees rebirth in Paradise. A most convenient doctrine, wouldn’t you say?”

“If all it takes is chanting the nembutsu, then why bother with expensive funerals or memorial services?”

The man, who had been sitting listlessly up to now, spoke mockingly. Beside him sat a young girl, apparently his daughter, nervously clutching his sleeve.

“Hey—you! How dare you speak so rudely to the priest?” a family member snapped in rebuke. But the man, seemingly a relative, ignored him and retorted, “What difference does it make what we do for someone who’s already dead?”

“…The young girl seated beside you,” Enki said, his voice calm and still smiling, “how old is she this year?”

“She’ll be seven soon. What of it?”

“If your daughter were to suddenly die, would you just leave her body by the roadside?”

The girl stiffened at Enki’s words, her face frozen.

“How dare you say such a thing to another man’s child!” the man shouted, leaping to his feet in fury.

“Then answer me this,” Enki replied, unflinching. “If you truly believe the dead feel nothing, and that funerals and memorial rites are meaningless—then even for this beloved daughter, whom you now rage to protect, would you really leave her unburied? Would you truly be at peace knowing you’d done nothing? Can you honestly say you’d be satisfied?”

2.

“In a calm yet piercing voice, the man stammered, unable to reply.

‘No matter the religion, why do we mourn the dead? Of course, part of it is to send the deceased to the next world. But the greater reason is for the sake of those left behind. By carefully seeing off the departed, we who remain can finally come to terms with the loss of our beloved. It brings closure to the heart.’

With a sharp *snap* of his fan, the man quickly straightened his posture.

‘Some may think memorial services are nothing but priests profiting from grief. But tell me—how often in a year do all these relatives gather like this? The purpose isn’t truly about “honoring” the deceased. After all, the departed have already reached Paradise through Amida-sama’s grace.

Rather, it’s for us—the living—to confirm one another’s well-being, to remember the one who has passed, and to touch once more upon the bonds of faith. That, too, is what a memorial service means to me. Indeed, thanks to this gathering, I’ve learned that your daughter, whom I only knew as an infant, has grown safely to the age of seven.’

When Enki smiled gently, the man—who had been hunched in tension—slowly reached out and stroked his daughter’s head.

‘Feel free to call us monks deceivers if it helps. If cursing us relieves your pent-up resentment, then we serve a purpose. We call it *Kinyū Kudoku Daihōkai*—just as rivers eventually flow into the vast ocean, so too do the sorrows of ordinary beings, mere trickling streams, find acceptance within the wide, deep sea of Buddha. Do not grieve over confusion or fear. Let all such burdens drift away into the sea of Buddha.’


2.

And Enki smiled, his stony face creasing deeply with wrinkles.

“The ticket to Paradise is prepared for us by Amida-sama. All we can do is live our lives to the fullest, no matter how soon our fragile lives may end.”

Even after the memorial service ended, Enki continued chatting at length, surrounded by parishioners. In a corner of the room, Sakamoto Kihachi quietly munched on biscuits with Inako, observing this scene—one that would have been unthinkable in the old days.

When it was time to leave, as they were putting on their shoes at the genkan, the host lowered himself at the entrance, placing his hands on the floor in deep gratitude.

“Thank you so very much today.”

Then he turned and smiled at Kihachi.

“My, how you’ve grown, Kihachi-kun… By the way, Reverend Goen-san, I have a small matter to discuss.”

“Yes, what is it?”

“You’re probably aware that Shinya’s Kōemon-san is salmon fishing in Canada. Recently, he moved to a different port town somewhat farther away. There are apparently quite a few people from Ōmi Province living there, and they’re hoping to have a Buddhist priest stationed there—something like a branch of the Vancouver Buddhist Church. They’d like a monk to come.”

“Can’t the Vancouver Buddhist Church send someone?”

“Well, there are subtle differences between Zenshoku-ji and the Vancouver Buddhist Church, aren’t there? And Kōemon-san is a man of deep faith—he specifically expressed the wish for a priest from his hometown, if possible.”

“So you’re suggesting… that I send Kihachi-kun?”

“No, no—not at all. You have your duties at Zenshoku-ji, Reverend. I was wondering… what about Kihachi-kun himself?”

2.

Suddenly, the conversation turned toward him, and Kihachi was taken aback.  

“You can handle basic Buddhist rites, and you still remember the *Sanbukyō* and the *Shōshinge*, don’t you?”  

Though spoken casually, the words brought back memories of Enki’s stern instruction, stirring up bitter recollections.  

“If you don’t have anywhere to go after graduating middle school, how about going? Kōemon-san says he’d spare no effort in supporting you.”  

“I see. That sounds like a fine offer.”  

Enki placed a large hand on Kihachi’s head, as if to comfort him, and Kihachi tensed, bracing himself for what was to come.  

“But I won’t send my son. He’s studying electricity, after all.”  

Stunned, Kihachi stood frozen. The master, realizing his blunder, repeatedly bowed in apology. “No, no—forgive me, that was terribly rude of me.”  

“I’ll pass this along to a priest I know. If anyone expresses interest, I’ll let you know.”  

“Really? That would be a great help. Kōemon-san will be delighted.”  

Accepting a wrapped gift from the master as thanks for the day’s service, the three left the site of the memorial service.  

Under the falling rain, Kihachi held an umbrella and watched the two walking ahead of him. The sound of the rain muffled their voices, but occasionally Inako turned her face toward Enki with a soft chuckle. Each time he saw that expression, it felt as though Enki were digging into old wounds, so Kihachi averted his gaze from them and turned it instead toward Lake Biwa.  

The water lay dark gray, and Mikami-yama was hidden behind the rain. The ground beneath his feet was heavily mired, making walking difficult.  

Back at Zenshoku-ji, with Inako’s help, Kihachi began searching for the *Denki Mokuroku*.  

2.

Rain fell steadily as Kihachi held an umbrella and watched the two walking ahead of him. The sound of the rain muffled their voices, but occasionally Inako turned her face toward Enki with a soft chuckle. Each time he saw that expression, it felt as though Enki were digging into old wounds, so Kihachi averted his gaze from them and instead looked toward Lake Biwa. The water lay dark gray, and Mikami-yama was hidden behind the veil of rain. The ground beneath his feet was heavily mired, making walking difficult.

Back at Zenshoku-ji, with Inako’s help, Kihachi began searching for the *Denki Mokuroku*.  

They combed every corner—Seiroku’s chest of drawers, the storage closets—until finally they even checked places like the hondō and the back of the forbidden shrine. Yet by sunset, they still hadn’t found it.

Exhausted, the two slumped heavily into the inner room and listlessly gazed up at the kamidana.

“Nowhere at all,” said Inako. She clapped twice and folded her hands before the sacred mound of Akubi Ōmyōjin.

Somehow, she’d expected this. Knowing Seiroku, he wouldn’t hide something in a place easy to guess. Though the outcome was just as anticipated, seeing Inako’s solemn profile bowed in prayer filled Kihachi’s chest with restless unease.

“That shrine of Akubi Ōmyōjin has been enshrined here since my childhood,”  
a voice said behind them. Turning, they saw Enki standing there. Kihachi quickly looked away.

“My mother prayed for it when Bunshichi was born sickly—she asked Oda-i-san at the Inari Shrine to perform rituals so he might grow strong and healthy. That’s how we received the *bunrei* of Akubi Ōmyōjin.”

“Oh, is that so? Well, Mr. Bunshichi is doing well now—your mother’s wish truly came true!”

Inako clasped her hands reverently once more. Kihachi, inwardly surprised that Bunshichi had any connection to the forbidden shrine, felt a sudden thump on his back as Enki cheerfully slapped both him and Inako.

“Come now—don’t wear such gloomy faces! Time for dinner. Tonight’s a feast!”

2.

They moved into the room with the wooden floor, where a metal pot simmered over the *irori* hearth, bubbling with sukiyaki. The contents of the package given to them by the host after the memorial service turned out to be beef—rare fare for their household—and Kihachi’s cheeks naturally softened with delight.

“Sukiyaki with beef… Are we really allowed to have this?”

Beside him, Inako sat timidly, shrinking back, not daring to reach her chopsticks toward the pot.

“It’s thanks for helping me with the service and saving Kihachi. Don’t hold back—dig in.”

“Or is beef something you dislike, Inako-chan?” Kei asked, touching his hand to his cheek with concern.

“This one’s favorite food’s mice,” Kihachi said—then yelped as his side was pinched sharply.

“Well then, I’ll begin,” Inako said, reaching in, plucking a piece of meat, dipping it into the beaten egg, and slipping it into her mouth.

Instantly, her face brightened, and Enki and Kei both exhaled in relief. But without hesitation, Inako reached straight for a piece of meat that Kihachi had been eyeing secretly.

“That one’s still raw,” he warned.

“Oh, really?” she said, pausing mid-motion—just long enough for Kihachi to swiftly snatch the meat away.

“Hey! You tricked me!”

Kihachi shoved the morsel into his mouth, already coated in egg, and groaned with pleasure as the sweetness of sugar and soy-based broth-soaked meat mingled with the rich creaminess of raw egg, filling his mouth completely. “This… this is the taste of civilization!” he moaned.

He lunged for another piece—but this time, Inako called out, “That one’s raw too.”

Honestly, what a transparent woman.

“I’m not falling for that again. I’ll eat it anyway.”

He bit down—and immediately recoiled, gagging. “Ughhh!”

The overwhelming raw stench flooded his mouth. “You… you tricked me!”

2.

“Hey! You tricked me!” Kihachi shoved the morsel—already coated in egg—into his mouth, groaning with pleasure as the sweetness of the sugar and soy-based broth-soaked meat mingled with the rich creaminess of raw egg, completely filling his mouth. “This… this is the taste of civilization!” he moaned. He lunged for another piece—but this time, Inako called out, “That one’s raw too.” Honestly, what a transparent woman. “I’m not falling for that again. I’ll eat it anyway.” He bit down—and immediately recoiled, gagging. “Ughhh!” The overwhelming stench of raw meat flooded his mouth. “You… you tricked me!”

“It *was* raw, like I said. You’ve been eating nothing but meat since we started. No wonder divine retribution has caught up with you.”

“Don’t be disrespectful. Look here—I’m eating vegetables properly too, see?”

“There’s meat hidden underneath.”

Instantly seeing through the ruse, Kihachi clicked his tongue in annoyance.

“You’re supposed to be a proper young lady. Sukiyaki shouldn’t be some novelty to you. Can’t you leave us common folk our simple pleasures?”

“Our sukiyaki uses chicken. Haven’t had beef in ages.”

As their chopsticks clattered against each other over the pot like swordsmen locked in a duel, Enki and Kei burst into laughter at the same moment.

Kihachi suddenly felt embarrassed, while Inako bowed her head apologetically. “I’m sorry. That was terribly rude of me.”

“No need to apologize. It’s been a long time since things were this lively… And it’s been just as long since I set four rice bowls on the table.”

Kei’s voice carried a quiet melancholy, and for a moment, the room fell silent. Enki drained his small sake cup in one gulp.

“What kind of person was Seiroku-san, exactly?” Inako asked as she poured more sake for Enki, and Kei smiled wistfully.

“He was such a playful child—always cracking jokes, sneaking off behind his parents’ backs to run all over the place…”

Then, catching Kihachi’s eye, Kei suddenly let out a soft snort of laughter.

“And he used to talk about you all the time. When the first electric lamp lit up in Ōtsu, Seiroku took him to see it. Apparently, Kihachi saw people’s faces under that pale blue light and shouted, ‘They look like ghosts!’—and was terrified out of his wits.”

Kihachi nearly spat out the tofu he’d just put in his mouth.

“When little brat Kihachi got scared by a train whistle after Seiroku carried him piggyback to see the locomotive, he started howling like a baby…”  

2.

“The place…” Then, catching Kihachi’s eye, Kei suddenly let out a soft snort of laughter. “And he used to talk about you all the time. When the first electric lamp lit up in Ōtsu, Seiroku took him to see it. Apparently, Kihachi saw people’s faces under that pale blue light and shouted, ‘They look like ghosts!’—and was terrified out of his wits.” Kihachi nearly spat out the tofu he’d just put in his mouth. “When little brat Kihachi got scared by a train whistle after Seiroku carried him piggyback to see the locomotive, he started howling like a baby…”

“What on earth is this mother getting at now? What happened to Seiroku’s story?” Inako glanced over, feeling a cold sweat trickle down her back—and found herself met with a smug, triumphant gaze.

“Oh, that’s right—another thing Seiroku told me: when you two saw X-rays at the exhibition, Kihachi, you completely lost your nerve—”

“That’s enough! Don’t say another word!”

In the end, Kihachi was tormented as Inako relentlessly dug up one mortifying memory after another, all the while grinning mischievously.

When the pot ran dry, Kei slipped away into the kitchen to brew tea. Enki patted his swollen belly contentedly and puffed at his Shikishima cigarette with evident pleasure. “Paradise, paradise.” He looked utterly devoid of gratitude or reverence.

“…I wonder if, when you reach *Paradise*, you can eat sukiyaki with beef every single day…”

At Inako’s sudden, unprovoked mention of “Paradise,” Kihachi shivered.

“You never change. Do you seriously believe there’s some place like that waiting after death?”

Kihachi deliberately adopted a mocking tone.

“Of course there is—if *Goin-san* says so, then it must be true.”

“But it’s like a gatekeeper who’s never seen beyond the door telling you, ‘Beyond this gate lies a magnificent world.’ Anyone who believes such a suspicious claim is the one who’s truly deluded.”

In earlier days, Enki’s fist would have already come crashing down. But now, he simply smiled, puffing at his cigarette, watching the two of them exchange barbs. Inako leaned toward Enki, pressing close as if seeking shelter.

“Goin-san, of course *Paradise* exists, doesn’t it?” Her eyes shimmered with desperate hope.  
“After we die, we really can go to Amida-sama’s realm, can’t we?”

2.

“No, I don’t know about that.”

Zenshoku-ji’s expression froze at Enki’s blunt reply, the hope in her eyes vanishing completely.

“The so-called *Paradise* people talk about these days—well, it might exist, or it might not.”

“…Please, don’t joke like that.”

“Same as Kihachi. I’ve never seen it myself, so I have no right to carelessly say it exists.”

Inako’s voice trembled as she whispered, “Why…?”

“Why do you say such things, Your Reverence? Don’t you yourself believe in Paradise?”

“…There was a boy named Shōhei, a parishioner’s child—one of my students from the Night Study Meeting.”

The sudden mention of a former pupil caught Kihachi by surprise. The Shōhei family were also parishioners of Zenshoku-ji, so Enki knew them, but why bring up Shōhei now?

“Shōhei was a late-born child, dearly beloved by his parents. After you took the Night Study Meeting children to visit his grave, his parents came to the temple with downcast faces. ‘We’re glad our son had good friends,’ they said. ‘But because of that, we can’t help but worry—he must be so lonely in the afterlife, taken from us too soon.’ That’s what they confided in me. Without hesitation, I answered thus:”

—*Paradise is a land free of all suffering. Shōhei must surely be living happily there.*

“The very next day, the parents drowned themselves.”

For the first time, Enki’s normally cheerful face twisted with anguish and remorse.

2.

“If the Pure Land is truly such a wonderful place,” they wrote in their suicide note, “then the three of us—mother, father, and son—shall live together there.” …That was never my intention. I only spoke of Paradise to comfort those grieving parents, to ease their sorrow over losing their child too soon. But in truth, I did no different than telling them, ‘Die.’” For the first time, Enki’s face—normally so bright—twisted with anguish and guilt.

“…So that’s why you stopped believing in Amida?”

It was Kihachi who broke the heavy silence.

“You spent all this time preaching to us children about faith in Amida-sama, yet now you abandon it so easily? If you don’t believe in Paradise anymore, why remain a priest at all? You don’t believe in Buddha or salvation—you can’t possibly.”

“That’s not true. I cannot carelessly tell others, ‘Yes, it exists’—that would be irresponsible. But *I* still believe in Amida-sama and the Pure Land. Let me ask you this instead: Why can’t *you* believe in Buddha?”

“Because… I can’t see it. What I can’t see, I can’t believe in.”

“There’s a parable about a gatekeeper who’s never seen what lies beyond the door. True enough, we mortals cannot see the form of gods or Buddhas. But tell me this: Have *you* ever seen electricity—the force that powers machines? Have you seen radio waves or magnetism with your own eyes? Like the gatekeeper, you’ve never passed through that door either. So how can you say with certainty that nothing lies beyond?”

Kihachi could not reply.

Then Enki turned toward Inako, who sat in stunned silence, and said gently, “There’s wisdom in the old saying: *If you hurry, take the long bridge at Setta.* Rushing headlong along the land route, ignoring everything around you just to reach Paradise quickly—that path is neither efficient nor kind. Whether you take a shortcut or wander far out of your way, in the end, everyone arrives at the same destination.”

2.

An image came to Kihachi’s mind: people walking across the Tōhashi bridge, and leisurely round-hulled boats crossing Lake Biwa.

“Hey, Inako-chan,” Enki said with a gentle smile.  
“The boat journey may not let us know exactly when we’ll reach Paradise—but even if we’re swept off course by waves or caught in a storm, Amida-sama’s ship will surely carry us there in the end. Whether the gate we arrive at truly opens onto Paradise? That’s for us to discover once we’ve arrived. Let the steering be entrusted to Amida-sama. Instead, look forward to the rainbow after the storm, the breathtaking scenery along the way that you’ve never seen before—shouldn’t we just grow thick-skinned and enjoy this voyage with ease?”

Just as Inako opened her mouth, as if about to say something, a knock sounded at the front door.

“Who could it be at this hour?” Enki said, heading toward the entrance on behalf of Kei, who was still in the kitchen.

“…Could it be that Amida-sama is saying, ‘Since you’ll end up in Paradise anyway, just live however you like’?”

Kihachi flinched at Inako’s low, somber voice. She turned a tearful face toward him.  
“Dr. Paikaru used to say, ‘When you die, it’s over. So live however you want.’ But I don’t want to become like Dr. Paikaru. That kind of thinking invites divine retribution—it sends you straight to Hell.”

Her tone was one of stern self-reproach. Kihachi didn’t know what to say. Helplessly, he looked down at the flashlight he had pulled from his pocket.

Electricity itself is invisible, yet undeniably present. Then again—what of gods and Buddhas?

“That flashlight of yours—you take good care of it, don’t you?”

Kei approached then, carrying a tray with teacups. He gazed at Kihachi’s flashlight with a nostalgic expression.

2.

“Say, ‘When you die, it’s over. So live however you want.’ But I don’t want to become like Dr. Paikaru. That kind of thinking invites divine retribution—it sends you straight to Hell.” Her tone was one of stern self-reproach. Kihachi didn’t know what to say. Helplessly, he looked down at the flashlight he had pulled from his pocket. Electricity itself is invisible, yet undeniably present. Then again—what of gods and Buddhas?

“That flashlight of yours—you take good care of it, don’t you?” Kei approached then, carrying a tray with teacups. He gazed at Kihachi’s flashlight with a nostalgic expression.

“I used to wonder why you, of all people, had such an expensive thing. So once, I asked Seiroku: ‘Where on earth did you buy that *denki akari*?’”

“And what did Brother say?”

“‘Didn’t buy it. Got it from an acquaintance in Hachiman Town.’”

Hachiman was a rural town on the opposite shore of Lake Biwa, in the eastern lakeside region. A place long known for commerce, it had once hosted the commercial school Seiroku attended—though the school had since moved from Ōtsu to Hachiman.

“Maybe that acquaintance was Mr. Santakurō. If he’s the one who gave you this flashlight—”

The moment he spoke the name, it felt as if a circuit in his mind had suddenly closed.

“…Santakurō is the one who delivers the *Denki Mokuroku*.”

Kihachi let out a hoarse whisper. Inako and Kei both tilted their heads in unison.

“Brother said so before he left. That every December, an acquaintance of his would deliver the *Denki Mokuroku*.”

Since the *Denki Mokuroku* had never arrived that year, they’d assumed it was just another of his jokes.

“Maybe this Mr. Santakurō from Hachiman Town knows something about the *Denki Mokuroku*.”

Just as Kihachi and Inako exchanged startled glances at this unexpected clue—

A loud noise erupted from the front gate, accompanied by an angry shout.

“I’ve had enough! Sorry, but I’m coming in!”

At the familiar, low-pitched voice, the two, who had been looking at each other, froze in place.

“It’s… Father…” Inako whispered, her face draining of color—just as heavy, violent footsteps drew rapidly closer.

---

2.

Kei quickly stood and pulled Inako’s hand, dashing with her into the adjacent earthen-floored room, Kihachi following close behind.

Kei urged both to put on their clogs, then without waiting for reply, pushed them firmly out through the back door—his face calm, a resolute smile on his lips.

“Leave the rest to me and Father.”

“But—” Kihachi began to protest, only for Kei to cut him off with firm reassurance:  
“I’ll protect you both. Count on it.”

The back door shut, plunging them into darkness. Only a faint glow seeped through a small window; beyond a few steps, nothing was visible. Kihachi instinctively closed his eyes and leaned against the wall. He should’ve brought a lamp. His flashlight had burned out—the cheap bulb’s filament long dead.

His breath grew tight. Anxiety clawed at his chest. Then a small hand seized his arm.

“It’s okay. I’m here.”

Inako’s soft voice soaked into his body like warmth, and the heat of her small hand through the fabric of his kimono slowly loosened his tension.

“Are you still alright with your eyes closed?” she asked gently. Kihachi nodded shakily.

Her hand tightened around his sleeve.

“I’ll lead you.”

With steady, unwavering steps, Inako began walking. Pulled along, Kihachi stumbled forward awkwardly.

“I’ve got a firm hold. Just follow.”

Guided by Inako, they passed through the village and finally reached a narrow field path. With his eyes shut, ordinary sounds—the chorus of frogs, the wind brushing through rice stalks—now struck his ears with startling clarity.

---

2.

Though being led by the arm down a dark road, strangely, he felt no anxiety at all. Perhaps his heart had steadied and his senses sharpened—now he became aware of the rustle of fabric against fabric, the faint sound of breathing.

Each time Inako stepped lightly forward, a sweet scent—different from the lingering aroma of sukiyaki—floated toward him along with the smell of sweat and the texture of her kimono. Kihachi’s heart trembled, stirred as if shaken from within.

Of all things—to be relying on the very person he was supposed to protect, right after being told, “Make sure you keep her safe.”

Kihachi silently thanked the darkness for hiding this disgrace; it spared him the shame of being seen like this.

“Kihachi-san, should I still hold on?”

“…Yeah. Let’s keep going like this until we can see the lights of Ōtsu town.”

Inako gave a short “Mm,” then tightened her grip on his sleeve even more firmly than before.

　　　　　　　　　　＊

“It took longer than expected to confirm that the man who took Inako is the second son of this temple.”

Before the flickering candlelight in the main hall’s hondō, Enki and Jinbei’emon sat facing each other.

“Where have you hidden my daughter? Hand her over now.”

“I’m afraid I haven’t seen her. Is something the matter?”

“…Even when your own son is the criminal, you still deny it outright. That’s why I’ve always despised your sect. Spouting nonsense like ‘even evildoers can reach Paradise just by chanting the nembutsu.’ Only the righteous should be saved. People who cause trouble for others deserve nothing but hell.”

2.

“Confirm that the man who took Inako is the second son of this temple.” Before the flickering candlelight in the main hall’s hondō, Enki and Jinbei’emon sat facing each other. “Where have you hidden my daughter? Hand her over now.” “I’m afraid I haven’t seen her. Is something the matter?”

“…Even when your own son is the criminal, you still deny it outright. That’s why I’ve always despised your sect. Spouting nonsense like ‘even evildoers can reach Paradise just by chanting the nembutsu.’ Only the righteous should be saved. People who cause trouble for others deserve nothing but hell.”

Enki gently shook the cigarette pinched between his fingers and said, “What exactly is a ‘good person,’ I wonder?”

“In the past, rebirth in Paradise was a privilege reserved for those who could rely on their own efforts—those who practiced asceticism or made generous donations. The vast majority of people had no such means. They were sinners burdened with worries and desires, unable to reach the Pure Land despite longing for it. Do you know how many lives were saved simply by realizing that even they could attain salvation by chanting ‘Namu Amida Butsu’?”

Enki lowered his gaze to his outstretched hands.

“I myself was a good person. I was a foolishly virtuous man—one who never questioned whether my beliefs and actions, which I assumed were right, caused harm to others. …To my shame, it wasn’t until this age that I first carried Kihachi on my back.”

Recalling how much lighter Kihachi had felt than expected, Enki offered Jinbei’emon a faint smile.

“At last, I’ve become aware of my own arrogance—and so, I am happy. For I have become the kind of sinner whom Amida-sama seeks to save. And Mr. Hyakugawa—you, too, fortunately, are a sinner.”

“What utter nonsense—”

“You feel doubt about what you’re doing.”

For just an instant, the corner of Jinbei’emon’s mouth—a man whose expression remained fixed in anger—twitched.

“Is that all your preaching? Your second son here… his name was Kihachi, wasn’t it?”

Enki remained silent, his expression unchanged.

“My eldest would never stoop to such behavior—but I won’t let that boy get away with it either.”

2.

“At last, I’ve become aware of my own arrogance—and so, I am happy. For I have become the kind of sinner whom Amida-sama seeks to save. And Mr. Hyakugawa—you, too, fortunately, are a sinner.”  
“What utter nonsense—”  
“You feel doubt about what you’re doing.”  
For just an instant, the corner of Jinbei’emon’s mouth—a man whose expression remained fixed in anger—twitched.  
“Is that all your preaching? Your second son here… his name was Kihachi, wasn’t it?”  
Enki remained silent, his expression unchanged.  
“My eldest would never stoop to such behavior—but I won’t let that boy get away with it either.”

2.  
Kihachi and Inako reached the town of Ōtsu, then went to Yajiro Yamajirō’s Yagura Takeyukibuchi Craft Shop to explain their circumstances and were allowed to stay the night. At dawn, they thanked the Yajiro family and set off toward Banba Station.  

They bought tickets for Hachiman Station and took seats on the hard benches of a third-class carriage. The train departed Banba on schedule. As Kihachi sat uneasily amid the lingering scent of women’s hair oil and cigarette smoke inside the car, he stole a quick glance at his companion beside him.  

Inako, seated by the window, gazed curiously at the passing scenery. Her neck was faintly damp with sweat, and the morning sunlight made the fine hairs along her nape shimmer. Noticing Kihachi’s fixed stare, she turned and tilted her head slightly in puzzlement.  

Startled, Kihachi abruptly turned his eyes toward Lake Biwa and blurted, “Hey, did you know about the creature living in Lake Biwa?”  
“There’s a guardian spirit in the lake—some say it’s a catfish as big as a grown man. If it sees a child, it’ll swallow them whole in one gulp.”  
“Wh-what? You mean something that terrifying lives in Lake Biwa?”  
“Well, it’s deep enough to swallow Tōji’s five-storied pagoda whole—so who knows what might be down there?”  

Midway through his tale, Kihachi suddenly locked eyes with someone seated farther back in the car. For a moment, both stared at each other in dumbfounded silence—then the figure in the rear, Roku, widened his eyes, shot to his feet, and rushed toward them.  

He grabbed Inako by the shoulder. Startled, she noticed him at once—and without another word, the two of them bolted away in panic.

2.

But they had barely reached the end of the passenger car before coming to a dead stop. There was no passage connecting to the next car—only a solid wall blocking their way.

“Don’t run!”

Roku advanced toward them in long strides. As Kihachi scrambled for a plan, the train began crossing the Setagawa River.

To the north lay Lake Biwa; to the south, the ancient Tōgibashi Bridge at Setta. Kihachi’s panicked gaze caught sight of the side door on the carriage—and almost reflexively, he yanked it open. “Hold on tight!” He wrapped an arm around Inako’s waist.

“Wh-what? What’re you—”

Before she could finish, Roku realized his intent and lunged forward, shouting, “Stop!”

But just as Roku’s hand grazed the air, Kihachi pushed off hard—and leapt from the train with Inako in his arms.

Their bodies hung weightless for a split second before gravity seized them with greater force than expected. The look of stunned disbelief on Roku’s face shrank rapidly above them—then a violent jolt slammed into Kihachi’s back.

Inako’s scream cut short. His vision flickered. Cold water flooded his nose, sharp and stinging from the lake.

Somehow, he broke the surface and gasped, “Inago!”  
A moment later, Inako burst up with a sputter. Kihachi caught her flailing form. “Relax your body! Let me support you!” he urged, trying to steady her.

“—Zzzz…”

“What was that?!”

“The catfish is gonna eat us!”

“Now’s not the time to worry about that!”

He wanted to slap some sense into her—but unfortunately, he had no such luxury. Swept by the current, Kihachi stretched desperately for one of the railway’s bridge pilings. His fingers brushed it—just shy—and slipped away into empty water.

2.

The two of their bodies were being swept farther and farther away. If they kept drifting like this, they’d pass Tōgibashi Bridge at Setada, then the Arase Weir at Nangō, and after the Setagawa River changed its name to the Ujigawa, they would eventually meet the Biwa Lake Aqueduct at Fushimi.

“If we just keep floating, we’ll end up back in Fushimi. Not bad—saved us the train fare.”

“I’d rather walk back!”

A perfectly reasonable thing to say. Indeed, risking their lives wasn’t worth it. As Kihachi pondered what to do next, Inako cried out in a panicked voice:

“Kihachi-san, the catfish is coming!”

Kihachi turned his head in confusion—just what was she babbling about?—when a series of popping sounds reached his ears: *pop-pop-pop*.

Kihachi turned his head, and saw a white object, resembling a catfish, advancing slowly across the lake’s surface toward them, emitting strange noises. As it drew nearer, he realized it was a small Western-style boat with a flat, streamlined white hull.

“Oh my,” said a woman in a black dress coat, peering out from the boat. Noticing their predicament, she immediately tossed a flotation device toward them. The two grabbed it desperately, as if in a trance.

“Stuart-san, we’ve caught a person. We’re fishermen who catch people now.”

She called toward the back of the boat, and a man abruptly stood up from within.

He was tall and slender, with neatly groomed golden hair fluttering in the wind. His features were sharply chiseled, and his skin was as white as a daifuku. To the two stunned figures, the young Western man offered a gentle smile.

2.

On the deck of the boat, which chirped with an incongruously cheerful *pon-pon-pon*, the two sat drying their soaked hair and bodies with hand towels handed to them. The young Western woman in formal attire from earlier now seated herself before them. Her hair was tied up in a Margaret-style chignon, and her slightly thick eyebrows were neatly and strikingly groomed.

“Um… thank you so much for saving us,” Kihachi said, bowing deeply.

“Oh, don’t mention it. But more importantly—why on earth were you swimming in such a place?”

“We had our reasons. We jumped off a train.”

When he insisted, “It’s the truth, ma’am,” she burst into loud laughter. Perhaps drawn by the sound, the Western man who had been speaking with the helmsman stood up and came over, taking a seat beside the woman.

“I’m sorry—I should have introduced myself sooner. My name is Stuart Smith.”

With a warm, friendly smile, Stuart gently took Inako’s hand in his own.

Caught off guard by the sudden gesture, Inako froze stiff. Kihachi quickly stepped between them.

“Forgive her—she’s not used to shaking hands. I’m Sakamoto Kihachi.”

After Stuart shook Kihachi’s hand firmly, Inako snapped out of her daze and hastily bowed.

“My name is Hyakugawa Inako.”

“…Shirako?”

“Inako!”

Stuart scratched his head sheepishly as Inako glared at him, clearly annoyed.

Beside him, the woman giggled brightly. “Oh, come on—*Shirako*? That’s not even close!”

As Stuart smiled warmly, Kihachi let out a small sneeze. Though it was summer, being soaked to the bone like a drowned rat was still unbearable.

“You’ll catch a cold if you stay like that. Once we reach Hachiman, you can change clothes at my place.”

“Hachiman?” Kihachi and Inako echoed in unison.

“Mr. Stuart, you can’t just take them somewhere without even asking where they’re headed.”

“No need to worry—it’s fine. I happen to have business in Hachiman anyway. This is perfect.”

Talk about perfect timing. “Well then, all settled,” Saki said, pulling out a sake bottle. Ignoring the wide-eyed stares of the astonished group, she unfolded a small, origami-style *sakazuki* made of persimmon-tannin paper and poured amber-colored liquor into it.

“Saki-san, drinking this early in the morning again?”

“Oh, what’s wrong with that? I won’t get drunk and start causing trouble.”

When Saki handed the cup toward Kihachi saying, “This is whisky,” Stuart made a grimace.

“Saki-san, don’t encourage a child to drink alcohol.”

“It’s not like it’s legally prohibited or anything.”

Just to smell it, Kihachi brought the cup to his nose. But the intense smoky reek and sharp, stinging aroma made him cough violently. Next to him, Inako panicked. “Are you okay?”

2.

Still coughing, Kihachi mischievously passed the *sakazuki* to Inako, saying, “Smell it.” But contrary to his expectation, when Inako took the cup, she didn’t flinch or laugh—instead, she carefully brought it to her nose, her expression utterly serious.  

“This is the first time I’ve had such a liquor. It has a faint woody fragrance…”  

“You’ve got a good nose. This sake has matured slowly in wooden casks over many years.”  

Taking the cup from Inako, Saki downed the whisky in one gulp and groaned with deep satisfaction. Having lost interest in the drink, Kihachi turned his gaze around the boat.  

“…By the way, this boat seems different from a steamship.”  

“It runs on petroleum. Procuring fuel and maintenance are constant challenges.”  

Privately intrigued by the sensation of riding a petroleum-powered vessel for the first time, Kihachi asked, “But why does a teacher own such a boat?”  

Instead of answering with a smile, Stuart merely gave a wry grin. Saki answered in his place:  
“Strictly speaking, he’s a former teacher. He resigned to devote himself fully to Christian missionary work.”  

At the words “Christian missionary,” Inako’s eyes sparkled with interest, while Kihachi’s narrowed with suspicion.  

“This boat is our means of delivering God’s gospel. Normally we travel to Katata or Takashima, but today we were headed to Ishiyama at a friend’s request. Looking back, perhaps it was divine guidance after all.”  

“So you’re gonna twist even a random chance meeting into something holy, huh?”  

“Kihachi-san,” Inako chided gently, but Stuart stopped her with a soft “It’s fine.” Suddenly, he seemed to remember something.

“I’ve never met a foreigner before, but his manners are truly admirable.”

Stuart’s quiet consideration made Kihachi feel inadequate, as though he himself had failed to notice the missing footwear.

Inako’s damp hair clung to her cheeks, her entranced gaze fixed on Stuart. Droplets formed at the ends of her strands, trickling down her pale neck and delicate collarbones before vanishing into the hollow of her chest.

Kihachi averted his eyes from Inako and pulled a biscuit from his pocket. It was the last one—already softened by water. He chewed it bitterly.

Like a corseted waist, Lake Biwa has a narrow, constricted section called the *kubi*. North and south basins are divided at this point, and the boat was now passing through the *kubi*, advancing into the vast expanse of the northern basin.

To the west of the lake rose the mountains of Hiei and Hira, while to the east, beyond the flatlands, loomed Mikami-yama. Offshore from the ayame fishing villages, *eri* fences—used to catch young fish—were scattered sparsely across the lake’s surface.

2.

Ahead of the ship’s bow lay Okishima Island floating on the lake, Chōmeiji-yama rising prominently into Lake Biwa, and next to it, Hachiman-yama. The vessel turned its course toward the foot of Hachiman-yama, the farthest point to the right.

The town of Hachiman began when a castle was built upon Hachiman-yama—close to Azuchi, where Oda Nobunaga had his fortress—as a residence for Toyotomi Hidetsugu, with a castle town developed at its base. But after the castle was soon abandoned, the town gradually lost its military character. As the townspeople's mercantile spirit grew stronger, traveling merchants known as *Hachiman shōnin* emerged, joining other merchant groups from Ōmi Province to form the broader class known as *Ōmi merchants*.

These Ōmi merchants operated across the entire country, engaging not only in peddling but also in large-scale trade. So skilled were they that they came to rival the Ise merchants, another nationwide trading group—though this success bred resentment, leading to the mocking epithet: *"Ōmi thieves, Ise beggars."* Even into the Meiji era, some descendants of these Ōmi merchants adapted their methods and continued to thrive widely in cities like Osaka and Tokyo. In Hachiman itself, the main residences of such merchant families lined the streets.

When Kihachi and Inako arrived at Stuart-san’s home, they were overwhelmed by the three-story Western-style house.

Its walls were coated with stucco, lined with unusually large glass windows. The roof rose high in a hood-like shape, standing out unmistakably among the surrounding traditional townhouses, defiantly foreign in appearance.

Once inside, they were led to separate rooms to change clothes.

"These are clothes I wore when I was a teenager, but I'm glad I kept them,"

The roof rose high in a hood-like shape, standing out unmistakably among the surrounding traditional townhouses—defiantly foreign in appearance.

Once inside, they were led to separate rooms to change clothes.  
“These are clothes I wore when I was a teenager, but I'm glad I kept them,” said Stuart-san.

Kihachi slipped into Stuart-san’s white shirt, then tightened the belt around the slightly oversized trousers, folding up the hems. As he grimaced at the awkward fit between his legs, the door opened, and Inako peeked in—just her head.

Yet for some reason, she didn’t enter, instead fidgeting shyly, mumbling something inaudible.

From beyond the door, Saki-san’s voice urged, “Come on, quickly!”—and with a push from behind, Inako tumbled into the room.

Sunlight streaming through the window illuminated her white blouse. She stumbled, spun once on the spot, and her long white skirt flared outward like an opening parasol, fluttering gently as her slender feet came briefly into view. Her hair swayed, revealing a pale neck that flushed crimson. When the spin finally ended, Inako clutched her skirt tightly, her face on the verge of tears.

In that very moment, time truly seemed to slow—flowing with deliberate slowness.

“This is my first time wearing Western clothes… Do I look strange?”

“…Like a *teru teru bōzu*?”

Before the words had fully left his mouth, Inako lashed out, kicking him sharply in the shin.

“Can’t you say something more thoughtful?!” she snapped, baring her teeth at Kihachi’s pained groan.

Stuart-san, observing the exchange from nearby, wore a warm, affable smile.

“You look wonderful—very becoming. Quite lovely.”

“Lo—” Inako stammered, instantly wilting into shyness.

Stuart-san reached for a rectangular box resting on a nearby shelf. Opening the lid, he extended a bellows-style tube, its attached lens glinting in the light.

“It’s a Kodak camera.”

Kihachi instinctively rushed forward, peering closely at the portable camera made by America’s Kodak Company.

*Just how far back in time does this girl think she is?*

“Don’t be ridiculous! If that were true, the whole world’d be full of corpses by now!”

Still, Inako refused adamantly. With no choice, they reluctantly agreed to put the camera away. Seeing this, Saki clapped her hands together cheerfully.

“Well then, let’s have lunch! You two must be hungry. The rice should’ve finished cooking just about now.”

“No, really, that won’t be necessary. We’ll be leaving soon anyway—”

“What are you talking about?” Saki cut in, giving Kihachi’s shoulders a firm double pat.  
“Meals are more fun when shared! And besides, it’ll take time for your clothes to dry, right?”

In the end, the pair gave in and found themselves seated side by side at the living room table. As Saki set down dishes—simmered small ayu fish, dried tofu from Mount Kōya—Inako suddenly tugged at Kihachi’s sleeve.

“Kihachi-san… look! Something’s wrong—the konjac is red!”

On the little dish sat konjac dyed a vivid crimson, like the red-armored troops of Ii or Sanada.

“Around here, konjac is always red. It’s colored, sure, but the taste’s no different from regular konjac.”

“But why make it that color?”

During the meal, Stuart and Saki peppered Kihachi and Inako with questions about themselves, so they explained they’d fallen into Lake Biwa while playing around on the train en route to meet an acquaintance in Hachiman-machi.

“If you’re meeting someone around here, who might that be?”

2.

Kihachi was about to utter the name of Santakurō when a bright, lively voice called out from the entrance hall: “Teacher!”

“Hm? Who could that be?” Stuart turned toward the entrance—but did not return. After a long pause, a boy about Kihachi’s age timidly stepped into the room, muttering, “Sorry to intrude.”

“Saki-san. Mr. Stuart? He just went off toward the church.”

“Church?” Saki tilted her head, puzzled.

The boy scratched his head, looking embarrassed. “Some folks who’ve been studying the Bible over there came asking questions—and just took Mr. Stuart off with them.”

“Oh, honestly,” Saki said, downing a gulp of whiskey before standing up. “That’s just like him—too kind for his own good. I’m heading over to the church now. You’re welcome to come along, if you’d like.”

*No way,* Kihachi thought. Not only might Roku or Jinbei’emon show up at any moment, but who in their right mind would willingly walk into a church to hear some dubious sermon about the Bible?

“Hey, Inako-chan—do you happen to be interested in Christianity?”

Saki had asked the very last person she should have. Kihachi shot her a pleading look, but, as expected, Inako’s eyes lit up at the mention of Christianity.

“If it’s about God, I’m in,” Inako replied at once. *This girl will eat anything,* Kihachi thought bitterly.

With the decision settled, they headed outside. At the entrance, Inako sat on the bench, struggling with her first pair of lace-up shoes. When she finally managed to put them on, she happily tapped her heels together.

“I’ve always dreamed of dressing like a modern, stylish lady. Are you sure I can really have these?”  

Saki, already outside the entrance, laughed and said, “Of course!”  

“I got them from the Western shoe shop in Hachiman Town—they had some old pairs they weren’t using anymore. It’s perfect.”  

As Inako happily stamped her feet to test the shoes, Kihachi watched her closely. “What? Is something weird?” Inako tilted her head slightly. He’d meant to say something else, but decided against it. If he said anything foolish and she kicked him with those new Western shoes, he feared his bones might actually shatter.  

The church they headed to in Hachiman Town was still under construction, scheduled to be completed next month.  

Though still being built, the interior was nearly finished. The small, triangular-roofed church strangely fit into the landscape of Hachiman Town. Inside, a cross hung on the wall, and before the altar opposite it, long benches were evenly arranged. Unlike the main hall of a temple, where incense scented the air, here the only fragrance was that of fresh timber. Voices and even small sounds echoed sharply beneath the high ceiling.  

Stuart-san sat at the end of one of the benches, surrounded by young people. When Saki explained their situation, he immediately agreed to improvise a mock Sunday service for Kihachi and Inako.  

“This is my first time doing anything like this,” Inako murmured nervously as she sat down.  

From behind, Stuart placed a hand gently on her shoulder.  

“Inako-chan, do you like singing?”  

“M-me? I—I like listening to songs…”  

“We’ll all sing together later, so look forward to it.”  

2.

Stuart-san winked at Inako before striding confidently toward the altar.

“Kihachi-san, are you nervous too?”

Turning at her sudden question, he saw that Inako had said, “Your face is all stiff.”

He hadn’t thought himself the type to get so tense, but now that she mentioned it, his body felt restless, his head strangely heavy.

Stuart stood at the altar as pastor, and the service began.

He read selected passages from the Bible, explaining God’s teachings, pausing frequently for everyone to recite prayers together. The whole event flowed less like a formal ceremony and more like a Bible study session. Stuart played the organ, and they sang several hymns in chorus. Inako kept missing the pitch, yet each time she sang with obvious delight.

When the service ended, Inako was the first to rush over to Stuart as he tidied up.

“So Christianity… doesn’t involve strict ascetic practices or anything?”

“It depends on the denomination,” Stuart replied, “but we place our emphasis solely on faith and Scripture. No amount of austerity or penance can bring you closer to God. Salvation comes only through trusting in God and surrendering yourself to Him.”

Privately surprised by how closely this resembled the doctrines of Zenshoku-ji, Kihachi glanced sideways—only to find Inako looking back at him, eyes bright with excitement.

“That’s right! It’s so similar. Even though it’s a completely different religion—it’s amazing.”

“Well then,” Inako pressed, leaning forward intently, “does Christianity have a place like Paradise?”

Kihachi shivered again at the intensity in her voice.

“Yes,” Stuart answered. “We call it Heaven, or sometimes the Kingdom of God.”

“What’s it like there?”  

2.

“Do you know what Paradise is like, Inako-chan?”

“Um… I vaguely think it’s a world where you can meet your deceased family, and live happily without worries or the suffering of illness.”

“Then the Kingdom of God is just such a place.”

Glancing sideways at Inako’s bright, animated expression, Kihachi muttered under his breath, “Man, that’s awfully convenient.”

“Still,” he added, “you play the organ really well, Stuart-san.”

“There was a piano at my home in Canada. Whenever I had free time, I would play it over and over.”

“Hey, Inago, isn’t that enough already?” Kihachi interjected.

Without even turning to look at him, she pressed down on the keys, and an off-key note rang out through the church.

Kihachi couldn’t help but burst into laughter—and immediately earned a sharp glare from Inako. At once, Stuart stepped beside her, placing his hand gently atop the keyboard. “Put your fingers on the same keys as mine. Let’s play together.”

While Kihachi looked on, bewildered, Inako timidly placed her fingers over his.

They played “The ABC Song.” Matching Stuart’s steady movements, Inako gradually pressed each key. It was slow and halting, but when they finished, her face lit up. “Wow, this is fun!”

“Well, this man can do anything skillfully,” Kihachi grumbled.

2.

“Well, I think that’s a far better name than calling me ‘Inago.’”

Then Stuart turned to Kihachi. “You, on the other hand, are ice cream.”

Kihachi looked puzzled, so Inako chimed in, “It’s not that you’re cold like ice, is it?”—and everyone burst into laughter. Mortified at being made the butt of the joke, Kihachi clamped his lips shut and fell silent.

Stuart and Inako approached the organ again and began to play, just as before.

The tones bounced against the eardrums, failing to resonate in the chest. Kihachi felt dizzy, a nausea like indigestion rising within him, yet he kept his eyes fixed on Inako’s back as she played the organ.

He waited until the long stretch of music finally came to an end, then stepped forward.

“Excuse me… but we should be going soon. We have matters to attend to.”

Stuart stepped away from Inako. “My apologies,” he said, lowering the corners of his eyes. “Come to think of it, where were you two headed next? Who were you planning to meet?”

“Ah, someone named Santakurō.”

Conversation ceased. The air grew still. For some reason, everyone stared wide-eyed.

“…Sorry—I beg your pardon. Could you say that again?”

“Santakurō. He’s apparently a peculiar fellow who gives presents to children for free during winter.”

As soon as he finished speaking, everyone burst out laughing all at once. Bewildered, Kihachi’s eyes darted about helplessly.

“That’s Santa Claus,” Saki said gently, her expression almost pitying. “Every year at Christmas, there’s a legend about an old man called Santa Claus who walks around giving gifts to children. In the West, adults follow this tradition by giving presents to children. Unfortunately… there’s no real person named Santakurō.”

Someone laughed. “How innocent—to actually believe in Santa.” Heat rushed through Kihachi’s entire body.

In his mind, Seiroku was pointing at him and rolling with laughter.

“No wonder. I thought you seemed off—your fever’s back.”
