—2.  

She was breathing faster than usual, her chest growing warm. Seiroku surely had no idea what was going on. If she ever explained the meaning behind those notes to him now, what kind of expression would cross his face?  

As a strange blend of anxiety and anticipation swelled within her, Noriko briefly savored the sensation of her body transforming into a hot-air balloon, slowly lifting off the ground.  

It was a single remark from Jinbei’emon a few days later that drove a nail into that feeling.  

“Your fiancé has been decided. Ken’go-kun from Riku Tsuneichi Shōten.”  

In Jinbei’emon’s room, Noriko sat frozen in formal seiza. Her hollow eyes followed the curling blue smoke rising from his cigarette.  

“He’s been under our care since childhood. You know each other well enough, don’t you?”  

This was no joke. With Riku, she could barely recall a single conversation that hadn’t turned into an argument—how could they possibly know each other “well enough”?  

“No objections, I assume?”  

But Jinbei’emon’s eyes held the sharpness of a hunter, and pinned beneath that gaze, Noriko found herself unable to move. The buoyant feeling deflated instantly, and her body grew utterly heavy.  

Early summer of Meiji 36 (1903). At a teahouse by Yotsutsuji, located at Inari Shrine, Noriko sat beside Seiroku, eating ice cream served in small bowls while gazing down at the sweeping view of Shijōkura below.  

“Hey, it’s melting.”  

Startled by Seiroku’s voice, Noriko—lost in thought—hurriedly shoveled the ice cream into her mouth.  

“How unusual. Noriko, you’re completely spaced out.”  

2.

“Maybe it’s the heat getting to me,” Noriko murmured, lifting a spoonful of ice cream to her lips, when she overheard the conversation of middle-aged men seated on the adjacent bench, nibbling dango.

“That English phrase ‘I love you’—don’t you think translating it as *‘wagimi o aisu*’ lacks flair?”

At the word *aisu*, Noriko’s heart leapt.

“Well, it means that, so what else can you do? Just because you’re a *rakugo* storyteller doesn’t mean you have to twist everything into a joke.”

“You’re such a dull man as always. Even in this age of civilization and enlightenment, you should still value poetic sentiment. For instance…”

The man raised his empty dango skewer toward the faintly visible moon in the daytime sky.

“How about something like, ‘The moon is beautiful’—doesn’t that feel more Japanese, more fitting?”

“That makes no sense at all. What does the moon have to do with love? We’re in the twentieth century now. With sensibilities this outdated, we’ll never catch up to the West.”

Listening to their foolish banter, Noriko turned to Seiroku and asked, “Seiroku-san, how would you translate it?”

“Hmm… How about *‘Wagimi o aisukurīmu to omou’*—I think of you as ice cream?”

“What on earth does that mean?”

“Can’t you tell? It’s a pun—*aisu* from *aisu*, and *aisukurīmu*. If literal translation and poetic elegance fail, why not go for a witty play on words?”

“You’re an idiot,” she snapped dismissively, finishing off the last of her ice cream. Beside her, the rakugo-telling man chuckled softly, shaking his shoulder. “‘Love’ and ‘ice cream’… Now that’s clever.”

After the men had left, Seiroku stood, saying, “Shall we head out too?” But Noriko caught his sleeve and tugged him back down.

---

2.

“‘Wagimi o aisukurīmu to omou’—I think of you as ice cream?”  
“What on earth does that mean?”  
“Can’t you tell? It’s a pun—*aisu* from *aisu*, and *aisukurīmu*. If literal translation and poetic elegance fail, why not go for a witty play on words?”  
“You’re an idiot,” she snapped dismissively, finishing off the last of her ice cream.  

Beside her, the rakugo-telling man chuckled softly, shaking his shoulder.  
“‘Love’ and ‘ice cream’… Now that’s clever.”  

After the men had left, Seiroku stood, saying, “Shall we head out too?”  
But Noriko caught his sleeve and tugged him back down.

“……I want to stay here longer… I want to eat ice cream with Seiroku-san.”

Seiroku had been staring intently into Noriko’s eyes, but after a moment, he offered a faint smile, like one soothing a child.

“Don’t eat any more. You’ll get fat.”

As he urged her forward with a “Come on, let’s go,” Noriko followed behind him, crestfallen.

When they reached the sacred mound of Akubi Ōmyōjin, Seiroku turned around with a sigh.

“Don’t say such reckless things in public. If word gets out, you might end up unable to live at home.”

“……Recently, my future husband was decided.”

Seiroku lifted his eyelids slightly and murmured, “Who’s the match?”

“……I’m sorry. I can’t say.”

She didn’t want to say. If he knew the man was someone close to him, Seiroku would surely say, “That’s good,” and pull away from her. She didn’t want those words. What Noriko wanted was something entirely different.

“Well then, that’s good. At least you won’t have to worry about being left unmarried anymore.”

Yet Seiroku, of all people, calmly spoke the one thing she least wanted to hear.

Noriko narrowed her eyes sharply and stepped toward him. “I don’t want this!”

“But even if you say you don’t want it, refusing carelessly might get you disowned, won’t it?”

“If that happens, I’ll run away to your place.”

“What, become my sister-in-law? Is that what you’d have me do?”

“Don’t joke with me. …You know exactly what I’m trying to say, don’t you?”

Faced with Noriko’s earnest gaze, Seiroku—rarely so flustered—was visibly at a loss.

“But… I’ve always thought of you as a little sister… someone to cherish—”

2.

Before he could finish speaking, Noriko threw her arms around Seiroku from the front.

“I’m already sixteen… Can you tell I’ve grown since we first met? Not just my body—my heart too.”

As she tightened her embrace, she felt Seiroku’s breath catch.

“I’ve matured. I don’t cry in public anymore. It’s about time you acknowledged that I need you.”

Seiroku’s chest rose and fell gently with each breath—so warm, so tender, like a mother soothing a sleeping child. Noriko buried her face against him, lingering there.

Soon, his hands settled on her shoulders, and slowly, gently, he pulled her away.

“When I get some time off next, I’m thinking of taking Kihachi to the Osaka Exposition. If I leave him at home, Father’ll force him into electrical studies for the rest of his life—he won’t ever be free.”

“…What does that have to do with me? It’s nothing to do with me.”

“You come too. After the Exposition ends, the three of us will run away together.”

She turned his words over and over in her mind. Could this be real? Had she misheard?

“Let’s live together, somewhere far away—the three of us.”

Probably the happiest moment of her life. Noriko threw her arms tightly around Seiroku once more.

“When the date’s set, I’ll send you a letter.”

  

But no letter came, no matter how long she waited. Summer passed, winter arrived in the blink of an eye, and in December, Roku enlisted in the 9th Regiment in Ōtsu.

“There’s still time before enlistment. Why don’t you go see Roku-kun?”

2.

“Let’s run away together.” She turned his words over and over in her mind. Could this be real? Had she misheard? “Let’s live together, somewhere far away—the three of us.” Probably the happiest moment of her life. Noriko threw her arms tightly around Seiroku once more. “When the date’s set, I’ll send you a letter.” But no letter came, no matter how long she waited. Summer passed, winter arrived in the blink of an eye, and in December, Roku enlisted in the 9th Regiment in Ōtsu. “There’s still time before enlistment. Why don’t you go see Roku-kun?”  

So said Jinbei’emon as he lazily puffed on his cigarette—his face unnaturally calm.  

From experience, whenever her father wore that expression, he was almost certainly plotting something.  

Suspicious, Noriko seized the chance while he was out and searched his room. She combed through every corner, and finally, when she opened the bottom drawer of the chest, a single letter slipped out from beneath a stack of documents—and her entire body froze.  

“This… a letter from Seiroku-san?”  

The handwriting on the envelope matched Seiroku’s, but Noriko had never seen this letter before. Why was it hidden here? And why had the seal already been broken?  

With trembling hands, she pulled out the contents and quickly scanned them—then stared in shock.  

“I’ll be waiting for you in front of Dairin Kōtō at the time the *illumināshon* begins—”  

She glared at the specified date, but no matter how she looked at it, it was months in the past. Her head spun, her breath came in ragged gasps, and she collapsed right there onto the floor. She didn’t understand—couldn’t understand.  

But one thing was clear: without her knowing, everything had already spiraled beyond repair.  

“Okaa-san! You hid this letter, didn’t you!”  

When Jinbei’emon returned, Noriko confronted him with a ferocity that chilled the air. But he didn’t flinch—just slowly turned his eyes toward her, exhaled a thin stream of smoke, and said nothing.  

“I merely brushed aside a nuisance insect. You ought to thank me.”  

“…Go die.”  

“What did you say? How dare you speak to your parent like that!”  

“You call *this* being a parent—hiding things with such underhanded tricks?”

When Jinbei’emon returned, Noriko confronted him with a ferocity that chilled the air. But he didn’t flinch—just slowly turned his eyes toward her, exhaled a thin stream of smoke, and said nothing.

“Who’s really acting petty, I’d like to know? While engaged to Riku-kun, you’re sneaking around meeting some unknown man behind everyone’s back. What do you plan to do if the Hyakugawa name gets tarnished, hmm? How will you answer for it?”

With those words, Jinbei’emon struck her across the right cheek with a sharp exhale of breath. Clutching her stinging cheek, Noriko glared at him—and then fled the house. They had promised never to go to each other’s homes, but this was no longer a time for such rules.

She took the Keiden line to Shin-Kyōgoku. At Hōrai Butsugu-ten, she asked Bunshichi—without revealing her identity—about Seiroku. “He just left for Ōtsu,” she was told, “to prepare for enlistment.”

She hadn’t expected it would be easy to see him. Yet unable to give up, Noriko headed straight for Seven Roads train station.

By the time she arrived, an upward-bound train was just about to depart. The passenger car doors were already closed. Her heart pounding, she scanned the windows desperately—until at last, her eyes locked onto the face she sought.

“Seiroku-san!”

At the window, Seiroku—who had been resting his chin on his hand—looked up in disbelief, staring at her as if seeing a ghost.

He opened the window and leaned out. “Noriko? Is that you?”

“I’m sorry I couldn’t make it to the fair. My parents hid your letter.”

Seiroku opened and closed his mouth wordlessly, then finally murmured, “I see,” offering a faint, wistful smile.

“Oh—but the *Denki Mokuroku*... Kihachi never got around to finishing it, so I wrote in your *Denki Nishiki* as entry twenty.”

No—that wasn’t why she had given it to him at all.

2.

“Seiroku-san!” At the window, Seiroku—who had been resting his chin on his hand—looked up in disbelief, staring at her as if seeing a ghost. He opened the window and leaned out. “Noriko? Is that you?”  

“I’m sorry I couldn’t make it to the fair. My parents hid your letter.”  

Seiroku opened and closed his mouth wordlessly, then finally murmured, “I see,” offering a faint, wistful smile. “Oh—but the *Denki Mokuroku*... Kihachi never got around to finishing it, so I wrote in your *Denki Nishiki* as entry twenty.”  
No—that wasn’t why she had given it to him at all.  

---  

“What do you think of me, Seiroku-san? Am I just some girl you see as a little sister?”  

The moment Noriko cried out, the train began to move with a sluggish lurch, like a giant caterpillar stirring into motion.  

“Don’t follow me. It’s dangerous if you come any further.”  

“I won’t! I’ll cling on no matter what and go with you!”  

Gently prying Noriko’s hands from the window bars, Seiroku smiled broadly, guileless and warm. “Still such a reckless girl as ever.” The train rapidly picked up speed, carrying Seiroku farther and farther away.  

“But y’know—you’re Japan’s greatest beauty.”  

The instant Seiroku pulled his face back inside, a red ribbon tumbled out from within, fluttering freely against the gray backdrop of smoke. Noriko snatched it up and pressed it to her face, her shoulders trembling.  

No tears came. She was past the age where she could cry—even if she wanted to.  

---  

Sitting on the stone steps of Himure Hachiman-gū at the foot of Hachiman-yama, Noriko stroked the cover of the *Denki Mokuroku*.  

“If Riku were to see what’s inside this catalog… it’d be over.”  

Roku knew about the notes for *Denki Nishiki*, but he shouldn’t have any idea about the connection between her and Seiroku.  

Noriko removed the lamp’s glass chimney and brought the open flame close to the *Denki Mokuroku*.  

“Noriko…san?”  

Startled by the sudden voice, Noriko flinched violently.  

At the bottom of the stone steps, Sakamoto Kihachi stood holding a lantern, his gaze fixed dumbfoundedly upon Noriko—and the *Denki Mokuroku* in her hands.  

“What… are you planning to do…?”  

2.

Noriko tightened her lips into a thin line. Kihachi stepped forward cautiously, almost pleadingly.  

“I don’t know what’s happened… but if this disappears, Inako can’t be saved.”  

Noriko ground her teeth. Kihachi inched closer, slowly, deliberately.  

“You care about Inako. About your sister. You must.”  

“No! It’s not like that!”  

The cry tore from Noriko like a scream of pain, and Kihachi froze.  

“I’ve always hated Inako. Ever since we were kids. She’s too naive—believes everything people say, keeps failing, yet keeps trying anyway… I’ve always despised her for it.”  

*It’s a lie*, her heart ached. Inako was her beloved sister. The only one left in this world she could still call her anchor.  

“But even though she’s so dull-witted, someone’s always looking out for her. Okaa-san, Roku-san, you, Kihachi-san… everyone needs her. Her sincerity, her purity, the way she draws people to her—she’s even got Okaa-san’s love. Inako has everything I don’t… everything! It’s always just Inako!”  

“Noriko-san…”  

“I’ve always been alone! Even Seiroku—someone I trusted—didn’t need me!”  

The moment the words slipped out, Kihachi’s eyes widened in sudden understanding.  

“That red ribbon… I’ve always felt I’d seen it somewhere before. It was tied around the biscuit tin my brother bought me at the fair.”  

2.

“…Yes, it was a gift from Seiroku-san. I wanted to be with Seiroku-san. When the war ended, I steeled myself—I resolved to marry Roku-san for the sake of the family. But then Roku-san suddenly changed, acting unnaturally cold and distant. Postponing the wedding, always worrying about others instead of me… Does he think I don’t matter at all?”

“…Of course he does. After all, I’ve been betraying him all along. Now I’m complaining like some spoiled child. But I can’t let Roku-san find out about my relationship with Seiroku-san. If he discovers it and rejects me… I’ll be completely alone.”

At Kihachi’s look of quiet relief, Noriko let out a small, deflated “Huh?”

“You’ve always wanted someone to lean on, haven’t you, Noriko-san?”

She had no reply. Noriko bit down hard on her lower lip.

“Yes. I’m just a child throwing a tantrum. But I don’t ever want to be alone again!”

Resolved, she lowered *Denki Mokuroku* toward the flame.

But just before the fire touched it, a large hand reached out from behind and slapped the lamp’s wick, snuffing it out.

Darkness fell. Someone snatched *Denki Mokuroku* from her grasp.

Before she could react, Kihachi came rushing over with the lamp—its light revealing Roku standing behind Noriko.

“I finally found you,” Roku exhaled, *Denki Mokuroku* clenched in his hand.

“I can’t let you burn this. It’s the last memento of my dearest friend.”

As Noriko stood frozen, Roku’s face twisted in pain.

“It was I who gave *Denki Mokuroku* to Stuart-san. I… I know.”

“Know what?”

2.

The flame flickered, about to ignite the paper. But just before it touched, a large hand shot out from behind and slapped the wick of the lamp, extinguishing it. Darkness fell. Someone snatched *Denki Mokuroku* from her grasp. Before she could react, Kihachi came rushing over with the lantern—its light revealing Roku standing behind Noriko.

“I’ve finally found you,” Roku exhaled, *Denki Mokuroku* clenched tightly in his hand. “I can’t let you burn this. It’s the last memento of my dearest friend.” As Noriko stood frozen, Roku’s face twisted in anguish. “It was I who gave *Denki Mokuroku* to Stuart-san. I… I know.”

“Know what?”

“You and Seiroku—I know about your relationship.”

Noriko, like a puppet whose strings had been cut, crumpled to the ground.

“…Did you serve with my brother in the war, Roku-san?” Kihachi asked quietly.

“All along. From the day we met at school, until the fortress at Nanzen where he died.”

“You… you witnessed my brother’s final moments?”

Roku lowered his eyes. After a long silence, he slowly nodded.

　　　　　　　　　　Seven

Eleven years ago, I met Seiroku at commercial school.

We weren’t particularly close—just classmates in the same year, never really speaking much. We didn’t seem compatible, and our relationship didn’t deepen. That autumn, however, the worst flood in history struck Ōmi Province.

Back then, Lake Biwa looked unnaturally vast, and entire villages along the shore were completely swallowed by water.

Among those helping with the rescue was Seiroku. He skillfully maneuvered a boat through the flooded streets—water rising up to the second floor—and pulled people stranded in their homes to safety one after another.

But then, by sheer accident, Seiroku fell from his boat. Before anyone could act, he was swept away and nearly crushed beneath a mass of drifting debris. I was nearby. I plunged into the water and saved him as he was drowning.

“Thanks, man—I owe you my life. And damn, you’re strong!”

Seiroku laughed and thanked me warmly. From that moment on, we became close friends, talking constantly at school. I always found his jokes delightful.

2.

We often spent break time wrestling—sumo or judo-style grappling—with the guys in our group. The size difference between me and Seiroku was like that of a matchstick and a daikon radish, yet every single match ended with me losing. Seiroku would effortlessly dodge my attacks, sidestepping with lazy grace before suddenly sweeping my legs out from under me and dropping me flat on the ground.

“You’re easy to read—everything you’re thinking shows right on your face,”  
he said, laughing, after I’d lost for what must’ve been the umpteenth time, lying sprawled on my back.

“You’re just like a train—barreling straight ahead without stopping.”

“Are you making fun of me?”

“No way. I actually like that about you.”

Seiroku was the kind of guy who constantly climbed to high places whenever he got the chance. Whether at school or out somewhere else, if there was a spare moment, he’d scramble up a mountain, a tree, or a rooftop, just to enjoy the view from above.

Because of this, his classmates often joked, “You’re like the monkey from Hiyo Shrine,” though it was clearly said with affection.

I wasn’t nearly as good at getting along with people as Seiroku was. I always followed rules and lived properly, doing what others expected—so why was it that someone so carefree as Seiroku always seemed to be in the spotlight? I found myself wondering this again and again.

What surprised me most, though, was learning that Seiroku attended Mr. Stuart’s Bible class.

He became especially interested in the Bible’s descriptions of the Kingdom of God and in the story of Jesus’ sacrifice.

After reading deeply into the scriptures, he came across the teaching: “Let your good deeds be done in secret.” He immediately noticed its similarity to the Ōmi merchants’ philosophy of *inboku zenji*—“virtue practiced in silence”—and remarked with amusement, “Maybe Jesus was actually a businessman after all.”  
(Though, come to think of it, Jesus was more the type to drive merchants out of the holy place.)

2.

What surprised me most, though, was learning that Seiroku attended Mr. Stuart’s Bible class. He studied the scriptures in his own way, adding unique interpretations that left those around him deeply impressed.

In matters of faith, I felt as though I were falling behind Seiroku.

Being near someone bright is a miserable thing.

The brighter the person beside you shines, the darker the shadow you cast.

And it was Noriko’s presence that intensified this feeling within me.

We used to bicker constantly, face to face, but after I graduated from commercial school and after Nae’ko-san passed away, she became entangled in personal turmoil, and we drifted apart for a time.

When I visited the Hyakugawa household again after many years, I found Noriko playing the shamisen on the engawa.

I stopped in my tracks without realizing it. Her slender white fingers plucking the strings, her eyes carrying a faint trace of melancholy—I vividly remember how my heart trembled at the sight of Noriko, who had quietly become a woman.

After that, I began seeking out chances to talk to her, inventing all sorts of excuses.

But no matter what I said, Noriko always seemed distant, lost in thought. My arrogant self would end up lashing out even more than before, hurling insults and nitpicking—our old pattern of quarrels resumed unchanged. Yet the fool that I was took pride even in these arguments, because as long as she was arguing with me, she was still looking at me.

One day in Meiji 36 (1903), on my way home from visiting the Hyakugawa house, I happened to run into Seiroku again at Inari Station.

Seeing his familiar face—still sharp and spirited, yet retaining that boyishness—I was flooded with nostalgia. We exchanged stories about our lives since we last met, reminiscing warmly about days gone by.

2.

Lashing out even more than before, hurling insults and nitpicking—our old pattern of quarrels resumed unchanged. Yet the fool that I was took pride even in these arguments, because as long as she was arguing with me, she was still looking at me. One day in Meiji 36 (1903), on my way home from visiting the Hyakugawa house, I happened to run into Seiroku again at Inari Station. Seeing his familiar face—still sharp and spirited, yet retaining that boyishness—I was flooded with nostalgia. We exchanged stories about our lives since we last met, reminiscing warmly about days gone by.

Midway through our conversation, I suddenly noticed the bundle of papers tucked under Seiroku’s arm.

“What’s that you’re holding?”

“Oh, this?” Seiroku casually lifted the stack, and I froze in shock.

It was *Denki Nishiki*’s notes—the very ones Noriko had said she would one day give to “her future husband.”

“You’ve seen Noriko recently?”

“Once in a while,” he replied.

I recalled Noriko playing the shamisen on the veranda.

Her eyes, tinged with melancholy. Just then, I understood clearly why they had seemed so beautiful.

Without thinking, I grabbed Seiroku by both shoulders.

“What kind of relationship do you have with Noriko?”

“H-hey, what’s with you? So tense all of a sudden?”

“You… don’t happen to feel something for Noriko, do you?”

Seiroku looked flustered at first, but then, hearing my words, he broke into an easy grin.

“No, no way. Nothing like what you’re suspecting. I see her like a little sister, that’s all. Besides, I prefer older women.”

I let go of Seiroku’s shoulders, but my heart didn’t lighten.

If anything, I now keenly felt within myself a dark, violent storm of emotion churning violently.

Noriko’s feelings—what I craved more than anything, what I would have given anything to possess—this man held them effortlessly.

An unbearable humiliation. A little sister, is it?

Then if you don’t want her so much, I’ll just take her from you.

2.

Hearing my words, he broke into an easy grin. “No, no way. Nothing like what you’re suspecting. I see her like a little sister, that’s all. Besides, I prefer older women.” I let go of Seiroku’s shoulders, but my heart didn’t lighten. If anything, I now keenly felt within myself a dark, violent storm of emotion churning violently. Noriko’s feelings—what I craved more than anything, what I would have given anything to possess—this man held them effortlessly. An unbearable humiliation. A little sister, is it? Then if you don’t want her so much, I’ll just take her from you.

And then I committed my greatest mistake. Through family channels, I arranged to have Noriko become my bride. Before long, the engagement between Noriko and me was settled, and Seiroku could no longer approach her.

Afterward, Seiroku’s behavior remained perfectly normal. But I assumed he was merely putting on a brave front. What I wanted to shout at him was: *“Noriko belongs to me now.”*

I was, without a doubt, a thoroughly base and despicable human being.

Was this truly something love had driven me to do? No—it wasn’t. I had simply wanted to wallow in petty pride, even if it meant trampling underfoot the happiness of those dear to me.

In Meiji 37 (1904), war finally broke out with Russia.

Mobilization orders were issued to our Second Army, including the Fourth Division, and we crossed the sea toward the Liaodong Peninsula.

“You know,” he said, “once we win this war, I’m heading straight to Paris.”

Seiroku and I leaned against the ship’s railing, gazing out over the endless expanse of the Sea of Japan.

“We’ll drive the Russians out of Manchuria, seize the lands east of Lake Baikal, take control of the Trans-Siberian Railway, capture their capital, end the war—and then I’ll go to Paris and climb the Eiffel Tower.”

“Why are you so obsessed with getting to high places?”

“All people love high places, don’t they? Wanting to stand above others—that’s human nature.”

His words felt like a veiled indictment aimed directly at me, and I couldn’t utter a single reply. Whether he knew it or not, Seiroku then spoke again, his voice suddenly soft and solemn:  
“Hey, Roku… the man Noriko’s marrying—is it you?”

2.

My vision swayed unsteadily. It felt as though the Day of Final Judgment had suddenly been moved forward.

“At the Industrial Exhibition, I heard it from his old man.”

“From Jinbei’emon?”

“I’d invited Noriko to the exhibition, but apparently my letter was intercepted. Instead, her father showed up at the meeting place. That’s when I learned about her engagement to Roku—and he told me to give up.”

I suppressed the urge to scream, and deeply bowed my head.

“I’m sorry… I did a cowardly thing! I—I—”

“Cut it out. People are watching.”

“You’re holding back because of me, aren’t you? Back during the great flood, I saved your life…”

“Don’t say another word—or I’ll shoot you in the back.”

“So… you’re really okay with this?”

“I’m not that weak-willed. I got over it long ago.”

With those firm words, Seiroku laughed sharply and punched me hard in the chest.

The Liaodong Peninsula juts westward out of Manchuria. To isolate the Russian forces at Port Arthur at the peninsula’s tip, our troops landed at its center on May 7th and began attacking the enemy garrison holed up in the ancient fortress of Kinshū on May 25th. Our Fourth Division nearly achieved first entry—but the explosives meant to breach the city gate were ruined by rain, allowing the First Division, attacking from another direction, to seize the glory instead.

---

2.

“I got over it long ago.” With those firm words, Seiroku laughed sharply and punched me hard in the chest.

The Liaodong Peninsula juts westward out of Manchuria. To isolate the Russian forces at Port Arthur at the peninsula’s tip, our troops landed at its center on May 7th and began attacking the enemy garrison holed up in the ancient fortress of Kinshū on May 25th. Our Fourth Division nearly achieved first entry—but the explosives meant to breach the city gate were ruined by rain, allowing the First Division, attacking from another direction, to seize the glory instead.

After capturing Jinzhou Castle, the next target became the small elevated hill to the southwest—Fortress Nanzen (Nansan). It was an eerie fortress. There was nothing that could shield us as we advanced toward the enemy positions lining the mountainside; barbed wire had been laid, and their artillery emplacements were covered with solid concrete. Amid the ceaseless thunder of intense artillery fire exchanged between Japanese and Russian forces, our senses nearly numbed, we—the right wing of the Second Army—stared grimly at the enemy lines.

“The Osaka boys are pretty fired up.”

“They missed out on being first again. Now they can’t shake off that nasty rumor: ‘Once more defeated, Eighth Regiment—then the Ninth gets medals.’ And on top of that, these guys are mocking us too, even though we’ve got nothing to do with it. The Eighth from Osaka has never lost before, so this stings extra hard.”

“Everyone wants to be better than someone else. Those who mock others aren’t impressive people to begin with.”

“You’ve gotten quite witty lately, Roku. We’re counting on you—Roku-Jōki.”

“What… what did you just call me?”

“We mashed your name ‘Roku’ with the old-time word for train, *roku-jōki*. A cheap pun? Nah, doesn’t suit you. Charging straight ahead—that’s your style. Right, everyone? Don’t you all think it’s a good name?”

He turned to the others, who burst into laughter and shouted, “Exactly!” After cackling heartily, Seiroku pulled a folding-bound book from his backpack and gazed at its cover with reverent eyes.

“Wait… is that the *Denki Mokuroku* you’re always talking about?”

---

2.

“aren’t impressive people to begin with.”  
“You’ve gotten quite witty lately, Roku. We’re counting on you—Roku-Jōki.”  
“What… what did you just call me?”  
“We mashed your name ‘Roku’ with the old-time word for train, *roku-jōki*. A cheap pun? Nah, doesn’t suit you. Charging straight ahead—that’s your style. Right, everyone? Don’t you all think it’s a good name?”  

He turned to the others, who burst into laughter and shouted, “Exactly!”  

After cackling heartily, Seiroku pulled a folding-bound book from his backpack and gazed at its cover with reverent eyes.  
“Wait… is that the *Denki Mokuroku* you’re always talking about?”

“Yeah. My old man almost burned it once. Since then, I’ve been keeping it safe.”

Seiroku handed the *Denki Mokuroku* to me. The edges and corners were scorched and worn down from constant handling. I quickly scanned the ancient-looking catalog.

“*Number Nine: Lightning Coat*… Projects images onto the coat’s surface—lets even the poorest enjoy countless patterns with just one garment. *Number Nineteen: Denrikan*… Restores senses to the blind and deaf through electrical signals.”

“All dreams, right? But every time I read it, I feel alive again.”

Then Seiroku looked beyond the fortress before us, far into the distance.

“There’s also *Number Twenty: Denki Nishiki*, entrusted to me by Noriko. That one’s my talisman.”

As Seiroku smiled, the order for assault preparation was issued. Suddenly, everything became frantic. I shoved the catalog I’d forgotten to return into my belt. Gripping my rifle, I stared ahead, listening to my pounding heartbeat.

“Assault!”

At the command, the bugle sounded, and we charged en masse from our positions.

Until that moment, I’d imagined war as warriors rushing forward together, slashing at one another. But war in this new century wasn’t anything so clean. The instant we began our charge, the Russian lines erupted—every weapon blazing at once.

What do you suppose happens to a human being when an endless hail of machine-gun fire, bursting shrapnel shells, and every form of murderous intent rains down upon the battlefield like a storm?  
That day’s carnage… honestly, I can’t bring myself to speak of it now.

---

2.

The crackle of machine-gun fire, like roasting beans, erupted all around; without pause, shells rained down in relentless waves. Even so, into that world overrun with death, we had no choice but to hurl our own bodies forward as shields.

Later, I heard that during the subsequent Battle of Port Arthur, the lessons learned at南山 (Nanzan) had not been put to use—and I was filled with a deep, helpless despair. No… perhaps they *were* understood, yet there simply was no other way. This nation was facing, for the first time, the reality of twentieth-century warfare—war whose sole purpose was total annihilation.

I charged ahead, mindless and blind, when suddenly a bullet struck my abdomen. The impact sent me tumbling headlong down the slope.

I stood up again, strangely aware that my body felt little pain. On the ground lay my torn belt and the *Denki Mokuroku*, its surface dented by the bullet. The moment I realized it had taken the shot for me, Seiroku’s face flashed in my mind—and a chill ran through me.

Frantically scanning the area, I spotted a Japanese soldier lying on his back amid the crater left by an exploding shell.

It was Seiroku—I knew instantly. Rushing over, I saw he had already lost focus in his eyes, and had been hit almost in the same spot as I had.

His face was deathly pale, the color draining even from his irises. —He was beyond saving. With both hands pressed to his wound, I burst into tears and screamed, “I’m sorry, Seiroku! Forgive me!”

His hand—cold as ice—weakly grasped mine where it covered his stomach. His lips trembled.

“…Kihachi… Eiffel…”

People don’t die reciting grand, dramatic lines like characters in a play, not even at the very end.

---

2.

It was a shell. It was Seiroku—I knew instantly. Rushing over, I saw he had already lost focus in his eyes, and had been hit almost in the same spot as I had. His face was deathly pale, the color draining even from his irises. —He was beyond saving. With both hands pressed to his wound, I burst into tears and screamed, “I’m sorry, Seiroku! Forgive me!” His hand—cold as ice—weakly grasped mine where it covered his stomach. His lips trembled. “…Kihachi… Eiffel…”  
People don’t die reciting grand, dramatic lines like characters in a play, not even at the very end.

---

“…Nori… ko…”

Seiroku murmured deliriously in a fading voice. He must have been in unbearable pain,  
yet—Seiroku seemed to smile at the very end.

　　　　　　　　　　＊

“I owe my life to this *Denki Mokuroku*,”  
Roku said, tracing the bullet hole with his fingertip before returning the *Denki Mokuroku* to Kihachi.  
“If I had returned the *Mokuroku*, it might have been Seiroku who survived instead. If you’re going to hate someone, hate me.”  
Kihachi stared blankly at the *Denki Mokuroku* in his hands, his gaze unfocused.  
“I… I couldn’t possibly hate you,” was all he could manage to reply.  
“I see,” Roku murmured, lowering his eyes. “But I’d have been grateful if you did.”  
“…After the Battle of Nanzan, I decided I would die in this war. There was no point in living, so I always charged to the front lines. If I were to die, I wanted to use my body as a shield and save as many comrades as I could. But at Liaoyang, at Shaho, at Heigoutai—even when bullets struck my body, even when shrapnel pierced me—I did not die. I survived even the final battle at Fengtian.  
Before returning home, I tried to shoot myself in the head, but my finger on the trigger refused to move.  
In the end, I was a cowardly, pitiful man who couldn’t even bring himself to kill his own self.  
When I returned to Ōtsu, I happened to encounter a Russian prisoner of war walking through the town. There was a POW camp on the grounds of Mii-dera, and at that time, prisoners were allowed to move freely about the streets.

2.

The Russians in their military uniforms spotted me and came running over. I realized at once: *This could turn into a fight.*

But instead, they smiled warmly, embraced me tightly. And when they learned I was a Christian, they took me with them to a nearby church. Though our denominations differed, we prayed together as one.

That time was deeply comforting. We hadn’t fought out of hatred or desire to kill. We had both fought to protect our countries, our honor, our families. That yearning—there was no difference between nations. In that moment, I truly felt we were all brothers. Then, from the corner of the church, a vague human figure slowly emerged.

Kind, compassionate eyes. A full, flowing mustache. *Could it be?*—I thought, even as the vision dissolved.

At that instant, I felt as if my body had been infused with new flesh and blood.

I finally understood clearly why I had survived. I *must* live. For Seiroku’s sake, for the enemy soldiers I killed, and for all lives yet to come. To live on, endure, and save—by even the smallest measure—those who seek salvation. That, I realized, was my mission.”

“…So ever since you came back, you’ve devoted yourself to charitable work.”

Noriko, crouched on the stone steps, raised her face. Roku’s expression twisted painfully.

“I’ve been running from you all this time. The fact remains—I tore you two apart. Seiroku died because of my arrogance. How can I possibly face you now? What could I ever say? If my very survival causes you pain… then I…!”

2.

“…Seiroku-san was just hit by a stray bullet,” Noriko said quietly.

“It wasn’t anyone’s fault. But people want to blame either God or themselves—it’s how they come to terms with someone’s death. Humans crave acceptance, even in the face of the utterly senseless.”

Roku covered his face with his hands. “I couldn’t accept it,” he said, his voice strained, as if forced out.

“The more I thought about Seiroku’s death, the heavier the *Denki Mokuroku* became—so heavy I could barely bear to carry it. I kept thinking I should return it to Kihachi as soon as possible. But I was afraid—afraid my own guilt would be exposed. I didn’t want to be condemned for my sins, didn’t want my own ugliness dug up and laid bare.”

“So that’s why you entrusted the *Mokuroku* to Stuart-san?” Kihachi asked.

“I intended to retrieve it myself once I’d made up my mind and return it to you. But that selfish decision of mine led directly to this whole mess. I won’t ask for your forgiveness. I’m deeply sorry.”

Roku bowed deeply. Though he had once been a large, imposing man, under the dim light of the lamp, his frame appeared terribly small.

“I can’t bring myself to blame you, Roku-san—and I don’t intend to.”

Kihachi forced a smile toward Roku, who kept his eyes downcast, barely lifting his face.

“To be honest, I don’t even know how I should feel right now. But I’m glad the *Mokuroku* has come back. All that’s left now is to deliver it to Dr. Paikaru.”

That would settle everything. As for Roku and Seiroku, he could think carefully about them later, once things had settled.

“No. That’s not it.”

2.

A large, imposing man, yet under the dim light of the lamp, he appeared terribly small. “I can’t bring myself to blame you, Roku-san—and I don’t intend to,” Kihachi said, forcing a smile toward Roku, whose eyes remained downcast, barely lifting his face. “To be honest, I don’t even know how I should feel right now. But I’m glad the *Mokuroku* has come back. All that’s left now is to deliver it to Dr. Paikaru.” That would settle everything. As for Roku and Seiroku, he could think carefully about them later, once things had settled.  

“No. That’s not it.”  

Such hope was instantly crushed by Noriko, her voice trembling.  

“The doctor’s objective isn’t the *Mokuroku*.”  

Kihachi’s voice tightened. “What… do you mean?”  

Noriko began to speak haltingly, hesitantly.  

When Noriko and Seiroku had parted ways at the Seven Roads train station, the branch manager of Mitsue Shōten’s Fushimi outpost had been riding in the same passenger car—and had overheard everything from a distance.  

—The *Denki Mokuroku*, that strange book, contains the brewing method for Hyakugawa’s secret reserve sake.  

Yōsuke, upon hearing this during the handover, had used it as leverage. He subtly hinted that he knew of Noriko and Seiroku’s relationship, and pressured Noriko into cooperating with the search for the *Denki Mokuroku*. Of course, Noriko had no idea where the *Mokuroku* was—she couldn’t possibly have helped even if she wanted to.  

But the day after Inako fell into the Setagawa River, Yōsuke approached her again, this time holding a newspaper article about a failed suicide attempt. He demanded to know if she had any idea where Inako might have gone. She had intended to refuse, insisting her cooperation was limited to the *Mokuroku* matter and that she wouldn’t reveal Inako’s whereabouts—  

But then Yōsuke said, “They might have boarded the train to go after the *Mokuroku*.”  

Those words shook Noriko deeply. And so, despite her resolve, she revealed that the Western boat belonged to Stuart-san—and that Inako might be in Hachiman-machi.  

Yōsuke had promised, “Once we’re done with the *Mokuroku*, we’ll dispose of it.” But Noriko hadn’t believed him for a second.  

2.

“That loose talk of his—he said it to us too. ‘The *Denki Nishiki* will be mass-produced at a larger brewery, and the unprofitable Hyakugawa Shuzō will be shut down and turned into a beer factory.’ When I heard that… well, if the brewery was going under anyway, I figured I might as well burn the *Mokuroku* myself—make sure it was destroyed for good.”

Noriko stared at Kihachi’s face, which had gone ashen, her eyes brimming with tears.

“What Dr. Paikaru wants is the method for making *Denki Nishiki*—the very technique Seiroku must have transcribed into the *Mokuroku*.”

With trembling hands, Kihachi opened to entry twenty of the *Denki Mokuroku*: *Number Twenty: Denki Nishiki*.  

Beside it, only a single line was written: *"Pungent and dry—truly like electricity."*

“You see? This catalog is nothing but a child’s scribble.”

The next morning, after leaving Stuart-san’s house, Kihachi and the others boarded a train at Hachiman Station to begin their return journey. Kihachi nibbled on biscuits he’d bought in Hachiman-machi and gazed out the window at the Ōmi countryside rolling past.

As the train steadily headed south, reaching the foot of Mikami-yama, it raced through the lands of Yasu.

Even now just open fields—the area would transform during the farming off-season, when the famed Ōmi-jōfu fabric would be laid out to bleach in a process known as Yasu-zarashi, turning the entire expanse into a vast, snow-white plain. Recalling that image, Kihachi suddenly thought of Inako in her Western-style white clothes—and then idly wondered whether a pale *kasuri* kimono might suit her after all.

Across Lake Biwa, the Hira mountain range and Mount Hiei stretched side by side. If Inako were here, she’d probably be pointing eagerly out the window, asking, “Can you see Zenshoku-ji from here?” again and again.

2.

No matter what scenery he saw, thoughts of Inako were all that came to mind. Every time he imagined her expression or her words, his entire being seemed to grow warm with fever. It was as though a resistor labeled "Inako" had been wired directly into the circuitry of his mind.

And tomorrow, that very Inako would be getting married. Was he really expected to find, before then, notes on a sake unlike any he’d ever tasted?

The train had just begun crossing the Setagawa River. The moment he'd leapt from it and been rescued by Stuart-san felt like it had happened only hours ago.

"...I wanted to become someone like Mr. Stuart."

Kihachi murmured this quietly, prompting Roku, seated across from him, to lift his face in response: "Mr. Stuart?"

"I can’t say clever things, I can’t design buildings, and I can’t play the organ or piano. If only I’d become a man as accomplished as him… maybe things would’ve turned out better."

"So you wanted to be someone who plays the organ?"

Startled, Kihachi replied, "No."

"Then why compare yourself at all? Rivalry and jealousy are not the same. Rivalry is a positive emotion—it drives self-improvement. But jealousy is negative; it risks destroying not only oneself but others too. ...I learned that truth through my own suffering."

Roku glanced toward Noriko, seated on the opposite side of the aisle. Seemingly exhausted, she leaned against the window, softly breathing in sleep. Bathed gently in the light from outside, her resting form resembled a beautiful painting.

Turning back to Kihachi, Roku’s usual stern, imposing expression softened into one so gentle it seemed almost unbelievable.

"You’re the one who will create Paradise with electricity, aren’t you?"

2.

At Banba Station, Kihachi and Roku disembarked, while Noriko continued on to Kyoto alone. After parting ways with Roku as well, Kihachi returned to Zenshoku-ji. Once he’d explained everything to his parents, he collapsed onto his back on the tatami mat in the inner room.

There was nothing more he could do to save Inako. Helpless, Kihachi stared upward at the forbidden shrine within the kamidana.

As dusk deepened into night, a lamp was lit in the darkening room. Shifting only his eyes, Kihachi saw Enki enter, holding the lamp. He sat beside him and gave Kihachi’s head a light tap.

“You did your best.”

Those words—never spoken to him in childhood—stirred something rising from deep within his chest.

“…Even if I did my best, there was nothing I could do with my own strength.”

“That’s only natural. Human self-power is inherently limited. When things can’t be helped…”

“You gonna tell me to put my faith in Amida-sama’s other-power?”

Anticipating the start of a sermon, Kihachi began to sit up, ready to flee—but Enki’s hand caught his wrist.

“Rely on us,” Enki said, his rough voice thick with warmth and resolve.

“Rely without hesitation on me, on Kei, on Bunshichi, on the others from the Night Study Meeting—on all the people around you. No one lives purely by their own strength. Why else would we form bonds as family and friends? It’s precisely because self-power isn’t enough that people support each other and find happiness together.”

Enki’s gaze was unwavering. Through his calloused hand, Kihachi felt a steady heat pass into his own.

2.

As the sermon went on, Kihachi began to sit up, ready to flee—but Enki’s hand caught his wrist. “Rely on us,” Enki said, his rough voice thick with warmth and resolve. “Rely without hesitation on me, on Kei, on Bunshichi, on the others from the Night Study Meeting—on all the people around you. No one lives purely by their own strength. Why else would we form bonds as family and friends? It’s precisely because self-power isn’t enough that people support each other and find happiness together.” Enki’s gaze was unwavering. Through his calloused hand, Kihachi felt a steady heat pass into his own.  

“When my parents passed away,” Enki continued, “it was the very parishioners who had long drifted away from the temple who came to me with heartfelt concern. They brought vegetables and fish, checked on me, began requesting memorial services and gratitude lectures at our temple again… Through the bond of Amida-sama, I learned just how many people have carried me through life. The parishioners, my family—so many have sustained me.”  

Enki released Kihachi’s hand and offered an exceedingly gentle smile.  

“Perhaps this is what Amida-sama’s other-power truly seeks to convey.”  

Kihachi felt his chest tighten with emotion—when suddenly, “Kihachi-niichan!” came a bright voice as Yajirō peeked innocently through the sliding door, followed closely by Ito, Mitsu, and Sadakichi tumbling in together.  

“What are you lot doing? All piling in like this?”  

“I brought ’em,” said Bunshichi, strolling in with a carefree look, as Ito puffed out her chest proudly.  

“We came to visit the grave of Matsuhira. Since Yajirō’d be lonely, we’re all staying over at his place tonight. Right?”  

Mitsu and Sadakichi nodded vigorously, while Yajirō flustered, “Wait—nobody asked me!”  

Seeing them as unchanged as ever, Kihachi couldn’t help but smile. After the children filed into the back room, Bunshichi greeted Enki with a casual, “Brother, long time no see,” and settled down beside him.  

“Bunshichi, don’t you think it’s about time you went to the bathhouse? You’re covered in dandruff again.”  

2.

You’re covered in dandruff again.”

When the two lit their cigarettes, the smoke they exhaled curled together in the air.

“More importantly, Kihachi, did you find the *Denki Mokuroku*?”

Without a word, Kihachi handed the *Denki Mokuroku* to Bunshichi, who flipped through it with one hand holding his cigarette. As Kihachi recounted everything that had happened, Bunshichi let out a low “Hm,” opened the page of *Denki Nishiki*, and traced the text with his finger.

“Hey, have either of you ever seen the notes for *Denki Nishiki*?”

Seeing both Enki and Bunshichi shake their heads, Kihachi slumped his shoulders.

“…I got teased endlessly by my older brother when I was alive. Now, even after death, I’m still being dragged around like this.”

“Listen, Kihachi,” Bunshichi said, his face deadly serious.

“Seiroku’s an awkward man—more than you realize. He hardly ever shows what he truly feels. But in his own way, he always cared about you.”

“Then why’d he open the forbidden shrine? Why’d he trample on the one thing I believed in?”

“To you, Seiroku might seem like a man who does whatever he pleases. But opening Akubi-sama’s shrine—maybe he had no choice. Maybe there was some unavoidable reason.”

“You say it like it’s none of your business. That shrine meant something to my uncle too, didn’t it?”

“Yeah,” Bunshichi said, glancing up at the *kamidana*. “Your mother stood under the waterfall at Inariyama, ran the pilgrimage path all night long—she prayed desperately for you, weak as you were. For your sake, she received the *bunrei* of Akubi Ōmyōjin through ascetic practice, and had it sealed inside a *zushi*.”

---

2.

“Zushi…?” Kihachi felt a sense of unease. A *zushi* was simply a small container with hinged doors like those of a Kannon statue—hardly an appropriate term for the forbidden shrine, which resembled a miniature version of a full Shinto hall.

“When we tried to carry that *zushi* home on our backs,” Bunshichi said, glancing up at the *kamidana*, “I tripped along the way and left a large gash right on the front. Repairing the damage would’ve been difficult, and leaving it as-is would’ve looked unsightly. So we decided instead to cover the entire wound by enclosing the *zushi* within another case. We kept this a secret between just me and Okan—family would only worry otherwise.”

Then Bunshichi pointed directly at the forbidden shrine.

“So what you see now—the shrine itself—is actually an outer box built to protect the *zushi* inside.”

“Such a thing…” Enki murmured in astonishment, while Kihachi stood frozen, dumbstruck.

“Kihachi?” Bunshichi called out. Kihachi sprang to his feet as if jolted, fetched a footstool from the corner of the room, and placed it beneath the *kamidana*, then stepped onto it with trembling legs.

“You don’t mean to—” Enki gasped, eyes wide. Kei and Yamazaki, startled, hurried into the inner chamber.

His heart pounded; tension surged through him. This was absurd—he should stop now. And yet, driven by a conviction stronger than fear, Kihachi stretched out his shaking fingers and undid the small clasp on the front door of the shrine.

His heart beat harder than it ever had before. He was terrified beyond measure.

But the certainty he carried outweighed even that dread. Still unsteady in spirit, he steeled himself.

Slowly, he placed his hands on the shrine’s doors—and flung them open with force.

Peering cautiously inside, he saw deep within the shrine a small, dark, upright *zushi*, solemnly enshrined.

2.

There were deep scratches on the latticed *kōshibai* doors, and a heavy-looking fastener secured them shut.

This must be it—the true forbidden shrine, just as Bunshichi had described.

Leaning against the *zushi* inside was a bundle of papers labeled “Notes on *Denki Nishiki*.”

“This must be it…”

Kihachi reached for the bundle and immediately began flipping through it. This time, there was no doubt—he had found what he sought.

Glancing back into the shrine, he noticed other items crammed inside: wax cylinders and picture postcards—Seiroku’s personal belongings.

“Did Brother know about this forbidden shrine?”

Suddenly, something caught his eye: a small wooden box, no bigger than the palm of a hand, placed alongside the rest.

“What’s this…?”

A note on the box’s lid read, “From Santakurō-san.” Kihachi’s heart stirred violently.

He opened the lid. Inside were stacked *genjōban*—magic lantern slides. He pulled one out and peered closely at it.

“No way… could this be…”

As Kihachi stepped down from the footstool, he found Yajirō Yamazaki standing behind him, holding a magic lantern.

“You’ll need this. I fetched it from the closet over there.”

Handing over the device with a carefree grin, Yamazaki added, “But in return, you’ve got to let us see those interesting-looking slides.”

Behind him, the children of the Night Study Meeting were snickering mischievously.

“What a sly one you are,” Kihachi muttered with an amused smile. He took the lantern and inserted the oil lamp from the inner room.

A plain, round patch of light appeared on the darkened wall.

From the box, he selected the first *genjōban* slide and slid it into the lantern.

“*Number Twenty: Denki Mokuroku*.”

2.

Suddenly, characters appeared in the light.

Trembling, Kihachi swapped out the *genjōban* slide, and an image was projected of a man wearing a glowing Inverness cape. After staring at it for several seconds, Kihachi gasped and pulled the *Denki Mokuroku* from his pocket, flipping open its first page.

“*Number One: Yoru Shirazu*… Garment of Light. Walk the night roads without carrying a lamp.”

He replaced the slide with the next one.

“*Number Two: Shashinki*… A photograph taken together with ancestral spirits or family members made visible by electric light.”

This time, the image showed people joyfully posing for a commemorative photo alongside translucent ghosts.

“I told you, didn’t I? That man never stopped worrying about you.”

Bunshichi had somehow appeared beside him and spoke in a solemn voice.

“Seiroku regretted leaving you behind at the fair. Seeing how you’d been ruined by the darkness—it tormented him. He’s wanted to make amends ever since.”

Bunshichi narrowed his eyes, gazing at the magic lantern. “That’s why he made this.”

“So that even if you were trapped in darkness, you could still read the *Denki Mokuroku*.”

“…So all this time, he kept the *Denki Mokuroku* from me… just to make this? No matter how much I resented him—no matter what cruel things I said—he stayed silent and hid it all along…?”

“Don’t screw with me,” Kihachi muttered, his anger flaring.

“If he was making this lantern, he could’ve just told me! What was there to hide—”

He cut himself off mid-sentence, his eyes falling on the label pasted to the box: *"From Santakurō-san."*

2.

Long ago, when he had said he couldn’t believe in his older brother, what was it that Seiroku had replied?

──Then, if Santakurō brings you the *Denki Mokuroku*, will you believe in me?

Kihachi pulled out several *genjōban*. They must have been commissioned from a specialist magic lantern artist—the drawings were delicate, clearly painstakingly crafted over long hours. “No way,” Kihachi murmured, lifting his gaze.

If this had already been completed just before Seiroku departed for war—

“Was opening the forbidden shrine that day really about hiding the completed *Twentieth Century Denki Mokuroku*?”

So that, upon returning from the war, he could present it to Kihachi as a gift from Santakurō.

For nothing more than this one reason—to make Kihachi believe.

“I always thought my big brother looked down on me as a fool…”

“No… no, that’s not it, Kihachi.”

Bunshichi spoke up hesitantly, his face heavy with sorrow.

“It was Seiroku who made it possible for you to attend middle school.”

“Middle school…? But my uncle paid the tuition, didn’t he?”

Instead of Bunshichi, Enki spoke quietly. “Kihachi.”

“Think. Why would Seiroku, who was ready to elope with the Hundred Rivers’ daughter, so easily let go of her?”

Kihachi could not answer. He shook his head slowly.

“At the time of the fair, Hundred Rivers approached Seiroku with a contract: *‘I’ll pay for Kihachi’s middle school tuition, on the condition that no one from the Sakamoto family ever involves themselves with Hyakugawa Shuzō again.’* I only learned of this after the war ended. One day, Hundred Rivers showed me the contract.”

---

2.

“That means… the one who paid my tuition wasn’t my uncle…”

“It was Mr. Hyakugawa,” Bunshichi replied.

“I’d heard about the school fees from Seiroku, but he made me promise not to tell—said he didn’t want Kihachi to feel awkward and fall behind in his studies… I’m sorry I kept quiet all this time.”

*What the hell is this?* Kihachi repeated in his mind, over and over. His limbs felt heavy, and his mouth wouldn’t move properly.

The truth of that night at the fair—the fact that his very life now was built on Seiroku’s giving up Noriko—pierced Kihachi’s chest like a knife, leaving him utterly speechless.

“I’ll continue reading,” Bunshichi said, gently taking the *Denki Mokuroku* from Kihachi, who stood frozen.

“*Number Three: Denpō*… Transporting humans at the speed of lightning.”

Reading with deliberate cheerfulness, Bunshichi advanced the magic lantern. One by one, new幻燈 slides appeared: flowers blooming year-round through electricity, mechanical puppets performing automated plays, machines that automatically cooked all kinds of dishes—children clapped and cheered in delight.

Suddenly, a piercing cry shattered the lively room. Kihachi turned toward the sound—and saw Enki collapsed face-down on the tatami, weeping uncontrollably. Kei, her own cheeks wet with tears, was gently stroking his trembling back.

It was a shocking sight. Kihachi had always believed—his father was not a man who cried.

Kihachi turned back to the magic lantern—just as the final幻燈 slide appeared before his eyes.

“*Number Twenty: Denki Nishiki*… Pungent and dry—truly like electricity.”

---

2.

A pair of figures—a man and a woman—drinking together from the same cup. Their faces were blurred, but it was clearly a picture brimming with happiness.

Seeing this radiant vision of Roku’s electric paradise shining before his eyes, the words spoken to him by Roku on the train suddenly came rushing back into Kihachi’s mind.

 —You’re going to create paradise with electricity, aren’t you?

Driven by some irresistible force, Kihachi yanked the lamp out of the magic lantern.

“I’ll be right back.”

“Now? Where’re you going?” Bunshichi asked, frowning in suspicion.

“To a friend’s house. I need to confirm where Inako’s wedding ceremony will be held tomorrow.”

Outside, as expected, it was pitch black. But Kihachi took a deep breath, steeled himself, and broke into a run.

He passed through a village where faint light spilled from homes, then emerged onto a narrow field path. The night sky was overcast, with only occasional stars peeking through scattered gaps in the clouds.

To his left should have been Lake Biwa, but he could make out only a few sparse lights across the water—the boundary between shore and lake indistinguishable. To his right, the looming shadows of mountains loomed with an eerie, oppressive presence.

Trying hard not to look around, he kept running—and gradually, the distant glow of Ōtsu city began drawing nearer ahead.

Perhaps it was the relief of seeing those lights—

His foot caught on something, and he tumbled forward headlong.

The extinguished lantern slipped from his hand; he heard it land in a rice paddy. Instantly, the world plunged into total darkness. Sweat burst from every pore. His breath came ragged and shallow; his limbs felt numb and powerless.

Kihachi struggled desperately to steady his mind, but fear and dread overwhelmed him. He collapsed where he lay, crouched motionless on the ground.

2.

Not looking around, he kept running—and gradually, the distant glow of Ōtsu city began drawing nearer ahead. Perhaps it was the relief of seeing those lights—his foot caught on something, and he tumbled forward headlong. The extinguished lantern slipped from his hand; he heard it land in a rice paddy.
