Kihachi struggled desperately to steady his mind, but fear and dread overwhelmed him. He collapsed where he lay, crouched motionless on the ground.

So darkness really was impossible after all? Even now, he despised himself for being so disgracefully afraid of the dark.

Just as Kihachi felt an almost overwhelming urge to burst into tears, a gentle light from above softly illuminated him.

Looking up, wondering what the light could be, he saw the moon emerging from behind the clouds—a perfect circle, like the beam of a flashlight. The moon’s glow, with its rabbit diligently pounding rice cakes, was serene, slowly loosening the tension frozen within Kihachi’s heart. Kihachi took another deep breath.

Having regained his composure, Kihachi slowly stood up and stepped forward once more.

　　　　　　　　　　＊

In Hachiman, Inako heard from Yōsuke about Noriko and Seiroku’s relationship, and about the agreement Seiroku had supposedly made with Jinbei’emon.

“If Sakamoto-kun interferes any further with the Hyakugawa family,” Yōsuke said, “he might not be allowed to attend middle school anymore.”

Kihachi was destined to create Paradise through electricity and eventually save countless people.

Someone like herself must not stand in his way—resolved Inako, deciding then and there to end her journey.

The day after returning from Hachiman, she sat blankly in her room when Jinbei’emon entered cheerfully.

“Well then, tomorrow’s the big day—the wedding ceremony with Dr. Paikaru!”

Once tomorrow arrived, there would be no turning back. Yet because she had faintly entertained the idea of escape until then, she carelessly let slip: “I wonder… if this is truly for the best.”

2.

Jinbei’emon slapped his hand against the tatami, and Inako flinched. Yet he did not grow angrier. Instead, his face softened into a pleasant smile as he began, “There’s something I’d like to tell you.”

“Inako, how do you suppose I found out about your connection with Noriko and Sakamoto’s eldest son?”

Inako paused briefly in thought before replying, “Did you hire a detective?”

“That’s right—exactly a detective. An exceptionally capable one. And this detective, being an honest sort, once told one of the maids who was close to him at the time: ‘You should believe in God more. Receive letters from God, just like your older sister.’”

Inako, who had been listening intently, suddenly felt every hair on her body rise as memories from the past surged violently back into her mind.

Her body trembled minutely. Her vision wavered.

“The maid, finding this suspicious, came to consult me. —Aren’t you, Detective?”

A high-pitched scream tore through the room. A shriek like that of a demon—she could hardly believe it was coming from her own mouth. If she didn’t keep screaming, her chest felt as though it would be torn apart from within.

Until now, she had lived believing her suffering was utterly unjust. She hadn’t done anything wrong, hadn’t made a single mistake—so why must she endure such torment?

But she was wrong. She, too, had wounded others—her own sister. She had callously trampled upon another’s happiness and still dared to hope for Paradise. This hell she now endured was the punishment God had given her.

The scream subsided. Inako collapsed forward onto the tatami, her body crumpled face-down. Jinbei’emon’s emotionless hand reached out and stroked her back.

2.

Her scream echoed, her chest feeling as though it would be torn apart from within. Until now, she had lived convinced that her suffering was utterly unjust. She hadn’t done anything wrong, hadn’t made a single mistake—so why must she endure such torment? But she was wrong. She, too, had wounded others—her own sister. She had callously trampled upon another’s happiness and still dared to hope for Paradise. This hell she now endured was the punishment God had given her. The scream subsided. Inako collapsed forward onto the tatami, her body crumpled face-down. Jinbei’emon’s emotionless hand reached out and stroked her back.

“Don’t blame yourself. If anything, you’ve done well. Your strength has always been your blind obedience—following others as faithfully as one worships God. Dr. Paikaru has status, power, and the will to act. There can be no mistake in what he says. Rest assured and entrust yourself to him completely, and you will live a happy life.”

Jinbei’emon stood up and turned to leave the room. “I’m counting on you tomorrow.”

“…Where exactly is the wedding ceremony to be held?”

Inako asked in a dry, lifeless voice. Jinbei’emon turned back, a sly grin lifting the corners of his mouth.

“Over Lake Biwa.”

　　　　　　　　　　Eight

A chartered steamship, newly put into service this month, floated upon the surface of Lake Biwa.

—Why go to such great lengths to celebrate something as simple as registering a marriage?

Under Yōsuke’s direction, there had been no formal engagement gifts, no visits of greeting to either family. Perhaps because the gathering had originally been planned as a celebration for Yōsuke’s appointment as branch manager, no formalities such as mediators or ceremonial roles were observed. After a brief address from Yōsuke, the guests—men and women dressed casually in unadorned *kasuri* kimono or lightweight *ro* haori fastened with *himo* sashes—fell freely into idle chatter and began sampling the Western liquor and cuisine laid out on the tables.

Inako sat in her usual dark blue *kasuri* kimono, perched stiffly on a chair. She did not touch the food on the table, instead gazing listlessly around the deck. Most of the attendees were employees from the Mitsue Mizoe Shop’s Fushimi branch or workers from sake breweries affiliated with it. There was no sign of anyone from the main Mitsue family, not even Saki.

2.

The guests—men and women dressed casually in unadorned *kasuri* kimono or lightweight *ro* haori fastened with *himo* sashes—fell freely into idle chatter and began sampling the Western liquor and cuisine laid out on the tables. Inako sat in her usual dark blue *kasuri* kimono, perched stiffly on a chair. She did not touch the food on the table, instead gazing listlessly around the deck. Most of the attendees were employees from the Mitsue Mizoe Shop’s Fushimi branch or workers from sake breweries affiliated with it. There was no sign of anyone from the main Mitsue family, not even Saki.

Meanwhile, Inako felt dejected at the thought of how lightly her marriage was being treated, while Jinbei’emon was in high spirits, going around pouring drinks for everyone. Roku stood leaning against the railing, his expression stern as he stared out over Lake Biwa, and Noriko wore a forced, social smile as she exchanged greetings with those around her.

Suddenly, Inako’s eyes met Noriko’s as the latter turned toward her, and she quickly looked down in panic.

Since returning to Fushimi, Inako had been kept in a state bordering on house arrest, so she had barely exchanged words with Noriko beyond perfunctory greetings—and she was grateful for it.

"Poor Yōsuke-kun. It’s a real shame. In the end, he couldn’t bring the *Denki Mokuroku* in time."

The voice came from Yōsuke himself, his face already flushed from drinking, addressing Inako, who sat there like an empty husk.

"The *Denki Mokuroku* is unfortunate, but don’t worry. I’ll get it eventually—no matter what."

She was to become this man’s wife. She had made that decision, had steeled herself for it. Yet that resolve felt as flimsy as a stick thrust into mud.

The summer sun beat down mercilessly, yet strangely, she felt no heat. A suffocating tightness had gripped her since morning. This unpleasant tension reminded her of the moment before receiving an injection at the hospital.

"Well then, now that the mood’s warmed up, it’s time I gave this to you."

When she saw the pearl ring resting in Yōsuke’s open palm, every organ in her body recoiled in dread.

She had steeled herself. She had accepted living in hell.

"Quickly. Stand up."

2.

She had steeled herself for it. Yet that resolve felt as flimsy as a stick thrust into mud. The summer sun beat down mercilessly, yet strangely, she felt no heat. A suffocating tightness had gripped her since morning. This unpleasant tension reminded her of the moment before receiving an injection at the hospital. "Well then, now that the mood’s warmed up, it’s time I gave this to you." When she saw the pearl ring resting in Yōsuke’s open palm, every organ in her body recoiled in dread. She had steeled herself. She had accepted living in hell. "Quickly. Stand up."

Yōsuke grasped Inako’s hand; reflexively, she pulled away. For a fleeting moment, Yōsuke grimaced, but then twisted his mouth into a sneer and, gripping her wrist tightly, tried to force her to her feet.

“I don’t want to—” she whispered—and in that instant, finally understood.

She wasn’t ready at all.

Somewhere deep inside her heart, she had always believed—still believed—that he would come to save her.

Impatient, Yōsuke yanked her upright and called out to the gathered guests.

“Ladies and gentlemen. From this moment—”

“Hey, some weird-looking boat’s comin’ this way!”

A man gazing out over Lake Biwa cut him off with a drunken slur. Someone else rushed to the railing: “Looks like a damn catfish!” Inako broke free from Yōsuke and stumbled toward the railing, catching sight of a white vessel approaching with a rhythmic *pom-pom-pom* drone.

A familiar boat latched onto the stern of the chartered vessel, and two men stepped aboard.

One was Yajirō, oddly cradling a *roukan* phonograph. And the other—

“Kihachi-san—!”

Kihachi met Inako’s eyes and grinned proudly.

Ignoring Jinbei’emon’s stunned glare—“What are *you* doing here?”—Kihachi strode straight up to Yōsuke, who stood haughtily before him.

“I don’t recall inviting you.”

“I’ve brought what was promised,” Kihachi replied, holding out a paulownia box tied with cord.  
“I have delivered the *Denki Mokuroku*.”

　　　　　　　　　　＊

2.

The gathered crowd murmured in confusion, and beside Roku, who stood with arms crossed and a severe expression, Noriko anxiously pressed a hand to her chest. Jinbei’emon’s face twisted with a mix of fury and shock, as if he might lunge at any moment.

“So it’s really been found?”

“Yes. It includes the precise method for making *Denki Nishiki*, the secret reserve sake.”

Without dropping his haughty demeanor, Yōsuke glanced toward the paulownia box Kihachi held. “Oh?”  

“*Denki Nishiki*, you say?!” Jinbei’emon’s shrill roar pierced the air.

“Monk—where did you find that?”

“From the man you once betrayed,” Kihachi replied bluntly. Jinbei’emon snapped his mouth shut as if rebuked.

Yōsuke reached to inspect the box, but Kihachi stopped him. “Before verification,” he said, “please declare here and now the cancellation of Inako’s engagement, as promised.”

“…What are you talking about?”

Yōsuke wore an infuriatingly blank expression.

“You keep saying ‘promise, promise,’ but I have no recollection of any such thing.”

As the onlookers stirred restlessly, Kihachi stood calm, his silence charged with quiet anger. *What kind of upbringing could produce someone capable of such audacity without flinching?*

Even as he burned with indignation, Kihachi also felt pity for Yōsuke.

Suddenly, a distorted, crackling voice echoed quietly through the air—Yōsuke’s voice, laced with static. Everyone froze in stunned silence.

“‘Who’d want to marry a woman who’s useless alive? Only a man like me, who enjoys such things. This marriage is charity, you know. Don’t you think I’m admirable?’”

2.

“‘Promise, promise,’ but I have no recollection of any such thing.” As the onlookers stirred restlessly, Kihachi stood calm, his silence charged with quiet anger. *What kind of upbringing could produce someone capable of such audacity without flinching?* Even as he burned with indignation, Kihachi also felt pity for Yōsuke. Suddenly, a distorted, crackling voice echoed quietly through the air—Yōsuke’s voice, laced with static. Everyone froze in stunned silence.  
“‘Who’d want to marry a woman who’s useless alive? Only a man like me, who enjoys such things. This marriage is charity, you know. Don’t you think I’m admirable?’”

『──Inako-chan may seem like a good girl, but she’s surprisingly arrogant. For someone so incompetent, she dares to pick and choose men just like that.』

“This is—” For the first time, Yōsuke’s face twisted in shock.

Kihachi turned around—and there was Yamajiro, calmly operating the *roukan* phonograph as if nothing were amiss.

“When… since when?” Yōsuke stared at the machine. Yamajiro stuck his tongue out.

*This guy really misses nothing,* Kihachi thought, nearly laughing.

Back when Yōsuke had made dealings with Hōrai Butsugu-ten, Yamajiro, who owned the phonograph, had recorded their conversation—just in case.

『If you can prepare the *Denki Mokuroku* before the wedding, I’ll call off my engagement to Inako-chan.』

『Then by the deadline, I shall deliver the *Denki Mokuroku* to Dr. Paikaru.』

“Hah! Nicely recorded,” Yamajiro said with satisfaction, nodding. All eyes in the room turned toward Yōsuke.

“…That’s not it. It was just… something I said to a child—a joke.”

The crowd’s gaze, already uneasy, now began to brim with suspicion. But right then, Jinbei’emon let out a loud laugh that seemed to blow the tension away. He pointed his cane at the paulownia box.  
“The *Denki Nishiki* inside isn’t some secret reserve sake or anything special—it’s merely a failed batch.”

Another murmur rippled through the assembly. At this point, hadn’t everyone had enough surprises?

“In other words… it’s unsellable?” Yōsuke asked, eyes wide with disbelief.

“Exactly. The brewing method written there is a failure. It’s nothing more than a mere trial record.”

2.

“I said to a child—a joke.” The crowd’s gaze, already uneasy, now began to brim with suspicion. But right then, Jinbei’emon let out a loud laugh that seemed to blow the tension away. He pointed his cane at the paulownia box. “The *Denki Nishiki* inside isn’t some secret reserve sake or anything special—it’s merely a failed batch.” Another murmur rippled through the assembly. At this point, hadn’t everyone had enough surprises? “In other words… it’s unsellable?” Yōsuke asked, eyes wide with disbelief. “Exactly. The brewing method written there is a failure. It’s nothing more than a mere trial record.”

Jinbei’emon proudly boasted, puffing out his chest. He was the first person anyone had ever seen who spoke so cheerfully about his own failure.

“But regardless of the contents, I’ve fulfilled my promise by delivering the agreed-upon item,” he declared.

Kihachi tried to object, but Jinbei’emon fixed him with a ferocious glare.

“You… if you keep stirring up trouble for the Hyakugawa family, you won’t live to regret it.”

“I can’t pay your school fees anymore,” Kihachi replied flatly.

“That’s right. Do you intend to throw away your brother’s dying wish—to see you attend middle school?”

“It doesn’t matter.”

Kihachi cut him off sharply.

“My brother wanted me to create Paradise with electricity.”

Jinbei’emon gaped, dumbfounded—then slowly deepened every wrinkle on his face, grinding his teeth in frustration.

“It can’t be helped!” pleaded Inako, flustered. “You’re going to study electricity, aren’t you? Then staying as you are should be fine!”

“If I set my mind to it, I can study anywhere,” Kihachi shot back.

“…Why—” Inako broke down, her voice trembling with sobs. “Why would you do this… for someone like me? …I’m only the kind of person who brings misfortune to others…”

“You know I hate the dark.”

Inako blinked, startled. Just as he had resolved to speak, sudden shyness overtook him.

“Without light, I suffer. Without you alive beside me, I suffer.”

Kihachi smiled awkwardly, yet still managed a proud little grin.

“If you’re not laughing beside me, life would be too dark to bear.”

2.

Tears welled in Inako’s eyes, and she covered her mouth with both hands.

After a moment, she wiped her tears with the back of her hand, turned forward resolutely, and took one firm step ahead.

“Wait—surely you’re not choosing a man like that, just because of pretty words!”

Yōsuke tried to stop her, his voice rising shrilly.

“If it were me, I could buy you pearls and jewels by the handful! Women love shiny things, don’t they?”

Inako stepped beside Kihachi, then turned back and smiled mischievously. “Yes, I do love shiny things.”

“The light from electric lamps sparkles too, doesn’t it?”

The onlookers, who had until then remained silent, burst into laughter at her words.

Humiliated, Yōsuke looked as though he might burst into tears at any moment.

“Shut up!”

Jinbei’emon bellowed, and the entire crowd fell instantly silent. He snatched the paulownia box from Yōsuke’s hands.

“Roku, grab that kid and hold him down!”

Roku, who had been standing with arms crossed, shot Kihachi a sharp, piercing glare.

“Jinbei’emon-san, please show mercy!” came a cry from the crowd, followed by another jeer: “That’s despicable!”  
“Shut up!” Jinbei’emon roared once more.

“I am her father. I know better than anyone how my daughters can live happily.”

He turned triumphantly toward Kihachi. “Negotiations are broken off, then.”

With that, he untied the box’s cord and flung the lid open with force.

“Indeed, negotiations have failed.”

But as Jinbei’emon proudly peered inside the box, his eyes flew wide in shock.

---

2.

“Then I’ll just take Inako and go on a *nukemairi*,” Kihachi declared.

Inside the box, tightly packed like gray *inarizushi*, were creatures that, upon closer inspection, squirmed individually, their fur rippling with tiny movements.

As one of them squeaked, “Chuu!”, all the mice burst from the box in unison.

It was Jinbei’emon who let out a blood-curdling scream that echoed to the heavens at the sight of the swarm.

Roku, arms still crossed, shot Kihachi a sharp, piercing glare. “Jinbei’emon-san, please show mercy!” someone cried from the crowd, followed by another jeer: “That’s despicable!”  
“Shut up!” Jinbei’emon roared once more. “I am her father. I know better than anyone how my daughters can live happily.” He turned triumphantly toward Kihachi. “Negotiations are broken off, then.” With that, he untied the box’s cord and flung the lid open with force. “Indeed, negotiations have failed.”

But as Jinbei’emon proudly peered inside the box, his eyes flew wide in shock.

When Inako had once asked why Jinbei’emon had lost his composure chasing mice through the Ōtsu *ryōtei*, he explained it was due to the Hyakugawa Rice Riots—an infestation at the sake brewery that left him with an unshakable hatred. “Ever since, I can’t stand the sight of them—I chase every mouse I see without fail.”

The mice gathered overnight by the Night Study Meeting children were so numerous even Kihachi winced at the sight.

Amid shrieks of panic—“They’re under the desk!” “They’ve gone into someone’s *hakama!*”—Jinbei’emon raged, wildly swinging his cane, scattering people far more effectively than any mouse.

“Plague! The plague will spread!”

As chaos erupted, Yōsuke leaned heavily against the railing, visibly shaken. Kihachi and Inako approached him.

“You look like you’re having a rough time,” Kihachi said, annoyingly cheerful.

Yōsuke, hair disheveled, shot him a fierce glare. “Do you realize what kind of punishment awaits you for treating me like this?”

“What kind of punishment?” Inako replied coolly, effortlessly grabbing a mouse through her handkerchief and moving it toward Yōsuke.

“Hey—stop, stop it!”

2.

Cornered inch by inch, Yōsuke stepped onto the railing and began scrambling backward up the handrail.

“Kihachi-kun, don’t just stand there—do something!”

“Understood.”

Kihachi grabbed both of Yōsuke’s legs and hoisted him upward in one swift motion.

Off-balance, Yōsuke toppled backward and plunged headfirst into Lake Biwa.

“I can’t swim—!” His final cry vanished with the splash.

“There. I did something.”

“Kihachi-kun, quickly!”  
Stuart-san called from his boat. Beside him stood Yajirō, already grinning and waving them over.

First, they helped Inako aboard the unsteadily moored vessel. As Kihachi prepared to follow,  
“Wait, you damn brat!” came a roar from behind—Jinbei’emon charging forward.  

Confronting Kihachi, he swung his cane down mercilessly.  
Kihachi barely blocked it with the flashlight drawn from his coat; the force jolted through his arm, and the flashlight was knocked away, bouncing across the deck before shattering into lens fragments that tumbled into the lake.  

Kihachi fell hard onto his backside, suppressing a surge of nausea as he glared up at Jinbei’emon.  
“That flashlight… cost more than a month’s wages.”  

“I’ll pay whatever it takes!”  

Jinbei’emon, eyes wide with fury, raised his cane again and brought it down with full force—  
but suddenly, a thick arm shot in from the side. The supposedly sturdy cane snapped cleanly in two.  
“Aaah!” cried Yajirō, the bamboo-whip craftsman, as if wounded himself.  

“You…”

“Fine by me.”  

Roku seized Jinbei’emon by the collar and lightly threw him in a back suplex.  

Jinbei’emon crashed onto the floor, letting out a muffled “Guhh!”  

“I’ve always charged ahead like a steam engine—that’s just who I am.”  

Noriko ran over and looked down at Jinbei’emon, sprawled on the floor and dazed. Her face paled.  
“Roku-san… was that okay? Doing something like this…”  

“It’s definitely become a mess. But I don’t want to run from you anymore.”  

Roku swept Noriko up into his arms with bold, unhesitating strength.  

“Then let’s run away—together.”  

As Roku boarded the boat, holding a blushing Noriko whose head had turned bright red, the boat pulled away from the chartered vessel. The commotion with the mice on deck hadn’t yet settled. Roku set Noriko down and grimaced at the lingering chaos.  

“Wonder if they’re still buying mice? I wonder how many scoops of ice cream we could get for catching one…”  

“It was all worth it…” murmured Yajirō weakly, watching his prized product snapped in two.  

Inside the boat, Inako sat facing Noriko, her expression once again clouded with sorrow.  

“Inako, what’s wrong since yesterday? You’ve been avoiding me, haven’t you?”  

Noriko gently placed her hand over Inako’s, hesitating, then softened her lips into a gentle smile.  

Inako opened her mouth, tears welling, and whispered, “I… did something terrible.”  

2.

Roku set Noriko down and grimaced at the lingering chaos. “Wonder if they’re still buying mice? I wonder how many scoops of ice cream we could get for catching one…”

Noriko gently placed her hand over Inako’s, hesitated, then softened her lips into a gentle smile.  

Inako opened her mouth, tears welling, and whispered, “I… did something terrible.”  

“My sister’s letter to the gods—because of me, Father found out about it, and so…”  

Noriko immediately seemed to understand what she meant.  

“I’m sorry, I’m sorry. Please don’t hate me,” Inako sobbed, and Noriko pulled her into an embrace.  

“He would’ve found out sooner or later. It’s okay—I won’t start hating you over something like this. You’re my precious little sister. And I believe in you, Inako.”  

When they parted, Noriko wiped away Inako’s tears with her fingertips. “Alright,” Inako said, smiling. “I’ll believe. Believing is what I’m good at.”  

Waiting until Inako had calmed, Kihachi handed her the notes on *Denki Nishiki* he’d found in the forbidden shrine.  

“This is *Denki Nishiki*,” Inako said, quickly scanning the contents as she wiped her tears.  

But as she read further, her eyes grew dim.  

“This is the sake I brewed when I entered the kōjimuro. Just like Father said—it’s a failure.”  

Seeing Inako’s spirits sink, Kihachi spoke up. “About that *Denki Nishiki*… there’s something I want to ask of you, Inako. Will you listen?”  

Dazed at first, Inako gradually brightened, and finally smiled and nodded.  

After being dropped off by their pilot at Yabase Port, the group made their way to Kusatsu Station, where they parted ways: Stuart-san returned home, while Yajirō—who had shop duties—had to leave them as well.  

“Here, brother. The phonograph.”  

Kihachi took the carry bag containing the phonograph from Yajirō.

2.

The clattering sound from inside the bag made Roku tilt his head, puzzled. “You’re carrying quite a load.”

“There’s also the *roukan* my older brother hid in the forbidden shrine,” Kihachi replied. “Since it was already a fair amount of luggage, I figured I might as well bring it along and listen when I have time. But hey, Yajirō—”

Kihachi reached over and ruffled Yajirō’s hair affectionately.

“I owe you one. Thanks a lot.”

Embarrassed by the sincere praise, Yajirō mumbled, “Just look forward to hearing all the stories,” and ducked behind Stuart-san. Stuart stepped forward and smiled at Kihachi.

“I’ve acted like a reckless youth at my age. Maybe Seiroku-kun’s personality is rubbing off on me.”

As Stuart extended his right hand, Kihachi shook it with a warm smile.

“I’m truly sorry for the trouble. I’ll make it up to you another time.”

“No need for thanks. In return, come visit Hachiman sometime. Let’s sing together again.”

Kihachi and the others boarded the Kansai Railway train bound for the Mie Prefecture. As the train pulled away, Stuart and Yajirō remained on the platform, waving until they disappeared from view. Outside the window, the peaceful scenery of mountain valleys drifted by for a while.

“It’d be nice if the four of us could just live together somewhere far away.”

Inako spoke softly, her eyes fixed on the passing landscape.

“Living together, all four of us? So does that mean I’d have to call you ‘brother’?”

“Don’t even joke about it—that’s creepy.”

“What, ‘brother’? Are you saying you plan to marry Inako?”  

2.

He boarded the Kansai Railway train bound for Mie Prefecture. As the train pulled away, Stuart and Yajirō remained on the platform, waving until they disappeared from view. Outside the window, the peaceful scenery of mountain valleys drifted by for a while.  

“It’d be nice if the four of us could just live together somewhere far away,” Inako murmured softly, her eyes fixed on the passing landscape.  

“Living together, all four of us? So does that mean I’d have to call you ‘brother’?”  

“Don’t even joke about it—that’s creepy.”  

“What, ‘brother’? Are you saying you plan to marry Inako?”  

Inako blushed instantly, as if a sharp *pon* sound had gone off. Kihachi, feeling his own cheeks grow warm, stammered, “A-ah, no… that’s not what I meant—” trying to patch things up.  

“Still, we really are like siblings. The way you get all flustered like that is just like Seiroku-san.”  

Smiling, Noriko teased him. Kihachi pouted and shot back, “Well, the way you’re smiling right now looks just like Inako. It’s adorable.”  

This time, it was Noriko’s turn to redden.  

Her mouth flapped open and shut like a fish’s. She narrowed her eyes sharply and jabbed Roku’s side with her elbow.  

“W-what are you doing?”  

“You should say something thoughtful like that too, once in a while.”  

All of them flushed red, as if autumn leaves had come early to that single seat in the train car.  

Kihachi felt a strange emotion stir within him, thinking of how, though he had once believed himself unrelated to Inako and the others, Seiroku had quietly woven invisible threads connecting them all.  

“…Electricity and God—they’re alike, aren’t they?” Kihachi said quietly.  

“Both God and electricity are invisible. But they take visible forms—like electric lights, scriptures, or priests—and become light that illuminates the world. Just as electricity moves machines, God moves people, guiding them toward a better life, toward Paradise. Thinking that way… maybe humans are like electromagnets.”  

“An electromagnet, you say?” Inako seemed to recall the electrical therapy device she’d seen at Stuart’s house.  

2.

A quiet stirring rose within him, thinking of how, though he had once believed himself unrelated to Inako and the others, Seiroku had quietly woven invisible threads connecting them all.  

“…Electricity and God—they’re alike, aren’t they?” Kihachi said quietly. “Both God and electricity are invisible. But they take visible forms—like electric lights, scriptures, or priests—and become light that illuminates the world. Just as electricity moves machines, God moves people, guiding them toward a better life, toward Paradise. Thinking that way… maybe humans are like electromagnets.”  

“An electromagnet, you say?” Inako murmured, recalling the electrical therapy device she’d seen at Stuart-san’s house.  

“A magnet that only gains its magnetic force when electricity flows through it. When the current of God flows into a person, turning them into an electromagnet, they’re drawn together by the invisible magnetic force we call *en*—fate—and form visible bonds, supporting one another.”  

“Perhaps religion and science aren’t so different after all. Maybe both point to the same truth underlying this world.”  

“That kind of world—one without God—sounds frightening,” Noriko said uneasily. “Without that magnetic pull, no one would feel connected. Everyone would just think only of themselves. And yet… living completely alone, without relying on anyone else—it’s impossible.”  

Her words struck deep into Kihachi’s heart.  

Would such a time truly come—a world where God had vanished?  

When society’s scientific technology advanced to its ultimate limit, and humanity lost its desire to become better than it was now… what would people do then? What would become of those whose bodies no longer carried the flow of divine current?  

The train pressed forward, a symbol of civilization’s progress. The charging locomotive raced ahead mercilessly, leaving people behind in its wake.  

“…In a brightly lit place, you can’t see the magic lantern,” Kihachi muttered.  

“When the world becomes so bright by default because of advancing technology… will we no longer be able to see the magic lantern? Will dreams themselves vanish from sight?”  

Inako replied simply, “It’ll be fine, won’t it?” as if it were nothing at all.  

“When that time comes, you just need to make a magic lantern that *can* be seen even in the brightest of places, Kihachi-san.”  

Kihachi stared, dumbfounded—then laughed. “Yeah… I suppose you’re right,” he said, ruffling Inako’s hair roughly.

2.

Before long, the train passed over the mountain pass marking the border and entered Mie Prefecture. The four of them transferred to the Sangū Railway at Tsu Station, bound for Yamada Station—the gateway to the Ise Grand Shrine.

　　　　　　　　　　＊

After disembarking their chartered boat, Jinbei’emon and the drenched Yōsuke boarded a train operated by the Kansai Railway, following closely behind the four. Inside the swaying carriage, Yōsuke kept muttering under his breath, “This wasn’t supposed to happen… this wasn’t supposed to happen,” while Jinbei’emon watched him with growing unease.

The train carrying the two men arrived at Yamada Station. Though visibly exhausted, the moment Yōsuke stepped onto the platform, he clenched his fists and strode forward, shouting, “I’ll humiliate them… I’ll absolutely catch them!”

Tracking them down proved easy. Inako’s short hair stood out unmistakably.

As they questioned station staff and rickshaw pullers gathered outside the station, they soon confirmed the group had headed toward Naikū, the Inner Shrine of Ise. Following the trail, the two arrived at Uji Bridge spanning the Isuzu River. Unlike the vermilion *torii* gate of an Inari Shrine, a plain wooden *torii* stood before the bridge, built in accordance with Japan’s ancient architectural traditions.

Just then, near a teahouse, they spotted the two they’d been searching for finally sitting down on a bench beneath the eaves. Jinbei’emon and Yōsuke quickened their pace, closing in.

“Thought you could escape, you damn fool? Huh?”

But as they drew nearer, Jinbei’emon began to sense something was off. A short-haired woman and a man in shirt and *hakama*—their clothing matched, yes, but both had suspiciously mature builds.

“You two… could it be…”

“Oh my, we’ve been found out, haven’t we, Roku-san?”

Seated on the bench were Noriko, wearing the dark blue *kasuri* kimono that belonged to Inako, and Roku, who—perhaps having bought a change of clothes along the way—was dressed much like Kihachi. But more than that—

“Noriko… your… hair…”

With trembling fingers, Jinbei’emon pointed at Noriko’s hair.

“When I told the hairdresser in Tsu, ‘Make it just like my little sister,’ she was completely taken aback.”

“I can’t believe you cut it that short.”

Noriko’s long black hair had been trimmed short, just like Inako’s, neatly following the line of her collar.

“This Twentieth Century Hair isn’t so bad, though. It’s cool. Maybe I’ll keep it like this through the summer.”

“I think you look better with long hair.”

“Oh, how kind of you to say.”

Watching their easy, relaxed banter, Jinbei’emon felt dizzy.

“So you two… you actually counted on me searching for you based on Inako’s appearance…”

“Well, we’re father and daughter. We know you quite well, Father.”

Jinbei’emon’s consciousness began to fade, and he collapsed right where he stood.

　　　　　　　　　　＊

Jinbei’emon quickly came to, then hurried back along the path with Yōsuke in tow.

Meanwhile, Roku sat on the bench, watching the passersby. He glanced beside him and met Noriko’s eyes.

Her gaze was clear, as if it could swallow everything into its depths. Drawn by that look, he found himself saying, without even thinking, “There’s something I want to tell you.” Noriko tilted her head slightly in response.

2.

It felt as though Noriko would vanish from his life forever, and there was something he’d long hesitated to tell her.

But now that he had exposed himself so cowardly, there was no point in hiding it anymore.

"Actually… I have a message from Seiroku."

It had happened while they were marching toward Nanzan after landing on the Liaodong Peninsula.

"You once said to me, 'Noriko is like a little sister,' right?" Seiroku had suddenly remarked as they walked side by side.

"That was half a lie. Watching Noriko grow up over time, I gradually began to see her not as a sister—but as a woman. By the time I realized it myself, it was already too late."

Seiroku chuckled softly and nudged Roku’s shoulder.

"I'm not blaming you. If it's you she chose, I can accept it. At least I got to see your miserable face."

"Ngh! You’ve got a twisted sense of humor, you bastard."

"Says the one just like me. Besides, she came to see me before I left. Just being able to give her what I wanted to hand over—that’s more than enough for me."

After cackling lightly, Seiroku clapped his hands together as if struck by an idea.

"Ah—there is one thing I want you to pass on to her… if you make it back alive."

"Don’t talk such ill-omened nonsense."

"No, it’s a prayer. Think of it this way—it’ll ensure you *do* come back alive."

Roku sighed. "...Fine. Let’s hear it."

"Think of our lord as ice cream."

"...What in the world is that supposed to mean?"

"If it’s Noriko," Seiroku said with a smile, "she’ll understand."

2.

“Think of our lord as ice cream.”  
“…What in the world is that supposed to mean?”  
“If it’s Noriko,” Seiroku said with a smile, “she’ll understand.”  

Seiroku laughed brightly, his voice light and cheerful.  

When he had finished conveying everything, tears streamed down Noriko’s cheeks.  
When he asked, “Do you understand?”, she wiped her eyes with a sob and whispered, “Yes.”  
“This… is the one thing I’ve been waiting for—forever.”  

Noriko smiled apologetically. Her eyes glistened, but there was no trace of hesitation left in them.  
“I’ve only ever thought about myself… I failed to save the one person I most needed to save.”  
“Mr. Roku, you’ve saved countless lives—on battlefields and in this country. But the truth is… you were the one who needed saving most of all.”  

Roku turned away, unable to bear the sight of her expression. But Noriko gently cupped his face in both hands and turned him to face her directly. Her moist eyes sparkled like ramune.  
“This… is called repentance, isn’t it? Even if God refuses to forgive you, I will forgive you.”  

I don’t want to run away anymore, Roku thought, looking into Noriko’s determined gaze.  
“I’ll wait for you. Even if you never choose me, it’s all right. Even if your heart still holds a place for Seiroku—that’s fine too. I’ll wait for your answer… forever.”  
“…Will you really wait, even if I grow old?”  
“If you think of it as rehearsal for the Final Judgment, it’s not so hard.”  

Noriko reached into the furoshiki-wrapped bundle beside her and pulled out the *Denki Nishiki* notes. The very same notes Roku had retrieved from Kihachi on the train, she now clutched tenderly to her chest.

Noriko reached into the furoshiki-wrapped bundle beside her and pulled out the *Denki Nishiki* notes—the very same notes Roku had retrieved from Kihachi on the train. Now, she clutched them tenderly to her chest.

2.

“I… still can’t give you an answer,” she said softly. “This may be presumptuous of me, but… if someday, down the road, my heart finally settles—will you forgive me then? Will you still want me? Need me?”

“If it brings you salvation, then yes.”

Still holding the notes, Noriko leaned her head against Roku’s broad chest and murmured, “...Ookini,” bowing her head deeply.

“And at the very least, you’re already needed by Inako.”

“Huh?” Noriko lifted her face, surprised.

“When Inako was sick with a fever once, long ago, she told me over and over how only her older sister stayed by her side the whole time, nursing her without rest. That’s why she loves you. Why she respects you.”

“No, that’s not right,” Noriko shook her head. “It was me who got sick first. Everyone around me said, ‘You’ve taken your medicine, so you’ll be fine,’ and went right back to work. …It was a lonely time. But just before noon, Inako came to my bedside, her clothes all muddy. Can you guess what she said?”

Noriko’s face softened into a gentle smile.

“She smiled and said, ‘I went and prayed at Akubi-sama’s shrine, so it’s going to be all right now.’ She must’ve walked such a long way—from our house to the sacred mound, all on a child’s feet. And from then on, she stayed by me the whole time until I recovered. The fever passed to her after that, and I was the one who nursed her back to health. That’s all it was—just returning the favor.”

“She’s a kind child.”

“That’s why I love Inako. She’s my pride, my little sister—someone I’d never be ashamed to show to anyone.”

“You may seem cold on the surface, but you’re kind too. You know… I think I’ll compare you to ice cream.”

---

2.

Staring blankly, Noriko gradually began to suppress laughter, her shoulders trembling.

“You don’t even understand what it means, do you?”

“Well then, *what* does it mean?” Roku asked, pouting.

Noriko burst into bright laughter. Roku turned away with a “Hmph,” then murmured, “...I wonder how Sakamoto Kihachi and Inako are getting along by now.”

“They’ll be just fine, I’m sure. You know, there’s another word for thunder—*inazuma*, right? Apparently, ‘wife’ used to mean ‘opposite-sex companion’ in olden times.”

“I’ve never heard that before,” Roku said.

Noriko laughed again, as if it were her own joke.

“Rice paddies and electricity—those two are perfectly matched. They’ll definitely support each other and get through this.”

　　　　　　　　　　＊

Before Kihachi and Inako stretched a body of water too vast to take in all at once.

The horizon stretched straight from end to end, sharply dividing the evening sky from the sea. Waves ceaselessly rolled onto the sandy shore, their endless washing of sand a soothing sound. The water was leaden gray, while the blue sky above was beginning to speckle with red and yellow.

“It’s so vast,” Inako breathed in awe.

Side by side on the beach, they gazed without tiring at the endless sea.

From Yamada Station, they had taken the Iseden electric railway straight to Futami-gū—not to the Inner Shrine, but to the seaside. Behind Futami-gū’s shore extended a pine grove, and beyond it, inns lined up one after another beneath their eaves.

“I finally got to wear the kimono my older sister gave me.”

Inako stood up and fluttered the sleeves of Noriko’s white *kasuri* kimono. Though only a temporary disguise, the joy of finally wearing the garment she had longed for was overwhelming—Inako ran toward the sea.

At the water’s edge, she spread her arms wide and spun around and around, the white *kasuri* fabric swirling fantastically in the evening light.

Her figure called to mind the *Denki Hi Odori*—the Electric Fire Dance—that Kihachi had once seen at the National Industrial Exhibition, and he stood transfixed for a moment.

Soon, Kihachi rose and stepped beside her. “Hey, Inako… this outfit suits you,” he said.

“You gonna call me *teru teru bōzu* again?” Inako narrowed her eyes sharply.

“No, not that. I mean… you really suit white clothes. You’re beautiful.”

Inako flushed red, and Kihachi quickly turned his face away, flustered. He hadn’t meant to go that far as to say “beautiful.”

To hide his embarrassment, Kihachi shouted with all his might out toward the sea.

His voice was swallowed by the water, echoing back nothing. Still, he kept shouting. Inako stepped beside him and likewise cried out with all her strength—her voice higher than his, tinged with something almost joyful. The motherly sea absorbed their overlapping cries one by one, like soothing children who had begun to play too wildly.

“…In the end, we never saw Mount Fuji, did we?”

Inako spoke softly, wistfully, after her voice had faded.

The words that Seiroku—and likely Moneko too—had repeated: *From Ise, you can see Mount Fuji.*

But before them now stretched only sky and sea, no mountain silhouette anywhere. At that moment, Kihachi felt just as he had when he realized Santakurō was a fictional character—empty, disillusioned.

---

2.

“Are you satisfied, the two of you?”

Turning at the low voice, they saw Jinbei’emon and Yōsuke approaching, clearly exhausted.

Jinbei’emon’s face was ashen, his Kaiser-style mustache disheveled like an octopus’s tentacles, while Yōsuke’s eyes were bloodshot.

“What do you think you’re doing, running off like that in a fit of spite? Don’t make us waste our time chasing you.”

The two men, looking like fallen samurai, staggered forward unsteadily.

“Oh, sorry to keep you waiting, you two.”

At that moment, someone came dashing from the direction of the pine grove, and Yōsuke flinched, his face twisting.

“Sorry, I didn’t mean to keep you waiting, especially after you went to the trouble of coming all this way. My business meeting ran longer than expected.”

“S-Saki…?” Yōsuke stammered in a high-pitched voice, and Saki tilted her head in mild confusion.

“Oh, Brother? What are you doing here?”

“So you actually came to Ise just to see Saki?”

Saki glanced back and forth between the two disheveled adults who looked like ghosts, then turned to Kihachi.

“Well then, what is it you wanted from me?”

Saki had been staying at a distinguished inn with a spacious garden adorned with pine trees and stone pagodas.

As Kihachi walked down the second-floor corridor, he felt a sense of inadequacy, thinking this was the kind of place he’d never belong in. Soon, he was led into the private room where Saki had been conducting her meeting.

Opening the glass window, a cool breeze drifted in, carrying with it the faint sound of waves and the scent of pine from the garden.

Saki settled herself gracefully into seiza on the cushion provided, while Yōsuke remained fidgety throughout, and Jinbei’emon grew visibly tense the moment he realized Saki was from the Mizoe family.

“Actually… there’s something I’d like you to have, Saki-san.”

While Jinbei’emon looked suspicious, Yōsuke cowered like a child caught just before mischief is exposed.

“It’d be embarrassing if I’m wrong…”

—Was Dr. Paikaru really Yōsuke—or not?

Inako had harbored a vague suspicion about this for some time.

“At that restaurant in Ōtsu, Yōsuke said our Hyakugawa sake tasted dry. But our sake is ‘female sake’—a mellow, typical Fushimi style. A ‘dry’ impression fits better with male sake from Nada. At the time, I didn’t know who Saki-san was, so I didn’t think much of it… but after meeting her, my doubts grew.”

Inako shot a glance at Saki, who was smiling faintly.

“You mentioned that ‘Paikaru’ means baijiu—the distilled liquor of Qing China. And Saki-san’s tastes run entirely toward distilled spirits like whiskey and vodka. Most likely, all those stories about Yōsuke traveling the world for business… were really yours, Saki-san.”

“My, Inako-chan, you’re such a sharp observer.”

Saki laughed innocently, then bowed slightly to the stunned Jinbei’emon. “She’s absolutely right.”

“I am Dr. Paikaru—Saki Mitsue. From this moment on, please remember me.”

After that, Kihachi told Saki everything that had happened and played the recorded conversation as evidence on the phonograph he had brought with him.

When the phonograph recording ended, Saki called out, “Brother,” in a cold, detached voice.

“N-no, this was just to make business go a little more smoothly, you see—”

But before he could finish, Saki’s expression turned furious. “Brother!” she snapped, and Yōsuke flinched, shrinking back.

“You’ve always been full of excuses. When you failed to leap from the diving pole, when you grew tired of life in Vancouver and returned home with your relatives. You constantly complain, yet rely on others to survive. You sit cross-legged atop other people’s freedom just to preserve your own. You’re like a petty lord. That’s precisely why you, who scorned women, ended up having me—your sister—inherit the family headship.”

“B-but even if you say that, I—”

Shrinking under his sister’s gaze, Yōsuke stammered helplessly.

“Excuses again?” Saki cut him off sharply. “You’ve got no real power at all, yet you’re always the loudest.”

The following account is what Saki later told me.

After returning from her travels as an itinerant merchant across the world, her talents were recognized, and it was decided that Saki would inherit the headship of Mitsue Shōten. Humiliated that his elder brother’s position had been usurped by his younger sister, Yōsuke, determined to prove his worth to the main house, took up the post of branch manager in Fushimi. From then on, he had been impersonating Dr. Paikaru, using her name and achievements to pressure the sake breweries under his jurisdiction.

---

2.

The story about *Denki Nishiki* that the former branch manager told me had already been greatly exaggerated, and Yōsuke, eager to make a name for himself, came to believe—without any real evidence—that *Denki Nishiki* was a miraculous sake capable of turning failure into triumph, growing increasingly obsessed with it.

When Saki clapped her hands, an attendant appeared.

“Please take my brother to Matsusaka. When he comes to, I’ll deal with him myself.”

The attendant dragged Yōsuke, utterly dejected, out of the room.

“I’m sorry you had to witness such an unsightly scene. Now then, I’ll receive the *Denki Mokuroku*, if you please?”

Saki briefly took the *Denki Mokuroku* into her own hands, then returned it to Kihachi.

“Yes, with this, my brother’s engagement to Inako-chan is hereby nullified.”

Inako let out a deep breath of relief so obvious even an onlooker could perceive it, and bowed deeply, touching her hands to the tatami. “Saki-san, thank you so much.”

Meanwhile, seeing Jinbei’emon slumped disconsolately, Saki spoke gently.

“Mr. Hyakugawa, as you’ve just seen, my brother is who he is. He’s simply not suitable for Inako-chan.”

“…Saki-san, I know it’s presumptuous, but I must ask a favor.”

Jinbei’emon spoke with solemn gravity. Saki tilted her head slightly. “Yes? What is it?”

“Could you leave me alone with this man here for a little while?”

“Oh, of course. Please, take your time.”

Inako cast an anxious glance toward Kihachi, but was led away down the corridor by Saki.

Now, in the wide sitting room, Kihachi and Jinbei’emon sat facing each other. Jinbei’emon, arms crossed, murmured, “*Denki Nishiki*, huh?”

“I suppose I did give it quite a grandiose name.”

“What exactly does ‘electric’ mean in this context?”

What is it?” “Could you leave me alone with this man here for a little while?” “Oh, of course. “But that’s just it,” Kihachi interjected, and Jinbei’emon frowned.  

Kihachi placed the *Denki Mokuroku* on the tatami and opened every page.  

Behind *Entry Twenty: Denki Nishiki*, numerous pages had been filled with handwritten notes.  

“This is my prophecy. That’s why, on the train, I wrote down the prophecy of *Denki Nishiki*.”  

“What do you mean?” Jinbei’emon asked, furrowing his brow.  

“First, by introducing machinery from the stages of rice polishing, washing, and steaming in the *koshiki*, we can improve work efficiency and stabilize quality. I’ve heard that when cooling the steamed rice, speed is key—just like fanning *sushi* rice with a *uchiwa*. Here, a *ryōfūki* will play its part.”

Kihachi moistened his lips and continued speaking.

2.

He opened every page. Behind *Entry Twenty: Denki Nishiki*, numerous pages had been filled with handwritten notes.  

“This is my prophecy. That’s why, on the train, I wrote down the prophecy of *Denki Nishiki*.”  

“What do you mean?” Jinbei’emon asked, furrowing his brow.  

“First, by introducing machinery into the stages of rice polishing, washing, and steaming in the *koshiki*, we can improve work efficiency and stabilize quality. I’ve heard that when cooling the steamed rice, speed is key—just like fanning *sushi* rice with a *uchiwa*. Here, a *ryōfūki* will play its part.”  

Kihachi moistened his lips and continued speaking.  

“If we use climate control machinery, we can precisely regulate the temperature in the *kōjimuro* to suit the growth of *kōji* mold. No—better yet, if we manage the entire brewery with climate control machinery, sake brewing—traditionally believed to be best only in winter—could be done year-round. That would allow for increased production, and perhaps even give rise to entirely new styles of sake.”  

“Year-round brewing?” murmured Jinbei’emon, fixing Kihachi with a sharp stare.  

“…Are you telling me you came up with all this yourself?”  

To Jinbei’emon’s question, Kihachi flatly replied, “No.”  

“I know nothing about sake. All of this came from what Inako told me—we worked out these ideas together.”  

“That girl… When did she become capable of such things?” Jinbei’emon said, eyes widening in astonishment.  

“Oh, I nearly forgot. Even in pasteurization, electric controls would allow sterilization that maintains quality while ensuring safety. That means Inako won’t have to risk burning her hair during practice.”  

Jinbei’emon fell silent, understanding the unspoken implication.  

“Some of Inako’s hair was singed before it was cut. When she caused that small fire, you cut off her hair, didn’t you, to prevent the flames from spreading?”  

Jinbei’emon crossed his arms, exhaled deeply, and nodded. “Yes.”  

“Still, there must’ve been other ways. To cut the hair of a girl at that age…”  

“Enough. Inako’s habit of carelessness… that’s from me.”  

Jinbei’emon cleared his throat, then glared at Kihachi.  

“By the way, you seem awfully obsessed with electricity. Are you planning to become a doctor or something?”

2.

“No, my goal is to use electricity to create Paradise in this world.”

Jinbei’emon snorted.

“So what you want is a world where a bunch of idle fools just sit with their mouths open in front of a shelf waiting for bean cakes to drop into them?”

“Eh…?”

“I’ve fought tooth and nail for the sake of the brewery’s survival—stepping over others, shoving my way forward, clawing at the few bean cakes on that shelf just to stay alive. The people I despise most are those who stand there like idiots, gaping at the shelf, waiting for scraps to fall into their laps.”

Jinbei’emon clenched his teeth hard, and Kihachi nearly flinched under the intensity of his glare.

“And then, those very people turn around and call the ones who actually worked for those bean cakes greedy. They demand a share as if it were their rightful due—the very same people who did nothing but wait. Are you really aiming to build a world that *rescues* people like that?”

Jinbei’emon fixed Kihachi with bloodshot eyes. Kihachi couldn’t begin to fathom what kind of life Jinbei’emon had lived—but precisely because of that, he decided to throw caution aside and speak from the heart.

“Everyone should become the rabbit on the moon.”

“…Huh?”

“Each person becomes a rabbit, pounding rice cakes and making bean cakes themselves. If someone can’t do it alone, they help each other. Some rabbits might be especially good at making mortars or mallets—that’s fine too. As long as everyone has the will to live together, striving for abundance, caring for one another, recognizing each other’s worth… That’s the kind of world I want to build—with the power of electricity.”

“A world… where everyone makes bean cakes together.”

2.

“…Huh?”

“A world… where everyone makes bean cakes together.”

Jinbei’emon sank into deep, profound silence. Kihachi waited patiently for Jinbei’emon’s reply.

After an agonizingly long silence, Jinbei’emon slowly raised his eyes.  
“What exactly do you intend to become with Inako?”

At the mention of Inako’s name, his heart leapt. Instantly, his entire body throbbed with pulse; hot blood surged to his face.  
“Inako-san’s smile… it’s so bright… that’s why…”  
“Spare me your excuses!”  
“Please give Inako to me!”

Once again, the room fell deathly silent. His own heartbeat roared in his ears, unbearably loud.  
Kihachi couldn’t suppress the rough breathing escaping through his nose. Blood raced violently through his veins, his face pulsing from within. He feared what Jinbei’emon would say next. Terror and tension gripped him completely.

Evening light streaming through the window cast deep shadows across Jinbei’emon’s face as he sat motionless, arms crossed, staring into empty space. Tension coiled tighter and tighter—time stretched endlessly, infinitely.

“To break a promise, ruin another man’s engagement, and then have the gall to propose one yourself… Tell me, don’t you think that’s far too selfish?”

“I do,” Kihachi croaked, voice dry. Reaching into his bag, he pulled out a thick furoshiki-wrapped bundle and laid it open before Jinbei’emon. The sight of the wad of banknotes made Jinbei’emon’s face twitch.  
“This is every yen I’ve received for tuition—I’m returning it all.”

“You… a boy couldn’t possibly gather this much money. What’s going on?”

“My father lent it to me. One of the parishioners was looking for a Buddhist monk, and I took on the job as the condition.”

2.

“Are you really okay with this?”

When Kihachi nodded firmly, Jinbei’emon exhaled deeply and slowly.

“Inako is slow-witted. I thought—if I married her off to a rough, easygoing man who wouldn’t care about her slowness—she might live a somewhat freer life. But that only holds true if the family’s standing was respectable.

It might be an indulgent ideal, but I want to do everything in my power for my daughter, for our family. Inako’s husband must come from a good family—or at the very least, he must be a graduate of the Imperial University. I can’t honestly say your family background is anything special. And unfortunately…”

Jinbei’emon grinned defiantly and grabbed the wad of banknotes.

“Now you’ve lost even your path to higher education. There’s no way you can marry Inako.”

By the time Kihachi finished speaking with Jinbei’emon, Roku and Noriko had arrived at the inn. Since night had already fallen, Saki kindly arranged accommodations for everyone at the inn as compensation for the trouble.

Kihachi shared a room with Roku. That night, listening to Roku’s exhausted breathing, Kihachi sat at a desk lit by a lamp, facing the *Denki Mokuroku*.

After closing the *Denki Mokuroku*, he glanced at the red ribbon placed beside him—the one Noriko had returned to him when she cut her hair, saying, “I don’t have hair long enough to tie anymore.” Kihachi poked at the ribbon with his finger, then stretched widely. His heart was too stirred to sleep tonight.

Stepping out of the room into the corridor lit by the dim glow of andon lamps, he saw Inako standing by a window, lost in thought as she bathed in the night breeze.

Inako turned, startled, upon noticing Kihachi.

2.

2.

“Can’t you sleep either, Inako?”

“In my case, it’s been… a lot,” Inako replied with a faint smile. From the open window came the sound of waves washing over sand, and as they listened, the two naturally found themselves agreeing to go see the sea at night. Since they were at it, Kihachi brought along the phonograph and a wax cylinder taken from the forbidden shrine. Together, they quietly slipped out through the inn’s front entrance.

Outside the inn, there was little light; they could barely make out each other’s faces as they walked side by side.

“The path is still dark—sure you’re okay without a flashlight?”

Kihachi smiled at Inako, who answered, “I’ll be fine now.”

Along the row of pine trees in front of the inn stood stone lanterns glowing softly. The two followed the pine-lined approach to its end, then arrived at a small shrine built against a rocky cliff.

They proceeded down a narrow path where waves lapped close beside them, until the shrine building came into view. There, they sat side by side on a convenient spot. This place was famous for its two rocks jutting from the sea—the Meoto Iwa, or “Wedded Rocks”—but the sea before them lay pitch black, and unfortunately, the rocks’ forms remained invisible.

Lanterns illuminated the precinct, and the moon floated in the sky. Kihachi prepared the phonograph and inserted a random wax cylinder; soon, the mellow tones of a shamisen began to drift into the night.

After listening quietly to the music for a while, Inako murmured:

“Kihachi-san, do you know what ‘a touch from the gods’ means? In sake brewing, even if you follow exactly the same steps every time, the flavor turns out different sometimes. My mother used to call that ‘a touch from the gods.’”

“Huh. That’s an interesting way to put it.”

---

2.

The rocks’ forms remained invisible. Lanterns illuminated the precinct, and the moon floated in the sky. Kihachi prepared the phonograph and inserted a random wax cylinder; soon, the mellow tones of a shamisen began to drift into the night. After listening quietly to the music for a while, Inako murmured: “Kihachi-san, do you know what ‘a touch from the gods’ means? In sake brewing, even if you follow exactly the same steps every time, the flavor turns out different sometimes. My mother used to call that ‘a touch from the gods.’”

“Huh. That’s an interesting way to put it.”

---

“We always prayed desperately because we wanted that divine little push in everyday life. I used to think that as long as we just honored and believed in the gods, it would naturally come. But really, whenever something bad happened, deep down I was blaming the gods—and turning away from my own shortcomings.

You know how shrines often have a mirror enshrined? Lately, I’ve come to think that the gods themselves are like mirrors. When people make requests or give thanks at a shrine, maybe they’re borrowing the presence of the divine just to face themselves. The consequences of laziness or effort—those always come back to you.

In sake brewing too, if you just rely on the gods, you’ll never make good sake. Only when you’ve given it your all does the divine lend that final, subtle touch.”

With fierce determination in her eyes, Inako gazed far across the sea.

“I want to become a *tōji* like Okaa-san.”

Unnoticed, the music had stopped. Her clear voice struck Kihachi’s ears.

“I loved the brewery where I spent time with Okaa-san. I want to study sake more, and complete the *Denki Nishiki*. Even if Otō-san opposes me, this time I won’t run away. It’s still hard now—but I want to believe in myself. Believing—that’s what I’m good at.”

She turned toward him a bright, warm smile, so full of energy it made him feel uplifted too.

“Thank you, Kihachi-san. Because you lit things up with electricity, I finally noticed something precious that had been lying right beneath my feet all along.”

Taking advantage of the silence, Kihachi averted his face from Inako and fiddled with the phonograph.

“I’m not embarrassed,” he muttered.

2.

“Shut up.”

Kihachi, thinking it was time for a more cheerful tune, slid another *roukan* into the phonograph.  
“Now then, what shall we have next?”  
“Maybe something from the Salvation Army hymnbook?”

“—Can you hear me?”

Suddenly, a man’s voice rang out from the phonograph, and the two turned to look at each other in shock.

“This letter’s hard for me to write, so I’ve decided to leave my words on this *roukan* instead.”

It was Seiroku’s voice.

Faint and crackling with static, yet unmistakably the voice of his brother—heard for the first time in years. Startled, Kihachi instinctively lifted the needle, then with trembling hands reset it to the beginning and started playback again.

“—Kihachi. Can you hear me?

I’ve never been good with letters, so I decided to leave my words on this *roukan*.

If you’re hearing this, then you must’ve opened the forbidden shrine. I don’t know why you did it. Maybe, like in the past, people have stopped believing in the gods.

Back when I was young, I didn’t believe in gods or Buddhas either. Once, long ago, I opened the forbidden shrine myself. And the moment I saw the *zushi* inside, I realized I felt relieved.

All that time I’d been preaching arrogantly that there was no god—yet deep down, in some corner of my heart, I’d still hoped one existed.

Why do people believe in gods and Paradise? Only now, after all this time, has even a fool like me finally understood.”

---

2.

“Because you can’t see it, that doesn’t mean the souls of the dead or Paradise don’t exist. But if we use that as an excuse to forget about the deceased, isn’t that just too lonely? The dead can only live on through those who remain behind. Even if it’s utterly absurd, people try to accept their grief by believing the dead rest in their graves, stay close to their loved ones, and live peacefully in Paradise. Not being able to see is painful—but it also holds hope.

There’s a saying in Christianity: ‘The Kingdom of Heaven is like a mustard seed.’ Though tiny, when sown, it grows larger than any other plant, becoming a great tree where birds come to nest.

Kihachi’s electric Paradise must be just the same.

When each person begins to shine, even if their light is small, these sparks will gather through the bonds they share, and eventually form a world filled with light. Kihachi’s Paradise, illuminated by electric lamps like *illumināshon*, would surely sparkle like the Milky Way when seen from the moon.

Up there on the moon, pounding rice cakes alongside the rabbit, I’ll be looking forward to the Paradise Kihachi creates.

And when Kihachi finally lives out his full lifespan and comes here, then—we’ll all have sukiyaki together.”

The moment the playback stopped, Kihachi broke down sobbing toward the pitch-black sea.

Regret and sorrow pierced through every part of him; tears streamed from his eyes without restraint. As he cried out, he finally understood why Seiroku had always loved high places.

He had wanted to confirm whether the next world truly lay up in the heavens.

He climbed higher, ever higher—because he so desperately wanted to believe Paradise existed.

“I didn’t do it to prove myself to my brother.”

At the fair’s tall tower, Seiroku had said wistfully: “When you die, you go to the moon.”

“I wanted to prove with electricity that Paradise was real, so I could set my brother’s mind at ease.”

Seiroku had always believed. Believed in Kihachi. Believed in Kihachi’s electric Paradise.

Kihachi picked up a small stone and flung it into the sea. Faintly, amid the sound of waves, came the splash of water.

“Come out! If gods and Buddhas truly exist, then come out!”

Again and again he shouted, hurling stones, only for them to vanish, swallowed uselessly by the vast sea.

“If you’re there, come out! Show yourselves… Please… just come out… One word is enough… Let me say ‘I’m sorry’… Let me say… ‘Thank you’…”

Kneeling where he stood, Kihachi wailed. Then, gently, Inako’s warm hand touched his arm.

“All this time, you’ve been deliberately doing disrespectful things at shrines and graves.”

Inako rubbed his arm and spoke softly.

“Kihachi-san, you’ve always wanted to catch God’s attention.”

When his tears began to subside, Kihachi murmured, “It’s just… not being able to see—it’s been so painful.”

“No matter how hard I strain my eyes, the fact that my brother isn’t anywhere in this world… that’s what hurts.”

“What are you saying?” Inako tightened her grip on his arm.

“Don’t you already know exactly where your brother went?”

Tears streaking her cheeks, Inako pointed triumphantly at the moon floating in the night sky, gazing at Kihachi, who sat dazed and uncomprehending.

2.

“There he is—pounding rice cakes with the rabbit on the moon, just like you said. Isn’t that enough? This time, let’s believe what your brother said.”

Gazing up at the small light floating in the night sky, he murmured, “So that’s where you were.”

He had been searching endlessly for that precious person who had shown him so many wonders and given him so much.

“Santakurō-san was on the moon.”

After that, as they listened again and again to the will, the night began to fade. Kihachi and Inako sat side by side, shoulders touching, watching the edge of the sky slowly brighten.

“There,” said Inako, pointing at two rocks protruding from the water’s surface.

They were the Meoto Iwa—the “Husband-and-Wife Rocks.” A shimenawa rope hung between them, and a small *torii* gate stood atop the larger rock.

The two stood and stepped forward, lining up side by side against the seawall.

The light strengthened, and the stars gradually vanished. The texture of the rocks and the expression on Inako’s face grew sharp and clear. And beyond the sea—there was the horizon—

“What the…?”

Kihachi stared in disbelief. Until yesterday, he was certain he’d been gazing out at an endless, unbroken horizon. But where the horizon should have been, an unfamiliar mountain ridge now stretched from end to end.

Until now, he had believed he was looking out over the Pacific Ocean. But as he mentally recalled the map of this region, it suddenly made sense.

Futamiura lay at the southern tip of Ise Bay, which curved like a reversed “tsu” character—and this coastline faced north.

---

2.

A sharp, clear expression came over Inako’s face. And beyond the sea—the horizon— “What the…?” Kihachi stared in disbelief. Until yesterday, he had been certain he was gazing out upon an endless, unbroken horizon. But where the horizon should have been, an unfamiliar mountain ridge now stretched from end to end. Until now, he had believed he was looking out over the Pacific Ocean. Yet as he mentally recalled the map of this region, it suddenly made sense: Futamiura lay at the southern tip of Ise Bay, which curved like a reversed “tsu” character—and this coastline faced north.

In other words, the mountains had merely been obscured by haze yesterday; now, with the air so clear, he was seeing the true form of Ise Bay—the inland peaks rising in a continuous chain.

Inako tugged insistently at Kihachi’s sleeve as he stood frozen.

“Kihachi-san… Could that mountain possibly be…”

At the center between the Husband-and-Wife Rocks—behind them—loomed one peak distinctly larger than the others.

Slightly indented at the summit, yet majestically forming a broad, gentle triangle.

A sacred peak he had seen only in painted scrolls and photographs.

“That’s… Mount Fuji, isn’t it?”

Though morning mist softened its outline, there was no mistaking it. It wore no snowcap this summer season, yet it radiated a holiness that seemed to embrace all who beheld it with serene generosity.

“Smaller than Ōmi-Fuji,” Inako said with a smile. Kihachi nodded. Was it the great distance? The sacred peak appeared as small and charming as an upturned *sakazuki*. Beyond another mountain slightly apart from that Fuji, light began to spill through.

A brilliant, perfectly round glow—like a hundred billion trillion electric lamps—slowly revealed itself.

“You kept your promise,” Inako said, wiping moist eyes.

“You showed me Mount Fuji just as you’d promised, Kihachi-san. So now it’s my turn to become a god—and grant one wish of yours.”

At the sight of Inako’s faint, sunlit smile, Kihachi felt his chest tighten, burning with warmth.

“…Hand.”

“—Huh?”

“I want… to hold hands… with Inako.”

Without hesitation, Inako reached out and clasped Kihachi’s left hand.

2.

Softer and colder than imagined, the small hand sent a thrill of joy coursing through every drop of blood in his body.

They twined their fingers together and did not let go, even as Mount Fuji blurred into haze behind them.

　　　　　　　　　　＊

The two walked slowly along the sandy shore of Futami-ga-ura, still holding hands.

“Kihachi-san, aren’t you hungry?”

“I’m fine. How about you, Inako?”

“I’m a little. …We’ve eaten all sorts of things on the way here, haven’t we?”

“Benkei’s Strength Rice Cake at Mii-dera was delicious, Kinako-chan.”

“Don’t bring up weird memories. And sukiyaki was good too.”

“You were surprised by that red konnyaku in Hachiman-machi, Koya-dōfu-chan.”

“Ramune-san was pretty cold, though.”

“Dechi Yōkan was tasty too.”

“Huh? I didn’t eat any of that!”

“You’ve got a body just like a dechi yōkan.”

“I’m not *that* flat!” Inako puffed her cheeks.

“…And the coffee—”

“Tasted awful.”

“Yeah, it really did.”

They burst into laughter at the same time, squeezing each other’s hand tightly.

“We came all the way to Ise—might as well try some akafuku. Maybe Ise udon too.”

“You’re always thinking about food.”

“Oh! …We haven’t even paid respects to Ise-san yet.”

“We’ll come again next time,” Kihachi smiled, and Inako nodded.

“I want to go to more places with you… eat lots and lots of delicious things together.”

2.

When Inako gave his hand a squeeze, Kihachi gently pulled his hand away.

“If we go on like this, people will notice.”

Before they realized it, the two had already reached the vicinity of the inn.

“…It felt over in a flash, just like that dawn earlier.”

Regretfully, Inako murmured the words.

After finishing their morning meal at the inn, all of them headed together to the Futamiura stop. The electric train was just pulling in, and after Noriko, Roku, Jinbei’emon, and Saki boarded, Inako stepped onto the train as well.

Turning back, she saw Kihachi standing still, a soft smile playing on his lips as he looked up at her.

“Kihachi-san? What are you doing? The train’s about to leave!”

“…Sorry. I can’t go with you, Inako.”

His words didn’t sink in at first.

“I made a deal with Mr. Jinbei’emon last night—I have to take responsibility for taking you around without permission.”

Instinctively, Inako tried to leap off the train, but Jinbei’emon caught her arm and held her back.

“For now, I won’t be returning to Kyoto.”

“Won’t be returning…? Where are you going? What about school?”

Kihachi only smiled gently, saying nothing.

“Don’t ask, Inako. It’s part of the agreement,” Jinbei’emon said calmly.

“That’s… That’s just too cruel. Too cruel…”

Looking at Kihachi’s face, Inako knew for certain: he was going far, far away—somewhere she’d worry endlessly about him.

“Hey… Let’s travel more, just the two of us. Talk about all kinds of things. Share jokes.”

“Well then, why don’t *you* come with me?”  

2.

Kihachi spoke in a gentle tone, and Jinbei’emon let go of her hand. When Inako turned around, Jinbei’emon was already looking away, arms crossed. *Now’s my chance—I could go with Kihachi.*  

But—  

“I won’t run away anymore.”  

Inako didn’t budge an inch.  

“I’ve finally found something I can do. No matter how hard it gets, I don’t want to run.”  

“I knew you’d say that,” Kihachi replied with a satisfied smile, then handed her something he’d taken from his coat.  

It was the *Denki Mokuroku*, wrapped in a red ribbon.  

“I’ve finally completed it. All thanks to you, Inako.”  

Before she could sink into sentiment, the train began to pull away slowly.  

Inako stretched out her hand toward the receding Kihachi, raising her little finger high.  

Kihachi instantly raised his in return—but their fingertips barely brushed, missing by the slightest margin, and then parted without linking.  

“Don’t get hung up on form,” Kihachi called out. “Just keep believing!”  

“We’re connected by something invisible!”  

As Kihachi’s figure vanished from sight, Inako staggered and collapsed where she stood, eyes falling to the *Denki Mokuroku* in her hands. With trembling fingers, she untied the ribbon, opened the book, and flipped through to the very last page—where she found a prophecy newly inscribed in the blank space.  

There, written simply, was:  

**《Twenty-One: Love》**  

Closing her eyes as tears blurred her vision, she thought she could hear Kihachi’s voice echoing in her mind.  

2.

“Don’t get hung up on form,” Kihachi called out. “Just keep believing! We’re connected by something invisible!”  

As Kihachi’s figure vanished from sight, Inako staggered and collapsed where she stood, her eyes falling to the *Denki Mokuroku* in her hands.
